NOTES.
To make children acquainted with the
cardifxal points of sex-relations is a duty in-
culcated by the New Woman on the old
mother. The principle is probably sound,
though its application should., always dis-
criminate. “Ignorance is not innocence.”
Neither is knowledge ; and the risk of titil-
lating precocity by measured information is
almost as great as the risk of creating it by
silence. Health, constitution, character,
circumstances —a thousand things should
alter conduct according to individual cases.
When enlightenment is judged desirable,
there is now quite a substantial body of
literature (mainly American) to point out the
mode. A recent sample, “ Baby Buds,” a
booklet by Ellis Ethelmer, is supposed to be
addressed to a child four years old —which
is tough on the child four years old. E.g. :
“The female flower now begins to fade
away, but the ovary or fruit continues to
swell and increase ; the ovules also absorb
sap in the ovary and grow up,” &c.
Unlucky infancy is expected to assimilate
this ! Decidedly, there are no lengths to
which woman, old or new, will not go when
dominated by a fixed idea. Yet “ Baby
Buds,” kept for a little elder age, supplies
the anxious mother with some excellent texts
for translation into nursery vernacular.
Sept. Scribner’s Magazine starts with
“ A History of the Last Quarter-Century in
the United States,” which gives a striking
glimpse of corrupt politics from a reformer’s
point of view. A particularly well-illustrated
article shows how photography may be
applied to fiction, and seems to suggest that
a reversion to the prehistoric picture-writing
system would not be without its advantages.
Chapman’s Magazine for August deserves
special commendation as one of the best
literary budgets ever offered at the price (Is.
posted). The contributors are Bret Harte,
Edwin Pugh, Eden Phillpotts, C. E.
Raimond, F. C. Phillips, lan Maclaren,
Mary E. Wilkins and Violet Hunt ; and the
quality of the work is undeniable.
Saturday Review deals tenderly with
poet Parkes. “ That his sonnets, are in-
teresting as poetry,” it says, “it may be too
much to assert, but they are certainly curious
as human documents.” And, adds the re-
viewer, “let us be thankful that Australia
boasts no ‘ blood-stained Jeffries.’” Hm !
Anew illustrated London weekly, which
will not let a photograph appear in its
columns, is to be called the Unicorn, and
issued this month. A. JH. Girdlestone is
literary, and Raven Hill art editor.
From Curzon’s recently-published “ Pro-
blems of the Far East”:—“ln a country
like China the institution of sisterhoods
planted alongside of male establishments,
the spectacle of unmarried persons of both
sexes residing and working together, both in
public and private, and of girls making long
journeys into the interior without responsible
escort, are sources of a misunderstanding
at which the pure-minded may afford to
scoff, but which in many cases has more to
do with anti-missionary feeling in China
than any amount of national hostility or
doctrinal antagonism. Only last year,
at the remote inland town of Kuei-
hwa-cheng, a friend of mine encoun-
tered a missionary community con-
sisting of one male and of 20 Swedish
girls. The propaganda of the latter con-
sisted in parading the streets and singing
hymns to the strumming of tambourines
and guitars. The society that had com-
mitted the outrage of sending out these
innocent girls only allowed them 200 dollars,
or £27 10s. a year apiece for board, lodging
and clothing. Asa consequence they were
destitute of the smallest comforts of life,
and could not even perform their toilette
without the impertinent eyes of Chinamen
being directed upon them through the paper-
screens. Can anything be conceived more
futile or more culpable than such an enter-
prise?”
The exceedingly pretty “ Temple Shake-
speare,” now issuing in 40 pocket vols., at
£2 15s. the set, posted, can be warmly
recommended to anyone who wants a really
good edition. Twenty-three vols, are already
DO hand, and the remainder will follow by
monthly instalments.

Medical.
IT MAY BE SELDOM
That Medical Advertisements can be relied on, but
we here assert the following facts, which can be
borne out from Testimonials in our possession.
Thousands may see this, and treat it as some
announcements deserve, but in this they would
make a great mistake,

Q XT R S E X. V E S!
We wish it to be understood that the character of the
business we do, and the absolutely inclusive variety of the
goods we sell, entitle us to be called
UNIVERSAL PROVIDERS.
We are also not far out, considering the low prices we
Ask for everything we sell, and the consequent blessings
we are continually showering upon the people, when we
assert, not in timorous tones, but with the trumpet voice
Of confidence in the truth of what We say) when we dub
ourselves
PUBLIC) BENEFACTORS.
There are good men in every sphere of life—honest
citizens in every community, whose deeds stamp them
for the approval of their fellow-men, and single them out
from the common herd. Thus we find among the crowd
who aspire to serve their country on the benches of the
Legislative Assembly a patriot here and there who stands
out in bold relief against the majority, whose sole aim
is place and pay. And in commercial life the same thing
occurs, and the same strong contrast between the few
disinterested friends of the people, and the many selfish
lookers after the big profits they would like to make, is
plainly apparent. To the former class, to the little band
of merchants who study their customers’ interests, and
who, in self-abnegation and contentedness, are willing to
accept small profits in reward for their services, belong
ANTHONY HORDERN’S.
They say that a good action is its own reward, but if the
good action entail hard work, much tribulation, and con-
tinual strife, then surely the laborer is worthy of his hire,
and the doer of good actions entitled to some recompense
at the hands of those who profit by what he does.
ANTHONY HORDERN’S
nevercease from their toil; in the burden and heat of the
noonday sun they work, oppressed by the cruel frost of
somewhile baffled efforts and frustrated negotiations, they
continue, knowing always that the approval of millions of
souls is theirs. For three-quarters of a century it has
been so, and for a longer time than that it will remain as
it has been. Generations passed away have bought from
the great house, and generations yet to come will do the
same. Like the practice of honesty or truth, there is a
policy in it which brings to its adherents
PROFIT AND SATISFACTION.
ANTHONY HORDERN & SONS,
UNIVERSAL PROVIDERS.

Bards of the Backblocks.
DARRELL.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
So I ve taken his hundred notes in the end,
And now, as I turn them over,
I feel like a man who's been false to a friend,
Or has broken his troth to a lover.
And what will they purchase, when all is said,
For me with the world’s wealth laden ?
A barrel or two of Kaludah red,
Or the favor of some light maiden !
'^ l -ixTL , ' ne^urns ,e S ra y day’s birth
When the lamp of the revel paleth ;
We know what the kiss of a woman is worth —
But a good horse never faileth.
Your white arms clinging, my ringless bride,
Are bonds that the years will sever;
But the brave hoof-thunder of Darrell’s stride
Will beat in my heart for ever !
You know how little of truth there lies
In the heart of your hot caresses,
There is danger hid in your dreamful eyes,
There is death in your winding tresses ;
And, since you would turn for a fairer face
Or a stronger arm’s enfolding,
You will never hold in my heart the place
That one honest horse is holding.
The stars are fading by one and one
And the fires of the dawn are lightening
The web that a pitiless fate has spun,
And my own cursed hand is tightening)
Oh ! better this arm had lost its force,
This brain in the dust lain idle,
Before I bartered the grandest horse
That ever carried a bridle !
N.S.W.
GLENROWAN.
THE SHEARING OF THE FLEECER.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
a western township in Queensland two sundowners
got at " a shanty-keeper for £2O or £3O in the following
manner. .. . Vide western papers.
Is he from the backblocks ? Comes he here in
peace ?
lls he Union ratter—twig his hairy teeth ?
Comes he from the burning, smeared in kerosene?
Keek his hands of bloodshed—of a lamb just
weaned ?
Are those greenhide uppers off a stolen beast ?
Gosh ! what length of shin-bone —what enormous
feet!
Long and lank his hair is, merging into beard ;
Shaggy are his eyebrows—is he to be feared?
Piercing black his eyeballs; suddenly they change ;
Reins his rusty moke up—stricken bad with
mange.
Yes ! he sees the sign-board hanging on the post,
Hears the syren “Hullo, mate” of the anxious
host.
“Just a beer and a bit of prog, must get on to-
night,”
Not the least intention of even getting tight.
Gum ! he’s got a drought on judging by the way
He swamps that she-oak liquor, guess he’s on to
stay.
See him in the morning, beer in every pore,
Leering at a stranger riding to the door.
“Damme, here’s the Ripper—working mate o’
mine,
Both been cuttin’ tanks for Jones on the Conda-
mine.”
“G’arn, you drunken Fenian, you stole my
blarsted cheque,
An’ you an’ I ain’t settled quite another matter
yet.”
“Your cheque’s all ri’, old pard ; old pizen-seller
here
Has got it in his cash-box—let’s have a glass of
beer.”
From one they get to many, all enmity was sunk,
The Ripper and his partner were most completely
drunk.
When daylight chased the shadows from off the
shanty wall,
The Ripper’s partner had ’em, and had ’em pretty
tall.
The Ripper tried to soothe him, ’twas waste of time
indeed —
He’d a keg or two inside him, and not a bite of
feed.
By noon the Ripper’s mate had fled into the
scrubby range,
The Ripper sobered slightly, from the landlord
got his change.
The cheque was in the fifties and the Ripper’s
share was half,
Which the honest shanty-keeper handed over
fairly smart.
For the Ripper in his dungeon at his mate’s
attempted theft
Told our friend to keep his jumping share—got
both the mokes and left.
On the road from Oallangolly, when the dingo
raised his yell,
The Ripper and his grinning mate were riding
thro’ like .
At the shanty stood the squatter ; the cheque was
forged and bad ;
Just listen to the curses of the man the Ripper
“had.”
There’s a moral in this little fact. I’ve often
heard it told
The man that’s being “got at ” isn’t always him
that’s sold.

“ Yankee Doodle, ’’ blended with Politics.
The above is, of course, a caricature of the famous American national picture, which will be
given later on.
“YANKEE DOODLE ” is everywhere spoken of as the finest plug-tobacco that has ever
appeared here, and it has won greater success during the short time it has been on the market than
anything previously. This is no idle boast, and, to prove it, any smoker can test the statement for
there are few towns in N.S.W. where “YANKEE DOODLE” is not known and sold. Its’sale
has this year increased nearly one hundred (100) per cent over that of last year. And why is this!
Simply that “YANKEE DOODLE” has struck the popular taste. In the first place, it comes, after
years of careful study, from a combination of certain choice Virginian tobaccos, so blended and
exquisitely flavoured, that any smoker who can stand a tobacco heavier than the pure gold-leaf is
forced to admit that he has at last discovered something far and away better than anything he ha*
been accustomed to. DIXSON & SONS have taken over half-a-.century to accomplish' this ; and. as
their leading business has been built up on their reputation for QUALITY, they could not afford to
make the foregoing statement if any doubt existed. DIXSON & SONS, who use more American leaf
Uian all the other Tobacco Factories combined, are noted for several high-class brands- “CON-
QUEROR,” “SIR ROGER,” “CHAMPION,” and “DOLLARj” bub “YANKEE DOODLE”
ranks supreme.

A 'DAY OF THANKSGIVING.
The bounteous rainfall since the “Day of
Humiliation and Prayer” has led to a mo-
tion in the Anglican Synod now sitting
in the Chapter House. Bathurst-street.
Canon Moreton, haying obtained leave, yes-
terday afternoon moved that “This Synod
wishes to place on record an expression of
its grateful sense of the goodness of Al-
mighty God in sending in our time of great
necessity, an abundant rain over the co-
lony, and desires to regard the same as a
direct answer to prayer.” /

CIVIL SERVICE REFORM.
When
speaking upon the estimates the other
night Mr. Reid said that it was in-
tended to effect sweeping changes in
the whole, service whereby every de-
partment of it would be affected from
top to bottom, and all the salaries paid
regulated according to the value of the
services rendered.

NOTICE.
By sending direct to The Bulletin Office Twenty
Shillings sterling—with postage added where required—
which you may pay by Post Office Order, One Pound
Note, or stamps of any Australian province, you can
have Tub Bulletin posted regularly to you every week for
a year.
This offer is not open to residents of Sydney. In that
city The Bulletin is not delivered by mail, but is entirely
in the hands of our earners, with whom we do not wish to
interfere.
Cheques, Drafts, etc., and all Business Communications
to be addressed to William Maclbod, Managing Director.
Literary Communications to be addressed I. P.
Archibald.
The Editor will carefully read and acknowledge in the
‘Correspondence ” column all contributions submitted
whether in the form of Political, Social, or other Articles,
Verse, Short Tales or Sketches (those dealing with Aus-
tralian subjects, and not exceeding two columns in
length, or, say, 3000 words, are specially acceptable),
Paragraphs, Letters, or Newspaper-Clippings. All
communications will be regarded as strictly confidential.
The Bulletin will return all unavailable MS. u’hen
the address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But we
desire those sending MS. to distinctly understand, that
we are not responsible for its preservation or transmission.
To Amateurs in Itlacle and. White.
Anyone supplying to The Bulletin original humorous or
other topical drawings in line or wash, of sufficient merit
and interest and adapted for direct reproduction, or any
interesting photograph illustrative of a current event, will
be paid for same on acceptance. Any picture containing
a good or adaptable idea, but unfitted for direct reproduc-
tion— i.e., any picture which has to be re-drawn—will be
paid for as a suggestion only. The conditions under
which such artistic contributions will be received are those
applying to MS.
Copies of The Bulletin can be obtained in London,
after the arrival of each mail, at the office of the British
Australasian Co., Limited, 31 Fleet Street, E.C.
Also in the Transvaal -.—The Standard and Diggers
News Co., Limited, Johannesburg, South Africa.
Also :— lt. A. Thompson and Co., 3 Church Street,
Capetown, South Africa.

SATURDAY, OCTOBER 5, 1895.
The Victorian Railway Problem.
During the recent lean years of depression
the railway - deficit has been the great
stumbling-block in the way of the Victorian
Treasury, Since the close of the unspeak-
able Speight regime, stupendous efforts have
been made to cut down the expenses of rail-
way management, but though much has been
done in this respect it has been found im-
possible to overtake the decline in receipts,
andayawning shortageof £3Bo,oooper annum
has still to be met out of public revenue.
The Turner Government therefore ap-
pointed still another Parliamentary Com-
mission to find out new devices for running
the railway on commercial principles, and
the Commission, which seems to have done
its work remarkably well, has sent in a
report bristling with suggestions for further
economy. It proposes to save £85,000 by a
general reduction in wages ; £103,000 by
abolishing free passes and charging the other
State departments for services now rendered
gratis ; £107,000 by closing some of the rail-
way workshops and concentrating the car-
riage-building and repairing industries in
one or two large establishments, and £70,000
in other ways ; thereby reducing the annual
deficiency to practically nil. Also, it sug-
gests that still another effort should be made
to abolish political interference by placing
the railways under the control of a board of
independent trustees. In the old days,
before the advent of Speight, the Vic-
torian Ms.L.A, haunted the railway offices
day and night to get billets for their friends,
relatives, and constituents, or to get them
promoted, or to get their salaries raised, or
to get them reinstated when they had been
“ sacked ” for drunkenness, embezzlement,
or other offences, or to get new stations,
sidings, gates, sub-ways, reduced fares and
freights, free passes, extra trains, special
trains, &c., &c., to beg off constituents who
swindled the department out of their fares,
and many other concessions. Speight was ap-
pointed chief manager with extensive powers
in order to abolish this evil, but under his
festive regime it flourished very much as
before. It flourishes still under his sue-
cessors, hence the proposal to establish a
new form of independent control. The
Parliamentary Commission believes that,
when its recommendations are carried out,
Victoria will at last have arrived at those
commercial principles which it has been so
long striving after, and its railway system
will become a solvent concern, and the
annual deficit will trouble the laud no more.
* * *
Meanwhile, the voice of the person who
wants to lease the State railways to a British
syndicate is again beginning to be heard at
intervals. It is one of the fixed delusions of
some alleged Australians that a gang of
private speculators could work the country’s
chief asset at a profit under the same condi-
tions in which the State works it at a loss ;
that it could take over the lines at their
present valuation, and reduce fares and
freight-charges, and maintain the existing
rate of wages, and keep everything in
good repair, and maintain a regular service
on all the hopeless little branches which
Gillies constructed in the boom era, where
the traffic doesn’t even pay working expenses,
and that it could also realise handsome divi-
dends, and make everything bright and
prosperous. Therefore, there is a frequent
and idiotic demand in certain quarters that
the State should live up to this wholly
imaginary standard of excellence. At pre-
sent the State runs the Victorian railway
system to much better advantage than any
British syndicate would be likely to do, and
the insistence on purely “commercial prin-
ciples,” under the existing circumstances, is
a very large demand indeed. Victoria has
already nearly five times as much railway in
proportion to population as Great Britain
and Ireland, and its lines are being extended
faster than population increases. Its total
mileage of iron-road is one-seventh that of
the United Kingdom ; its receipts are con-
siderably less than one-thirtieth ; therefore,
it has to face the problem of meeting all
liabilities out of much less than one-fourth
the proportionate revenue. If it were pos-
sible to stop construction until population
increased by, perhaps, 25 per cent., the
deficit, under the present system of manage-
ment, would vanish of its own accord. But
todo this it would be necessary to give the pro-
posed trustees the sole power of construction
as well as of management. They must be
authorised to shut down on every suggested
line which could not show a certainty of
profit. When the mallee farmers explained
that a railway was essential to develope the
new country and carry their produce to
the coast, the board, on purely com-
mercial principles, would have to answer,
as a British syndicate would certainly do,
that it doesn’t care a straw whether the
country was developed, or whether the pro-
duce reached the coast or not—that its sole
business was to make the railways yield a
profit. It is not proposed to give the
trustees any such power, and it would be
both foolish and ruinous to do so. But
these are the commercial principles which a
private syndicate would certainly have to
adopt.
The Victorian railway-system, as already
mentioned, is five times as large, in propor-
tion to population, as that of the United
Kingdom, and has just about one-fifth of
the traffic. The people demand that it
should be made to pay all expenses and
interest on cost, and yet they want more
lines in empty regions where the want of
traffic* will further increase the deficiency.
The fares charged in many of the districts
around Melbourne are already lower than
those in some localities around London, and
there is a constant demand for further reduc-
tions. Also, the fares and freights over the
Vic. lines in general are a shade lower than
those in New South Wales, and considerably
lower than those in Queensland, Tasmania,
Westralia, or Maoriland. With four or five
times as much railway in proportion to earn-
ings, and five times as much in proportion
to population as the United Kingdom, Vic-
toria continues to work its lines with the same
proportion of expenses to receipts as do the
Great Eastern, Great Northern, Lancashire
and Yorkshire, and Manchester, Sheffield and
Lincoln companies of England, and it spends
£59 in working expenses out of every £lOO
earned, against an average of £57 for all
England. And if the outlay for pensions—a
charge from which the English private com-
panies are almost wholly free—is deducted,
then the Victorian railways, despite the
rather higher wages paid and the much
shorter working hours, are run at a slightly
lower charge for working expenses in propor-
tion to earnings than the English average.
These results appear—on the surface, at all
events —as if they should be sufficient to
satisfy the public demand for management
on commercial principles.
* # *
For the year just ended the Victorian
lines paid 3 per cent, on the amount borrowed
for construction purposes. This is a higher
rate than that earned by ten out of thirty-
two leading British companies, and but for
the exorbitant interest at which these bor-
rowings were effected—most of the money
being still owing at 4, 4£, and 5 per cent. —
it would be almost sufficient to cover all out-
goings. And it would be quite sufficient,
despite the high rate of interest, but for
the rascally manner in which the original
cost was swelled by the resumption of land
at from twice to twenty times its actual
value. The situation under which the new
era of management on commercial principles
is to start may be summed up in a few
sentences : The province has five times as
much railway in proportion to population as
Britain—the country that is always referred
to as a standard of comparison ; and no
country in the world with so much railway
to so little population has yet succeeded in
showing profitable results. The fares and
freights are below the average, and the people
who insist on the department paying its way
also insist continually on further reductions.
Whenever the railways show any signs of
approaching solvency, they are swamped by
the addition of new lines which don’t pay
more than a fraction of their working ex-
penses ; and yet Parliament won’t part with
the right of making new lines, and the
nation certainly wouldn’t let it do so even if
it wanted to, and if the Legislature did part
with the right it would almost certainly hand
it over to some one quite as foolish as itself.
The railways have to pay interest on at least
one-third more capital than they should be
burdened with, owing to the dreadful land-
resumption jobs of the past ; but for this
fact they would be a highly profitable pro-
perty even now. Also, they have to find £4
per cent, interest on this huge capital,
whereas the private lines of the United
Kingdom only contrive to produce an aver-
age of £3 12s. per cent. ; and in addition to
all this they have to pay higher wages and
maintain a lower standard of working hours
than the British companies. In the face
of all these conditions, the people who pro-
fess to believe that the leasing of the lines to
a foreign syndicate will remedy everything
are very sanguine, or very ignorant, or very
anxious to make commission out of the
transaction.
# * *
If the Parliamentary board of enquiry has,
as it believes, discovered a method by which
the system can be made solvent, despite the
enormous burdens which have been piled on
it by the muddled or dishonest politicians of
past days, then it has done remarkably well.
The fact that no railway - system equally
burdened has yet paid its way, is no reason
why the Victorian one should not be the
first to do so. And if Parliament will enact
that the outlay on future railway construc-
tion must be limited to a certain amount per
annum, so as to bear some relation to the
increase of population, and that all land-
resumptions must be based on the valuations
made for taxing purposes, so as to put a
definite end to the worst swindle of all, and
will apply the betterment principle in future
construction, then it is probable that it will
keep solvent wken it has once reached that
desirable state. And even if the annual
shortage can be limited under present con-
ditions to, say, £200,000 per annum, then
the desired state of solvency will come in
course of time, as the old loans fall due and
are renewed at a lower rate of interest. But
the assumption that the natural condition of
every railway-system is to pay its way, and
that its failure to do so is a certain proof of bad
management now, is an absurdity. Also, the
further assumption that because a few English
railways, under very different conditions,
pay reasonably good dividends, therefore
there is some magic in an English syndicate
which would enable it to put everything
right, is a still greater absurdity. The syn-
dicate, if it arrived, could only put things
right by reducing wages, increasing working
hours, raising fares and freights, shutting up
unprofitable lines, working the paying por-
tion of the system only, and leaving the
State to make pioneer lines in new localities
and stand the loss. English syndicates, as
evidenced by the Maoriland Midland Rail-
way fiasco, are about as stupid as other com-
binations ; and English railway-managers,
as evidenced by the disastrous Speight, are
sometimes even worse than the “colonial”
article.
A Tory on Sweating.
The anti-sweating measure, now proposed by
the Turner Government as an amendment of
the Vic. Factories Acts, finds ample support in
the arguments urged against it by the Argus.
That sapient journal has ruined many a cause
by advocating it, and the pity is that the
waning influence of the Argus makes its oppo-
sition to a good cause less and less valuable
each year. One of the large lies that it now
puts forward with characteristic boldness is
that “ the true remedy for sweating is to restore
the ‘colony’ to prosperity.” It ought to be
evident, to even a right-minded person, that
prosperity has been the ruin of Victoria. Infla-
tion of trade, from whatever cause arising,
means, as things are, inflation of laud-values
and the boomsters’ opportunity. For a mad
year Davies and Bent and their pals live on
champagne and chicken, and Munro and
Balfour drink inordinate quantities of lemon-
ade and tea—and the land-sales multiply apace,
with free whisky and buns for the wretched
buyers. Then comes the smash, and the
depression, hunger, sweating, and other ills,
• * •
Next lie is, “ Take the taxes off raw materials
. . . and sweating will disappear.” The
existence of sweating as an abuse in Freetrade
England is a piece of such commonplace know-
ledge that only a reckless journal like the
Argus would attempt to hold out against it.
Sweating commissions have been appointed in
England again and again. The London County
Council found it necessary to legislate a mini-
mum wage as a safeguard against this evil.
The last deceased British prince was stated to
have caught his fatal illness from clothes made
in a sweater’s den. Kingsley and Tom Hood
wrote their philippics against sweating as it
existed in their time ; and as it exists now.
Sweating can only occur when a needy worker
meets an unscrupulous contractor, and this is a
condition of things that is independent of Free-
trade and of Protection. Sweating is the
necessary effect of competition, and unrestricted
competition is the beginning and end and
middle of the old Manchester school doctrine.
But this lie that the tax on raw materials causes
sweating in Victoria is not only a false de-
duction from facts, it is also a wanton and pro-
fligate perversion of facts. The two worst
sweated trades in Victoria are bread-baking
and the manufacture of underclothing. Flour
is, of course, a local product and a large staple
export; cotton piece goods from which the
underclothing is made are free of Customs.
When the Argus tells the sweated baker and
the wretched woman who sews shirts at 9d. per
dozen that the cause of their wretchedness is
Protection, it is bidding almost too high for
the Tory support.
• * *
The third lie is that the restriction of factory
work to the factories, as proposed by the
Government, is an injustice to hundreds and
thousands of women. The report of the Vic-
torian Sweating Commission showed that the
perpetual menace to industrial employment in
these sewing-trades is the fact that employers
can give any pittance they please to their
wretched employes when they deal with them
separately. The homework must be regulated.
If a woman with domestic responsibilities is
compelled to earn money she can obtain a
permit, but she will be given a schedule of
rates that are obligatory on her employer. It
is on evidence that women have had to press
their infant children into the service of the
sweater, the sum-total of a family’s work being,
perhaps 9s. or 10s. a week. In one case, a child-
of five years old was found helping its mother
by sewing on buttons. To regulate this sort of
thing, the Argus says, is to interfere with the:
liberty of the subject. When the liberty of the
subject means the sweating of the subject it is-
time for a total revision of economic dogmas..
The Tory political economy is not economy at-
all—it is parsimony to the worker and prodi-
gality to the capitalist. We live in the dark
ages as long as the employe’s extremity is the
employer’s opportunity, and the louder the
Argus howls against a change, the sooner it-
will come.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Our Cheap Chinese Clergy.
Rev. Dr. Torrance, of Melb., charges a’
number of Vic. congregations with sweating
their pastors. He says there are 43 ministers
without parsonages receiving stipends of £2OO
a year or under, and 24 with parsonages and
stipends of £l7O. This doesn’t seem (consider-
ing that good, sound sermons may now be
bought at the rate of 2d. per lb. or thereabouts)
such dismally bad pay in these times. It
should always be remembered that parsons are
—on account of certain prejudices connected
with the nature of their calling—saved many
expenses which fall upon the average man.
They are not expected to “shout” for their
friends when they meet them in the street, nor
to “ put a shilling in ” at Yankee-grab, nor to
take an occasional hand at cards, nor to play
billiards now and then for “drinks and the
table,” nor to take shares in racing-sweeps, nor
to “dip in the lucky-bag” at church-
bazaars, nor to subscribe to charities,
nor—many other things which need not
be mentioned. Then, again, it is generally
looked upon as a grave scandal if a parson
smokes in public—consequently one who is
fond of his pipe can hardly consume more than
a cake of tobacco per week unless he sits up all
night to smoke. So that, taking all these
things into consideration, a parson with £l7O a
year and a house, or £2OO without a house, is
probably better oft'than the average layman with
£3OO a year. Before Dr. Torrance gave vent
to the Bitter Cry of the Underpaid Clergy he
should have thoughtfully reflected upon the
exemptions enumerated. A more trenchant
criticism, however, than any The Bulletin
has to offer in regard to his Bitter Cry, ap-
peared in the Age last week in the shape of the
following matter-of-fact telegram :
BAIHNSDALE, Tuesday.
Oh Sunday afternoon last a new Chinese ('impel was
opened here in connection with the Presbyterian Church
by the Rev. T. R. Cairns, of Ballarat. The building cost
about £l2O, and the whole of the cost was defrayed by
Paul Young, a Chinese missionary here, who has saved
t'-~* amount during 10 years from his annual stipend of
£36.
In other words, Paul Young managed to live
on £24 a year for 10 years ! Dr. Torrance
might do well to take this fact also into serious
consideration. He seems to think that the
present condition of the clergy in Australia is
pretty grievous, but when cheap Chinese-
parsons who can live on 9s. a week are pouredi
into the country he will probably begin to see
that there is an economical side to the noble
Chinese martyr-missionary question. If Chris-
tian men and women are willing to lay down,
their lives to convert the Chows, and are sent.
out from Australia (with much hallelujah i
and hosannah) for this very purpose, the
reverend gentlemen who give them such gush-
ing and soulful send-offs can hardly have the
face to complain if their own business is even-
tually brought down to the ‘level of cabinet-
making or market-gardening ifi consequence of
the competition of cheap Cho,w parsons. Andl
let it not be taken for granted Ihat the scope of
these latter must necessarily be confined to-
work amongst their own race in Australia. The
fundamental doctrine taught by the missionaries
is that the yellow man and the white man are
equal in the sight of Heaven. Immediately
the yellow man, who has taken orders, finds
this out by practical experience to be a fraud,,
the bottom will fall out of the missionary trade
for evermore—so far, at any rate, as the Chow
is concerned. It may happen that the clergy
of the next generation will curse with as much
fervor the folly of their predecessors who sent
missionaries to China as squatters and selectors
now curse the man who first introduced thistle?
and rabbits.
Ware Herring!
Let this be clearly stated : no possible issue of
the Dean embroglio can affect the public con-
demnation of the Judge who tried Dean.
Dean’s guilt or innocence has nothing whatever


to do with Judge Windeyer’s capacity or in-
capacity. In summing-up the case on July 6,
immediately after Dean was released on the
recommendation of the Commission majority,
The Bulletin said :
Dean’s guilt or innocence is possibly even now a matter
of conjecture. But Justice does not act on conjecture.
Justice demands that, before a man is imprisoned, his
guilt should be proven ; and Dean’s guilt has never been
proven.
The logic of the situation remains unchanged,
and would so remain if Dean had made a
thousand real or alleged public or private
confessions to as many real or alleged lawyers.
It is not charged against Judge Windeyer that
under his auspices an innocent man was im-
prisoned. It is charged against Judge Win-
deyer that under his auspices a man whose
life was at stake had only the mockery of a
trial. It is charged against Judge Windeyer
that under his auspices, and at his instigation,
and perhaps in consequence of his veiled threat
to imprison the jury from Saturday night to
Monday morning, a man who was possibly
innocent, who was certainly never proven
guilty, was doomed to penal servitude for life ;
so that the country had to interfere, at a great
cost, to redress the injustice aided and abetted
by Judge Windeyer, the servaht of
Justice. It is charged against Judge
Windeyer that Dean, as a victim, does not
stand alone; but that from the case of the
wronged Mrs. Butler (whom he divorced on
the evidence of her husband, the now-convicted
perjurer, and the detective-perjurer whom the
Kerjured husband hired) back to the case of the
fount Rennie youths (convicted before him
after a JEEEREYS-like travesty of a trial, and
mainly on the evidence of two people now
known to be an arsonist and a forger), His
Honor has blotted his career with examples
of what, in any other man than a Judge,
would be branded as obstinacy, incapacity, and
bias. It is charged against Judge Windeyer
that, no matter how good his intentions, he
has sapped the confidence of Parliament,
People, and Press, in the administration
of justice, and it is declared that the time has
more than come for him to retire from the
Bench which his conduct has surrounded with
odium and distrust. And, though every man
in Australasia become as confident that Dean
poisoned his wife as if he had seen him do it
with his own eyes, the gravamen of these
charges will not be lightened by one iota.
Two Months.
Whatever the communication Meagher made
to Salomons, that communication was abso-
lutely confidential, and was obviously intended
so to be considered. According to Salomons’s
own account, his disclosures involved, prima-
facie, anyhow, a breach of professional honesty
(or etiquette, if you please) as between client and
lawyer—worse, as between lawyer and lawyer,
as between two members of a profession bound
in its intercourse to most scrupulously observe
the code of honor and the etiquette of gentlemen.
Salomons pleads that the public interest o verrides
private and professional etiquette and honor.
Undoubtedly it does, but, as Salomons was so
chivalrous a guardian of the public interest,
why did he delay so long to make Meagher’s
statement public ? He says that Meagher
told him of Dean’s alleged confession on 18th
July, and the facts do not ooze out till 26th
Sept., after an interval of more than two mouths.
The brilliant Salomons should tell us where was
his anxiety for the public interest during those
two months. He saw, from his point of
view, a scandalous miscarriage of justice.
He saw a guilty man the recipient of public
honor, and innocent women the objects of
public reproach. He saw the intelligence of
his friend Judge Windeyer and his friend
.Attorney-General Want and his friend Rogers,
•Q.C., held up to public derision ; he might have
seen that tough trio gnawing their flesh vainly
in agonised search for a weapon of defence.
Yet apparently Salomons made no sign ! He
was repositary of the momentous secret of
Dean’s confession for upwards of two months—
;and never let on. Was he waiting for Meagher
to take action ? But two months ! Was he
(torn by contending scruples ? But two
months ! Two months ! two months ! two
months ! What was Salomons doing during
those two months ? If the responsibility doesn’t
lie with him it does with Want. When did
he tell Want? Two months! “The public
interest ” —after two months ! Guilt con-
founded—after two months ! Innocence jus-
tified, the acumen of Windeyer, Want, and
Rogers defended—after two months ! Holy
Moses ! two months !
A Yankee Notion.
The Vic. Railway Board was so pleased with
itsown appointment,at £sperdayeach member,
that it recommended Parliament to hand over
the railways to just such a body as itself —for
modesty’s sake, names not being mentioned.
The Board flatters itself that the principles of
running a private concern necessarily ensure
dividends, and that the application of those
principles to a State concern spells success.
During the enquiry, numerous references were
made to the U.S.A. railways as an example
either of economical management, of wise
management, or of reputable management. In
U.S.A. private enterprise has a new country
and a scattered population to work on—in
Victoria the same conditions obtain, only more
so. The U.S.A. railways, for the year ended
30th June, ’94, paid the half of 1 per cent, on
their capital cost. The Vic. railways, for the year
ended 30th June, ’95, paid 2’74 per cent. on their
capital cost. The burden of capital in all shapes
on the U.S.A. railways is 10,796,473,813d015.
On this amount the dividends declared during
the twelve months were 95,575,976d01. ; but
only 55,755,970d01. of this amount was pro-
vided out of the year’s earnings, the rest being
drawn from accumulations or from anticipated
earnings on good boom principles. The Vic.
returns show that the earning power of the
railways under State management leaves private
management at a discount of 500 per cent. But
it must be further considered that Vic. has had
the free use of the State railways for all sorts
of services for which a private co. would have
demanded cash down ; if these services were
reckoned to the credit of the department, as
they should be with a decent system of book-
keeping, the railway account would appear
more healthy. Moreover, there is no railway
line in Vic. that has not produced in land-
increment double the capital cost of construc-
tion, and there is not a mile that is not saddled
with corrupt charges for State resumption
which have made heavy the pockets of the
land-owner. The Vic. trouble is not to make
the railways pay, but to make the land-owner
repay.
The Dean Case Commentators.
Last Friday, when only the ex-parte statement
of Salomons was before the public as to the
alleged guilt of Dean and his alleged confession
thereof to Meagher, and the latter’s alleged
repetition of the confession, the S.M. Herald
already began to whoop for the gallows in its
good old Mount Rennie style :
The extreme gravity of the position made out by the
circumstantial narrative of Sir Julian Salomons pleads
for a suspension of judgment until the statement in reply
which may be expected from Mr. Meagher has been put
in an equally explicit shape before the public. But, quite
apart from any question upon which Mr. Meagher may
be expected to throw any light, we may be permitted to
refer to the support which this recent development ought
to give to the regular and legal way of determining ques-
tions of guilt or innocence as compared with the method of
trial by oratory and public meeting, of which the Dean
case has afforded such signal illustration.
On the same day the Telegraph took up the
parable thus :
One of the bases of this movement was the allegation
that Dean had been the victim of an improper trial, and
during its progress statements were made in and out of
Parliament reflecting the deepest discredit upon the
administration of justice. For a solicitor to lend himself
to such a proceeding as this, well knowing that it was all
in the interest of a guilty person, and for the purpose of
screening him from the legal consequences of his guilt,
whatever it might be professionally, would, in other
respects, be capable of no excuse.
The Herald’s attitude is explicit and blood-
thirsty. Salomons’s statement is not by itself
sufficient evidence to convict Meagher of con-
cealing the truth, but it is sufficient to show
that Dean should have been hanged. The
Herald suspends judgment as to Salomons’s
truthfulness, but on the trifling matter of
Dean’s guilt, which depends in this instance on
Salomons’s accuracy, it makes up its mind
at once. Its theory is practically that the next
man who is convicted on wholly insufficient
evidence should be hanged because Salomons
says that he has been told that the last man so
convicted deserved hanging, while, at the same
time, it urges the public not to rely on Salo-
mons’s statements until further proof is forth-
coming. And the Telegraph’s attitude is that
if Meagher knew Dean to have been convicted
on wholly insufficient evidence, but also knew
that in this case the law bad gone right by pure
accident, he should not have raised any protest
against the method of the trial—which is
illogical drivel. The matter at issue was not
that Dean was innocent, for no one can sav
with certainty that anybody is innocent, but
that he hadn’t been proved guilty by reason-
able evidence, and that if the mode of trial,
under which he was convicted, was allowed to
continue there was no safety for anybody
whether guilty or not. Dean’s innocence or
guilt was, and still is, a matter of the smallest
importance. Even if he is proved guilty now,
the Windeyer variety of justice remains as bad
and as utterly indefensible as it was before.
Betterment.
Maoriland Legislature is making its third or
fourth attempt to pass a Betterment Act, but
at present with little prospect of success.
Everybody admits that when the value of the
property in a particular district is greatly
increased through the construction of a railway,
or a road, or a harbor, or through the opening
of a new street, or the widening of an old one,
or any other work constructed at public
expense, it is quite fair on principle to make
the owners contribute some of that increase in
part payment of the work. But it is constantly
pointed out that the roads and railways in the
older and, therefore, the richer part of the
province, have been already made in times
when no one thought of applying the betterment
system; that the people of the backblocks have
to pay their share of the interest on these works;
and that it is unjust to make them pay a special
tax for their own roads and railways in addi-
tion to paying their full share for those of their
neighbors. In other words, it is claimed that
the country shouldn’t begin to do a thing unless
it has been doing it all along. There is a cer-
tain semblance of unnaturally accurate justice
about this theory, but then mathematical jus-
tice can’t be arrived at in anything, unless the
universe can be put back to the year One and
everything started afresh. If the country waits
to be mathematically just before it does any-
thing, then it will do nothing. And, in
any case, no one knows to a dead certainty
what is justice. The law fines the very
aggravated drunk 405., and the man who
runs an illicit still £lOO, but no one can say for
certain that to run an illicit still is exactly 50
times, and not 37, or perhaps 63 times, as bad
as the other thing. And when the law fines
the moderate drunk ss. and the rather worse
one 10s., it doesn’t pretend to know for certain
that one of them was exactly twice as objection-
able as the other—it only strikes an average.
This world is full of averages, most of them
very bad ones. Still it is often better to strike
even a bad average than not to strike anything
at all.
The Return from the Gutter.
There has probably never existed any genera-
tion that didn’t delight, up to a certain point,
in hearing itself described as more hopelessly
corrupt and desperately wicked than any gene-
ration that had gone before it. But as it was
certain to entertain, at the same time, a com-
fortable underlying conviction that it wasn’t
anything of the sort, and that, in any case, it
was just as righteous as it had the chance to be,
it was pretty certain also, sooner or later, to sit
down hard upon the author, or the playwright,
or the crier-aloud on the house-tops who was
unduly persistent in painting its morals of a
deeper blue than it really believed they were
entitled to. And the present generation, ac-
cordingly—having outlived the titillation of
being told that its wife is for the most part a
strumpet, and its bosom-friend a designing
scoundrel, and itself incapable of a lofty thought,
or a wholesome aspiration, or a disinterested
action—is beginning calmly to turn its back upon
the stamp of play and of book in which, by im-
plication, it has of late been asked with damn-
able iteration to accept this version of the circum-
stances. The average man, knowing perfectly
well that he is an average man—neither re-
splendeutly virtuous nor exorbitantly vicious—
and that society is mostly composed of average
men, knows consequently, also, that society
cannot collectively run much to either of these
extremes. For which reason he is as uncon-
vinced by bogus immorality as he is by spurious
holiness, and is getting desperately weary of
that dilettante depravity in literature and the
drama which he has all along shrewdly sus-
pected to be no more than a sort of inverted
snuffle—hypocrisy spelt backwards ! Neither
need he be artistically the loser. The most
complex human interest is generally evolved
from the least complex psychological materials,
and the neuro-erotic exploitation, for instance,
of sexual sophistications about which the vast
majority of men and women—untroubled of
morbid introspectiveness—persist in concerning
themselves not at all, has produced no work to
take rank with that of the men who have turned
to immortal account the perfectly normal
development of perfectly commonplace condi-
tions. The belief, also, that mankind, in the
main, possesses a fair share of common honesty
and common decency has so obstinately refused
to perish under these elaborate attempted
demonstrations of its futility that it may be
pretty safely set down as something more than
a mere conventional assumption. And, even
were it otherwise, no end would be served by
its destruction. The man who thinks he has
got rid of all his illusions—has but gained an
illusion the more !
The Lash—An Exploded SupersuaEauj*.
From The People (London) July 14th, 1895: —
Use of the Lash. —From an article in St. Paul’s it
seems that the “Myths of the Middle Ages” which have
been exploded by Mr. Baring Gould by no means exhaust
the list. The writer of the article referred to deals with
“The Use of the Lash,” which every now and again is
publicly credited with stamping out the garrotting
epidemic of the early sixties, and he proves from the
records of the defunct Social Science Association that the
Flogging Acts were introduced after the garrotting
epidemic had been practically overcome. Lord Abbrdarb
was Under-secretary to the Home Department in 1862,
and this is his testimony: “ That the singular outbreak
of garrotting which occurred in London in July, 1862,
was entirely suppressed by the ordinary action of
the police, who watched closely those parts of
the town where the offences were mainly per-
petrated before the winter set in, most of the
ruffians who practised violence being then safely laid by
the heels, and that the flogging Acts introduced in the
session of 1863 effected no reduction in crimes of violence
whatever. So far from there being any decrease in rob-
beries with violence during the six years that followed
the passing of those-Acts, there was, if anything, a slight
increase.” “A genuine mystery,” says the writer in our
contemporary, “ in connection with the garrotting scan-
dal is why this class of offence should have flared up into
extraordinary prominence in the autumn of 1862, only to
die out again within a very few months. It was not a
new crime, and although at the period I refer to several
public men and other well-known persons fell victims to
it, there is no reason to suppose that in 1862 it became a
more lucrative pursuit than it had ever before been.
Be the explanation what it may, the social rank of
a few of the victims attracted much attention, and put
the police on their mettle, with the result that the greater
vigilance of the authorities, and the heavy sentences
passed upon the offenders, stamped out the strange
epidemic with unexampled rapidity. That was the fact.
Why, of all the weapons of the law, should the lash alone
be held in respect by the criminal classes? And why
should garrotters only respect it? If it is useful to sup-
press one class of crime why not all crime ? The truth is
that public confidence in the lash as a deterrent is one of
the strangest superstitions that has ever possessed the
public mind. Let us hope it is now exploded.”
The Extraordinary Synnot Case.
The Synnot divorce-case, which thus far has
had the effect of throwing three Melb. consta-
bles out of employment, promises to produce a
huge official scandal presently. These three
policemen, it will be remembered, were “ tried”
by an Enquiry Board, consisting of Mr. Ake-
hurst, P.M., and two others, on a charge of
“ recklessly swearing” to the presence of Mrs.
Synnot at a certain alleged brothel in Carlton
(Melb.) during a time when, on the showing of
Divorce Court evidence, the lady must have
been in England. It was not a satisfactory
enquiry, from a common-sense point of view.
The constables, admitting that their story was
flatly contradicted by more or less convincing
proofs, desired to show good excuse for their
error in the fact that a dozen other casual wit-
nesses had made the same mistake. For some
reason the Enquiry Board declined to allow
this testimony. The constables were dealt
with as three unsupported, reckless tra-
ducers of Mrs. Synnot’s character, and their
dismissal from the force ensued. Thereupon
the Synnot case of mistaken identity was
written up by a barrister, whose account
was circulated in the Vie. Assembly. The
result of this light on the subject was a demand
to know whether the Crown Law Department
intended to take proceedings against all wit-
nesses who swore to having seen Mrs. Synnot
in Carlton at the time of her alleged residence in
England. The question challenged a re-opening
of the Synnot case. Premier Turner replied
that the Law Department “saw no evidence to
charge any of the persons referred to with
criminal intent,” and there the matter dropped
in the meantime. The constables are dismissed,
without being disgraced. Had all the witnesses
who shared their unaccountable error been civil
servants, they too must have been cast from
their billets unless the Enquiry Board had con-
descended to notice that, where so many are
deceived, none can be severely blamed for falling
into the general error. Very little reflection
should convince any intelligent thinker that
there is virtue in numbers as regards
witnesses in a case of mistaken identity.
The Synnot divorce-case, on the surface, was a
husband’s desperate attempt to get free at any
cost. In the first instance, Mr. Synnot pleaded
his wife’s desertion (with a .£lO,OOO settlement
in her pocket) as the sole ground for a divorce.
He was put out of court by his own deed of
separation, and subsequently he returned to
the attack armed with allegations of his
wife’s uuchastity during their long separa-
tion. These allegations were disproved by an
alibi. Mrs. Synnot, it seemed, had been
12,000 miles away from Carlton at the time of
her alleged residence there in a house of ill-
fame. Also, it was denied that the house ever
had any ill-fame. Altogether, Synnot cut a
very unsatisfactory figure in this second abor-
tive suit for divorce, and the world considered
him a wild, vindictive suspector of the lawful
wife who wouldn’t live with him. The true
facts of this second suit are not on the surface,
however. Mr. Synnot didn’t hunt up charges
against his wife’s honor. They grew out of
the original “ desertion ” suit, and were brought
to him by people who smiled at his ignor-
ance of matters which they (as they thought)
were well aware of. These volunteers
waited upon Synnot’s solicitors with various
tales relating to Mrs. Synnot and the
aforesaid house at Carlton. They agreed in
swearing that the house had an immoral
character in the neighborhood, and that Mrs.
Synnot was undoubtedly one of its inmates for
a while some years ago. They had recognised
her in court, or elsewhere, during the first
trial. Naturally, Synnot applied at head-
quarters for the names of policemen who were
on the Carlton beat at the time referred to.
Three policemen were named, and they, being
asked what they “ knew” about Mrs. Synnot,
declined to open their mouths without autho-
rity from the superintendent, or chief commis-
sioner, or somebody. The authority was forth-
coming. Then the three policemen joined the
chorus of allegation against Mrs. Synnot, and
so got into their present trouble. They also
were prepared to identify Mrs. Synnot as a
one-time lodger at a house of assignation.
It was their misfortune to have watched
the house, together with the scandalised
neighbors, in days of yore, and to have
firmly impressed upon their memories the
face and figure of a female whom they
now recognised without doubt, as they thought,
in the person of Mrs. Walter Synnot. This
is the story of a remarkable case of mistaken
identity, not apparently of reckless swearing,
and it explains why the Crown Law Dept.,
although sanctioning the policemen’s dismissal,
sees “ no evidence to charge any witnesses with
criminal intent ” —a very ingenious way of put-
ting it, anyhow. But there is a sequel to the
story. When the three policemen were being
sat upon by the Akehurst Board, fresh wit-
nesses volunteered a firm opinion that Mrs.
Synnot is the counterpart of the Carlton
sojourner. They had heard of the letters
proving that the lady was in England at a cer-
tain time, nevertheless they planked down their
honest belief that she is indistinguishable at
this distance of time from the person they saw
at Carlton. Mrs. Synnot has been the victim
of an exceptionally remarkable resemblance
to another woman. That’s her misfortune,
and three policemen are suffering for it.
The indications are that the man Johnson,
convicted of perjury in the notorious Butler
divorce-suit, which unexpectedly sprung some
alarming surprises on the public, will have his
condemnation endorsed by N.S.W. Executive.
The reconsideration of Johnson’s case has been
one prolonged farce. How invidious to place
a minor and junior Judge in a position in which
he might have to pass adverse judgment on a
superior and senior Judge, and to rebuke
inferentially the Attorney-General, and the
lawyer-Premier of an Executive in which there
are five lawyers or quasi-lawyers, with the
Lieut.-Governor as Chief Justice and colleague
of the Judge whose judgment would be impugned
by Johnson’s release ! For weeks the enquiry
was dragged along ; then Minister Gould wrote
“a minute,” which, judging by inspired press
expressions, was “ several hours ” long ; and last
week Premier Reid solemnly assured the
Assembly that he ‘ * was reading ” the docu-
ments “as rapidly as he could,” while his
colleagues would also have to read them.
O’Sullivan, M.P.: “In the meantime the man
is suffering.” Reid (blandly),: “He may have
to continue to suffer.”
The Age discourses on numerous baseless
charges lately brought against men by hys-
terical females in a manner not calculated to
please the W.C.T.U. and other vice-sniffers: —
Mrs. Potiphar, when urged to revenge merely by an
unappreciative swain, is not nearly so dangerous as a
woman in pursuit of pounds, shillings and pence, or a
young girl in whom a faulty physiological and psycho-
logical equipment produces hallucinations and delusions.
Unfortunately, there seems to he a marked tendency to
such hysterical developments among the Australian
women, although we have not as yet reached
the stage where a physician is afraid to see
a female patient except in the presence of a
nurse, as is the case in some American cities. ... It
is well-known that modest women will, when under the
influence of anaesthetics, indulge in language from which
the vilest of mankind would shrink, and it was recently
stated by a Victorian school-inspector that immodest
pictures and words were more frequently to be found in
the girls’ than the boys’ buildings. The experience of
hospital authorities is similar, and the cold bath and
electric battery have frequently to be applied to female
patients to reduce their amatory hysteria, just as the
Abrahams family was cured of malingering.
♦
The N.S.W. Upper House rejected the Bill for
its reformation by a large and bad-tempered
majority, and this week it proceeds, in a soured
frame of mind, to consider the Land and
Income-tax Bills, the new Customs tariff, and
the proposed loan for forest-thinning and build-
ing the Bogan bridge at Monkey. If the coun-
cillors remain in their present aggravated state
they will probably reject some of these
measures, or alter them out of all recognisable
shape, and then there will be a most interesting
deadlock in politics. For though the people
gave a reasonably distinct “ mandate ” for
the reformation of the Legislative Council, and
though there is a big Government majority
in the Assembly, it is really no easier
to reform the Upper House than it was before.
The present members won’t reform themselves.
The Governor, it is at least ten chances to one,
won’t consent to appoint 40 more members to
carry the reform scheme, the average Governor
being a person who dislikes violent excitement.
The British Tory Parliament, it is at least
twenty chances to one, won’t interfere ; and
the old men of the Upper House won’t die off
fast enough to remove the present hostile
majority within any reasonable time. The vast
blubbery inertia of the Constitution is against
Reid, whose situation in this matter is about as
unpromising as that of the person who tries t<?
convert a Chinaman.

Miss Juliet Flegeltaub Wray made her
f|rst Sydney appearance under hard conditions.
She caught a violent cold to begin with, and the
opening of “ Madame, Angot ” had to be post-
poned while she stayed at home and coughed her
glossy head off in strict seclusion, and had her
dainty feet put in hot gruel, and mustard plasters
fixed on her. When she did appear she was only
half recovered, and the rain came down in a
deluge, and Sydney was like the Great Dismal
Swamp, and the Lyceum was barely half-full.
Yet, despite all this, Juliet Wray was a pro-
nounced success. She made a bright and clever
Cilairette all through acts I. and II.; but in act
111. she carried the house by storm, and the air
grew thick with the heavy, excited breathing of
Israel,,and the new star had more encores than
she knew what to do with. The revolutionary
ballad which Clairette gets off her mind before she
is dragged away by the surreptitious policeman
with the blackened eye was sung quite as well as
it has been done in Sydney for many years past,
but Miss Wray’s triumph arrived when she ap-
peared in a brief striped skirt, and two limbs
which are a great deal less scraggy than „was
generally expected, and announced in melody her
resolve to follow in the footsteps of her sainted
mother. As an actress, Juliet is considerably
above the operatic average, so far as can
be judged from the results up to date,
though she makes casual mistakes here and
there as the result of zeal and inexperience.
She has an excellent voice, a graceful figure, and
two expressive eyes set in a not unhandsome
countenance ; also, she dances remarkably well,
and if she doesn’t fly off the handle, as the result
of too much early success, she should become a
star of magnitude.
* * »
This week Miss Wray is suffering a tem-
porary eclipse in the “Old Guard,” which is
Lauri’s opera almost exclusively, and affords very
little room for anybody else. Lauri has fixed up
sundry new tricks since his last appearance as
Polydore, and makes the “Guard” one continuous
yell of mirth from first to last. Miss Juliet Wray
fills the tight uniform of Patatout in a way
which does honor to those historic pants, and
May Pollard and Florence Young represent the
two girls who got mixed in infancy. The
former, .as the principal mixed girl, strikes
a very large patch indeed. The male -parts
of the oast are much as they were befere.
On Saturday and Monday “ Nitouche ” and “ An
Arcadian Eve ” will be presented together if the
co. can get through that stupendous double bill,
and “Dorothy” and “Paul Jones” will follow
for two nights each.
* * sie *
Mr. Dacre and Miss Amy Roselle com-
menced a short season at the Criterion on Satur-
day with the evergreen “ Scrap of Paper.” Why
these two brilliant comedians failed to catch on
better during their last Sydney season is one of
the mysteries of this complicated and ill-managed
globe. The fact that they are only doing mode-
rate business now is another darksome conun-
drum that will never be explained until the sea
gives up its dead in a shocking state of damage
and with prawns in their hair. The “Scrap of
Paper ” is a bright and healthy comedy, and the
acting of the two principals is as nearly faultless
as the rather depressing circumstances admit.
Miss Roselle is especially to be commended as one
of the very few ladies who can fill a comic part to
perfection, the average comedienne being about as
really humorous as a, ton of coals. Mr. Plimmer
takes Scot Inglis’s 'old character as the,weary
baronet, and though his natural energy pre-
vents him looking so artistically weary as his
predecessor, he fills the part sufficiently well. Miss
Kate Bishop’s Aunt Zenobia is a work of genius in
corkscrew ringlets, and Blanche Carlyle’s bright
smile is alone worth the admission-money. There
are several morals in the “ Scrap of Paper,” and
one of them is that it is safer to aggravate the
tiger in his lair than to go fooling around with the
wife of a depressed and torpid-looking baronet.
Torpid baronets are dangerous when roused. An-
other moral is that when the average woman is
calling on the other fellow and suddenly hears her
husband thundering on the stair with murder in
his footsteps, she always hides in the next room,
which is a supremely foolish proceeding, as that
is the first place where he looks for her. At such
a time as that, the really intelligent woman crawls
under the sofa.
** * *
Her Majesty’s (Sydney) re-opens on Satur-
day under the lesseeship of Alfred Woods and
Mrs. Marshall. The first production is “The
Prairie King,” by Garnet Waloh, a hair-raising
shriek in four acts, with the biggest tank ever
seen in this country. It is billed as “absolutely
the greatest, spectacular production ever placed on
the Australian stage,” and one cheering circum-
stance connected with it is the fall of a horse and
rider from a bridge into the aforesaid tank—the
astonished quadruped coming'down a distance of
20 feet. When he strikes the water he makes the
biggest splash that ever happened south of the
line—or at least he ought to, if he is any kind of
a horse. Mr. King Hedley, “ the renowned
American actor,' whom The Bulletin doesn’t
remember hearing about until now, is the star,
and the scenery is reported to be, next to the
splash and the tank, the greatest event in this
hemisphere.
** * *
Last week Prof. Kennedy gave a test ex-
hibition of his mesmeric powers to the medical
profession and the press of Sydney, and after the
medical profession had carefully inspected the
dismal “ subject ” who chews the raw onion and
goes up in the imaginary balloon, it decided that
he was absolutely genuine. The Bulletin, so
far, hasn’t worried much about the genuineness of
the subject; its prevailing impression has been
that the show—which is still prospering amaz-
ingly—is a ghoulsome exhibition, and its prevail-
ing desire is to give the Professor a dead cat on
his birthday. As a comprehensive expression of
its views on the subject that defunct animal covers
the ground better than a whole essav.

* * iff *
Sydney’s new music-hall, “ Tattersall ”
Adams s Palace of Varieties, to be erected near
the celebrated Marble Bar, promises to be the
most dazzling Thespian temple in Australia. The
new house, which will seat 1500, is to have a
spacious promenade after the manner of the
modern London music-hall, and the style of
entertainment to be provided is to be of the
variety brand, with leg-show and living picture
attachments. The decorations for the new temple
are being designed by Phil Goatcher, and will be
carried out under that eminent personage’s
direction. Mr. Clarence Backhouse, who designed
Sydney Lyceum and “modernised” the Sydney
Criterion, is the architect.
** * *
Prof. Anderson Stuart’s new patent, self-
adjusting larynx is not regarded with favor in
musical circles. It is said that the possessor of
one of these handy mechanical larynxes can fit
himself out as a basso, a baritone, tenor, contralto
or soprano by merely rigging up his apparatus
with the necessary reed. The prospect of the
whole of the grand opera of the future being sung
by one large, red, perspiring man whose natural
voice is like the bleat of a lost sheep in the wilder-
ness, but who carries a rare assortment of voices
in his vest pocket, and puts his larynx in a tum-
bler when going to bed, is indeed trying to the
trade; but entrepreneurs are bound to be influ-
enced by the large saving in travelling expenses,
and by the knowledge that with such a fit-out there
is no fear of their rival sopranos getting loose in
the dressing-room, and making each other quite
unfit for publication for the love of art. This
invention gives new life to a venerable gag, and
the falsetto voice will probably one day be quite
as common as the false set o’ teeth. This patent
will make an elegant and appropriate birthday
or Christmas present for many public singers The
Bulletin wots of. If Sims Reeves—
** * *
“ Gentleman Joe ” hasn’t thrown out any
new shoots at Melb. Princess’s. The story of the
impossible cabman who got .£lO on account for
writing an anti-fat advt. was expected to spread
itself into a bolder show after being watered with
a people’s cheers, but the poor little narrative
made no fresh growth. The god who led the
laughter on the opening night was not engaged to
reappear, and in the absence of that gracious deity
encores waxed fainter, and the curtain began to
fall every evening at 10.20, in spite of the musical
conductor's best efforts to, prolong the agony.
However, the law of compensation applies to a
theatrical season, and “Gentleman Joe’s” inher-
ent weakness will react favorably on the subse-
quent proceedings. “ The Shop Girl” (limited to
one week’s run in May last) comes up for another
week’s revival on Saturday, with the proud con-
sciousness of being rather better in all respects
than the mystery of a hansom cabby. Many
people, having been warned by their friends
against the current attraction, are waiting
patiently for the change of bill, therefore “The
Shop Girl ”is fated to boom. It boasts the special
recommendation of a fat part for Fred Kaye, and
everybody has learned to love little Kaye. He is
a pure comedian, knowing when to be funny with
his face, and when to be wildly comic about the
legs, and never trying to excite more mirth than
the occasion can carry.
** * *
The F east of Horse drawefch nigh to Mel-
bourne, which accounts for a sudden awakening
of show business. On Saturday the estimable
Joseph of Canaan ” will be produced at the
Royal by George Rignold. .This is a much ex-
pected event. Rumors from Sydney have painted
“Joseph’s” spectacular surroundings in glowing
colors, and the Y.M.O.A. is said to be coming in
a holy body to enjoy Mrs. Potiphar’s discomfiture.
Also, the more fleshly patrons of the drama—the
devilled worldlings, so to speak—will seize this
last chance of sampling a first-class actor in a new
character. For although they ought at all times
to assemble and meet together where their old
favorite Rignold is fluttering his banner, yet
ought they most chiefly so to do when he plays a
farewell season at popular prices.. “Joseph of
Canaan ” deserves encouragement, and The Bul-
letin hopes that an unprecedented yield of corn
in Egypt will result.
* * *
Fitzgerald Bros.’ new London co. wih
arrive at Melb. Exhibition Building on Saturday.
The Fitz.'s latest enterprise is not a circus, save
as regards Mahomet, the Talking Horse, and he
is more human than most members of society.
Mahomet can tell the time by any watch, and tip
the Caulfield Cup winner with his tail. A few
selected animals, ranging from a low comedian
rooster to an .elephant, are travelling with this
educated steed, but the 00. otherwise consists of
charming women and mighty men, all of whom
do something unusual in a style peculiarly their
own. One lady is an “absolute miracle,” who turns
a double back somersault from the roof, another is
described as the “Swirl Dancer.” Prof. Peart dives
from the domqjnto a tank of water only 6ft. deep,
whilst a brother professor is chucked from a less
height into the same tank after being sewn in a
bag. Every item on the Fitzgerald programme is
a novelty more or less startling. A shilling ticket
admits bearer to the Exhibition, and the season is
limited to nine performances, including a “pair
of matinees.’’,

At Melb. Opera House they broke out in

The Late Mr . J. M. TOOHEY.
Mark Twain, who, at time of writing, is
half through his course of ruminative “talks” at
Melbourne Bijou, will have moved along to Ben-
digo when number 816 of this scurrilous rag is let
loose upon the world. Mark Twain in one city
doesn’t seem to present any points of difference
from the same humorist addressing another audi-
ence, and his Sydney success follows him round,
as a matter of course. The term, “ genuine
attraction,” applies to the creator of the “Jump-
ing Frog ” with unusual force. He is just
Mark Twain telling his own yarns with his own
’Murkan accent to admirers of the man and all
his works. Non-admirers signify the same by
stopping away. His personality is everything
that one has been led to expect, which is greatly
to Mark Twain’s credit, inasmuch as some popular
authors are addicted to the venial crime of look-
ing more literary on the frontispiece of their
books than in real life.

The Bulletin feels glad to mention that
George Coppin is no more dead than of yore. A
Melb. evening newspaper lately interviewed the
venerable identity on the occasion of his retire-
ment from the Yic. Leg. Council, and the account
of what he said to the ’Erald’s young man was
ushered into type by sundry alarming headlines,
calculated to make the reader give Mr. Coppin up
for lost. Readers, as a rule, stop short at the
cross-headings—in an evening journal—hence this
timely mention that the curtain has not dropped
on George Coppin, except in a purely political
sense.

Pastor Walters’s Scriptural play, “ Joseph
of Canaan,” which goes up at Melb. Royal on
Oct. 5, comes upon the smellful city at a time
when the heavenly pilots are engaged in a spring
cleaning of their various meeting-houses. The
black-coated brigade are flocking into Melb. from
the country, and the simultaneous assemblage of
Joßeph s brethren at the T.R. at last affords to
the shepherds a reasonable and perfectly proner
excuse for visiting the theatre. The delirious
spectacle of Maud Williamson, with her soulful
eyes, attempting an assault on the guileless George
Kignold s virtue, should be a particularly joyful
experience for the sky-pilots.

Dontcherknow!
Miss Flo. Young’s Katisha is her most consistent effort
up to date. In the Melbourne performance she obligingly
sacrificed her fatal beauty, and made the lady of the
miraculous shoulder-blade quite sufficiently hideous. She
acted and sang the part, too, with such’ spirit that the
second act had quite the vim of other days, when tiny
Alice Barnet gurgled, “Oh, I’m such a silly little goose.”
One thing Flo. would not do—she refused to black out
her pearly teeth, so the tusk that almost stood alone
escaped mention for that occasion only.
Max O’Rell is said to have written a four-act comedy
which will be produced in America next month, as well
as a play for the Daly Co., to burst forth a few months
later. The story sounds bogus. Max has a Murkan
lecturing tour in view, and isn’t just now likely to spring
a probably sick dramatic effort upon possible patrons.

Katharine Hardy, the new B. and B. girl, is chaperoned
by a big sister. Katharine hails from Leeds, Eng., talks
Yorkshire, is goodly to look upon, and was respectfully
admired by the Girl of Yarmouth, who “acted” (save the
mark !) with her in Melbourne and Brisbane.
Re-christening an unpopular theatre is the weakest of
all devices for fetching the public. The old name is, at
any rate, an advt. of the house’s whereabouts, and, as for
bad luck sticking to any particular place of amusement —
well, it ceases to stick when the right show comes along.
London Lyceum was a notoriously “ unlucky ” house for
years before Bateman produced Irving there, in “The
Bells.” Then it began to develope into the boss temple
of the British drama, and up went the rent at the first
opportunity.
Sydney loses a nne musician and expert musical critic in
Neville Barnett, who died last week of throat and lung
disease at the early age of 41. Mr. Barnett was well-
known as the organist at St. Mary’s Cathedral, and for
some time acted as musical critic for S.M. Herald and
Evening News. He has left the musical world richer by a
number of excellent compositions and two works on
theory.

him a salary of two guineas a week to walk about.

A BEAUTIFUL COMPLEXION.
Ib has long been known to scientists that for
improving the appearance and beautifying the
face oatmeal is far and away superior to all other
foods, but it had cne drawback— the tendency to
heat the blood. To overcome this defect the at-
tention of eminent scientists has been devoted to
the removal of this imperfection; and now, after
long years of careful and diligent research, art has
triumphed, and Uncle Toby’s Rolled Oats is the
result, for, by skilful manipulation in the manu-
facture, all the heating properties have been ex-
tracted, so that now a beautiful compl xion of
peach and ivory may he had by anyone who imkefl
a continuous use of Uncle Toby’s Rolled Oats.
