Prayers for Rain .
People prayed for rain, and they got it; but they
often suppl icate for what they cannot get. They wish
to strike a winner at the races, or thatthenex-tchild
will be a boy, or that they may win a sweep ; and
now that their prayers for rain have been answered,
they will keep on praying for anything else they
desire, and hope they may get it. Smokers have
for a long time been praying for a genuine, full-
flavored, fragrant 6d. cigar ; and this is now on
the market in B. L. Corto Rothschilds. All first-
class tobacconists keep them in stock, and every
judge of the fragrant weed pronounces them Al—
the best value obtainable for the money. Every-
thing comes to those who wait; and for many
years tourists have wondered why the B. L. Corto
Rothschilds was not stocked in the colonies, ft
is only after a great deal of expense that the pre-
sent importers have been enabled to arrange for *
regular supply. Try the B. L. Corto Rothschilds,
and you will be satisfied.*
The Wild-Cat Column .
The Bank of North Queensland has given one
month’s notice of the release of all its reconstructed
deposits. Some of the other banks which died and were
resurrected in ’93 have set free a portion of their locked-
up cash ; the great majority have repaid nothing so tar ;
one has reconstructed a second time, and one has hurst a
second time and gone into liquidation. The B.N.Q. is the
only one of the lot which has released the reconstructed
money in full, and that without any "conversion” pro-
cess or any borrowing in fresh quarters. Asa lot of the
cash will probably be re-deposited at the reduced rates
now current, this proceeding ought to result in a con-
siderable saving in interest.
Melb. tram-shares, which crawled gradually
down to about 7s. 6d., have gone up by another gradual
crawl to a little over 15s. When they were down at zero,
in the worst days of the depression, large orders came
from England, and John Bull, who mops up everything
sooner or later, bought nearly the whole concern for a
gong. There are very few Vic. holders left now, so the
rise aoesn’t help the province much. A large and com-
rehensive absentee-tax for the discouragement of that
unlimited swallower, J. Bull, is still much wanted in
Australia. , , , ,
Two big Sydney institutions, the St. Joseph’s
Building Society and the Excelsior Land, Investment and
Building Cos., are calling meetings this month. Both
recently agreed to pay 5 per cent, interest on their
deposits, but they find the burden too vast to stagger
under* and intend asking a reduction—probably to 3 per
cent. * • * •••
The Freehold Investment and Banking Cos.
fMelb.) has carried through the most remarkable recon-
struction scheme of the century. The alleged business
and all the immense mass of unsaleable property are to be
handed over to the Freehold Assets Realisation co., which
is to issue to the creditors 5 per cent, deposit-receipts in
navment of their claims. The receipts are payable when
the co. feels equal to the work, which may be 10 or 100
vears henoe or more. The interest is to be paid in deposit
receipts also, and these are likewise to be redeemed when
the co. feels equals to the work. If the co. never feels
equal to the work it may go on accumulating deposit-
receipts for ever. It is a beautifully simple and restful
device, and the co. can now lie low and wait for the next
boom, ’whenever that may be.
At a meeting of the Vie. Freehold Investment
and Banking Cos., a shareholder; “They call us Austra-
lians bank-thieves at Horae. I have recently come from
there." “Us Australians!” Truly “a little leaven
leaveneth the whole lump.”

The Bank of Australasia cables a few particu-
lars of another depressing balance-sheet—one that is
even more depressing than the last. The net profit for
the half-year is £40,854 against £45,734 last half-year,
£4B 570 for the half ended April, '94, and £60,606 for the
one’ended October,’93. A 5 per cent. div. swallows
£4O 000, so there is only £854 to carry forward. The
Australasia’s div. has been falling away from the old,
gorgeous 14 per cent, for a good many years past, and the
present div. represents still another drop. Deposits and
advances, which have been decreasing with more or less
regularity for five years or so, have again diminished
slightly. The present rate of profit represents about
£3 Bs. per cent, per annum on the shareholders’ capital
and reserve-fund, so apparently the profits of the ordinary
banking business-despite the fact that it is a very large
one-are exceedingly limited.

The quarter’s revenue in N.S.W. is £IOB,OOO
more than for the corresponding quarter of ’94. In Vic-
toria there is a similar increase of £15,000, and in South

provinces looks almost like a general revival, it such a
thing be really possible.
* * * •
The conversion of the N.S.W, 5 per cent. loans,
which fall due next year, into a 3J per cent, liability will
save £14,661 annually in interest. In England a fixed
sum is set aside each year to meet the charges on the
public debt, and when the interest is reduced by a con-
version the amount thus saved goes to swell the sinking-
fund and make the process of extinction go on all the
faster. Thev are not built that way, however, in N.S.W.
There never was a N.S.W. Government yet which could
be trusted with a sinking fund any more than a mongrel
could be trusted alone with a beef steak.
* * * *
Victoria is much troubled because its railway
revenue doesn’t cover working expenses and interest on
cost, and is making extraordinary efforts to extinguish
the annual deficiency of £380,000. And yet, so far as
concerns its public works department, that province is a
lot better off than any of its neighbors. In Victoria the
net revenue derived from works that have been built with
borrowed money covers the interest all but £546,000. In
N.S. Wales the deficit to be provided out of revenue is
£592,000 ; in Queensland it amounts to £642,000. The
other provinces represent varying degrees of financial
disaster, Tasmania being, in proportion to its liabilities,
the worst of all. The N.S.W. railways yield the best per-
centage of profit in Australasia, but the loan-account is
weighed down by the interest on vast sums spent on
roads, bridges, dredging, and other jobs that yield no
revenue whatever, and the new forest-thinning industry
will further aggravate the evil.

«■* * *
A lot of good might be done by »a banking law
providing that all property which comes into the hands
of a bank by foreclosure shall be disposed of inside of
two years. It would prevent' the accumulation of pro-
perty in the bank’s hands. It would consequently pre-
vent all the banks becoming gorged with land at the
same time, and the consequent simultaneous rush to
realise, and the forcing down of values through half the
earth being offered for sale at once. It would make
the banks clear up their losses as they went along instead
of spending 10 boom years in accumulating foreclosed
land and putting it in the balance-sheet at book-values,
and then 10 busted years in making awful revelations
and gathering up the ruins and reconstructing. In
short, it would compel the ostrich to take his head out of
the sand every two years and realise exactly' where he is,
Instead of keeping it buried for an indefinite period as at
present.
* * * •
Maoriland is exporting timber at the average
rate of about £150,000 worth per an., and the total value
of the timber cut down in the fast decaying forests of ttie
province is about £1,000,000 yearly. There are but
20,000,000 acres of forest-land now remaining, and the
only attempt made to repair the devastation consists of
the establishment of about a million acres of State
reserves. Unless vigorous steps are taken in the direc-'
tiou of forest planting, the bottom will presently fall out
of a valuable industry.
* * * »
A Bill now before Maoriland Parliament pro-
vides that printed mortgage forms shall be sold at every
post-office in the province, so that any person of average
intelligence—if there is such a person—can fill in his own
mortgage, and save legal expenses. The man who lends
the money can help him to fill it m, and if, between them,
they don’t succeed in making the document valid, that is
the lender’s funeral. Anyhow, the constant desire to save
legal expenses is a good feature in Maoriland legislation.

Why can Queensland and Maoriland launch
boldly into meat-export trade, while N.S.W. has the
greatest difficulty in slowly organising its initial lines?
Because both Bananaland and Maoriland, whatever their
political faults, have seen the productive independence
that protection gives, while N.S.W. has only legislated for
the fatted importer.

If a N.S.W. alderman goes insolvent, he must
resign, and cannot stand again as a candidate for an
aldermanio vacancy until he has got his certificate of dis-
charge. Again : If a N.S.W. alderman resigns before his
alderraancy expires by effluxion of time, he is liable to a
fine of £25. How does the law dovetail these two clauses?
Does it fine every insolvent alderman and make him
hankrupter ?

Farmer-teamsters at Milthorpe (N.S.W.) have
contracted to take wool, hides, and sundries to Sydney,
an estimated 11-days’ journey, at £8 per ton. The railway
freight is £5 per ton. Being their own fodder-growers,
the teamsters calculate that they can earn 25s. a day.
That, however, is low earning, indeed, for a man and a
team.

A cable boycott is the initial trouble in estab-
lishing the new Sydney daily. Existing “ great ” dailies,
though they publicly hate each other, are glad to com-
bine for their cable communication and divide the cost.
A special service would cost the projected journal many
thousands more than people care to trifle with.
♦ * * *
In refusing to impose a 25 per cent, duty on
imported newspaper-supplements, Maoriland Parliament
has practically rendered futile the duty fixed on stereos,
and matrices. A majority of the M.L. meat-wraps make
extensive use of matter imported in one form or the other,
and, as it mostly conies from ’Murka, the alleged news-
paper is usually an incongruous admixture of obvious
Yankeeisms and home-grown incompetency. The stereos,
will now give place to supplements printed in Victoria
and sold at 80s. a 1000, or less. Had both duties been
made law, numerous small sheets would, no doubt, have
succumbed—rather a blessing than a curse.

The first Act of Parliament passed in Australia
was one making promissory notes legal. Before that the
common form of I O U was in vogue and was treated as a
mere reminder of a debt of honor.
* » * *
A cunningly-conceived little bit of N.S.W*
lawyers’ law makes it out that damages obtained against
a solicitor by a client for negligence in the conduct of a
case are not provable against the solicitor’s estate if he
becomes bankrupt. The intricate double-shuffle of this
pretty arrangement should amply compensate any client
with a sense of,humor— especially as he would not be
likely to get anything anyway out of such an estate.

“Tom ” Russell, erstwhile of Maoriland, made
£60,000 by a lucky spec, in VVaihi stock, just after being
hit by the B.N.Z. and N.Z. Loan and Mercantile Agency
troubles. Fourteen or 15 years ago, when a bosom pal of
the Prince of Wales, he “ got the office ” that the British
fleet was to he sent to the Dardanelles to ohe< kmate the
Russian advance on Constantinople, and bought Turkish
bonds in a panicky market. The astute Thomas is said
to have cleared £750,000 by that little deal.
♦ ♦ ♦ ♦
Warrnambool (Vie.) Council recently received
three tenders for contracts. One was for horses, drays
and drivers at 3s. lid., another at 4s. 5d, and the third
at 4s. 6d. per day. The 3s. lid. tender is the lowest
figure reached yet for this kind of work. Even if the
tenderers—who are. all men with families—stole their
horses, drays and horse-feed it might puzzle even the
Calculating Boy to reckon how they could pay rent and
feed and clothe themselves and their wives and children
on the money they would have to draw. The Council
very properly rejected all three tenders, although the
Mayor explained that the men who sent them in did nob
employ workmen, bub “sweated” themselves. These are
the kind of men who are the worst sweaters of others
when they have the opportunity.
* • » ♦
Nothing is so indicative of the depths to which
trade has fallen in Australia as the fact that no effort has
been made of late years by the various old and well-
known firms, who have voluntarily put up their shutters,
to dispose of their businesses under the ancient formula
of "sound and well-going concerns.” It is advertised in
the press that the stock of will be auctioned on such-
and such a date, the firm having decided to retire from
business. Nothing more ; nothing about good-will, and
so there’s an end of it.

Apropos the scare in Vie. bung-trade, the ingoing
is now very often not a matter of money providing a pro-
spective tenant possesses a few hundreds and a fair his-
tory. Scores of pubs, are being dropped by the breweries
as the leases expire, the consequence being that nowa-
days the future bonitaoe deals straight with the land-
lord, is tied to no one, buys where and from whom he
likes. Wholesalers tumble over one another for ids
orders, and the slave of the past enjoys in silence the
prospect of getting even with his masters of the days
when bills of sale were a power in the Kingdom of Bung.

A young lady has recovered .£500 damages from
the Balmain New Ferry Co. (Sydney) on the ground that
the ropes were loosened ai d the steamer moved while she
was iroing along the gangway, and she got scared and
jumped on board hurriedly, and as she landed on her feet
on the deck she received a shock which seriously injured
her system. The verdict was given on the ground—so far
as appears from a rider attached —that the gangway
should he shifted before the ropes are loosened. If this
is done in future, the next plaintiff will probably get a
shock by falling over the rope while getting on after_tne
gangway is shifted, and another jury wdUgive £SOO
damages on the ground that the ropes ought to be re-
moved first.

Highland Mary mine (Swamp Oak, N.S.W.)
has cleaned up a crushing of 69 tons stone for 3-ooz. re-
torted gold. The Highland Mary (owned by Brown
Bros.) extends over 16 acres, and the reef for 1600 ft. is
gold-hearing. The shaft is only down 90ft., and the shoot
of gold-bearing stone is known to extend 280 ft. This
quart z crushes out by ordinary battery appliances ooz. to
the ton, and yields 30cwt. concentrates to 70 tons of bat-
tery stone, worth loz. to the ton. The only capital put
into the mine was the £lO paid to Govt, for the lease, ana
the outlay since has been won from the stone, ihe
owners reckon that the yields will pay for additional
machinery and increased development until a return of
£I2OO a fortnight may become the usual thing.

In addition to treating about 480 tons of sulp-
hides weekly at its own concentrating mill, Junction
(8.H.) has just contracted to supply Dry Creek works
with 2000 tons, at the rate of 500 tons a week. The
Junction mill is doing good work, and the mine has
developed well lately. Joel Phillips, the present manager,
a peculiarly-unpretentious man, has served this 00.
admirably.
Half-yearly report of the B.H. South, for the
terra to June 30, is issued only at the end of Sept. It is
good enough, however. “ The net gain to the co. for
the period under review amounts to £11,738 13s. 7d., and
it will be observed that there is a credit of £4219 16s. od.
in lieu of a debit of £7518 16s. lOd. to profit and
loss account, as in the last statement submitted.
This report of the South is peculiarly valuable. It
is the first issued by any B.H. co. working sulphides
only during the whole half-year. To get this net profit
of £II,OOO odd, 28,775 tons of ore were treated, pro-
ducing 9867 tons concentrates ; hut only 8699 tons of the
latter were shipped ; the balance remained on the mine.
But most of this balance has been drawn against. The
mining and treating, from breaking the ore in the face
until it is bagged and loaded ready for dispatch, cost
14s. 4Jd. per ton of ore.
Since the beginning of July there has been a
considerable, and apparently permanent, rise in the price
of lead ; moreover, many improvements have been made
in the jigging appliances. In these circumstances the
South’s profit per ton of ore should keep for some time at
very little under 10s.—amounting to a trifle under £6OO a
week.
From July 1 to Sept. 21 the quantity of concen-
trates dispatched from the South mine was 5500 tons.
A parcel of concentrates from the B.H. North
has been passing through the B.H. South concentrating
mill. It proves good milling stuff; an extraction of over
65 per cent, lead has been made.
During the four weeks ended Sept. 21 the out-
put of the Broken Hill mines was 55,182 tons ore—the
highest on record. Of this, 23,246 came from mines
other than the Proprietary. The value of the extractable
silver and lead (a very different thing, of course, from the
gross value) was about £171,000.
The Howell (Illawarra Oo.) people talk of being
able to give 30s. per ton at the mine for such sulphides as
Block 10 produces. If the South can mine and concen-
trate for 14s. 4ld. per ton, there is clearly a big profit
ahead for Block 10, whose ore the co. appears to
desire most. Of course the Ten Co.’s ore is much richer
in silver—averages, perhaps, 220 z. to 250 z., as against 7oz.
or Boz. This consideration accounts for the recent sub-
stantial rise in Block 10’s—a rise which is not, however,
altogether approved at the Hill.
The Ashcroft sulphide process hangs fire in
England. “ Show us a big lot,” they say; and so a big
lotls going across the water—several hundred tons.
David Lindsay does nob, after all, join the
Howell Westralian Exploration Cos.
Melb. Age, in its article commemorative of the
B H. Proprietary’s 100th div., started on its rounds-the
statement that the mine’s largest yield since the com-
mencement of operations “was that for the week ending
July 4, 1894, which consisted of silver 675,9130 z., lead
1820 tons, copper 595 tons.” More rubbish ! There had
been the usual half-yearly clean-up, &0., which swelled the
figures. Asa matter of fact, the silver produced in that
week was 216,0300 z., which was the least for the whole
half-year. Copper 595 tons 1 Why, the mine has never
produced that imioh in any whole half-year.
The B.H. Proprietary with its Sept, div.—the
100th—declared a Is. bonus, thus distributing altogether
£96,000 (more, by the way, than the whole of the August
gold-export from Westralia was worth). The bonus was
paid because “the fire was not so serious a matter as had
been thought.” Just about six weeks ago we were first
told that there was, in the opinion of the general ma'nager,
no fire left in the mine. Since then, when smoke
has appeared, it has been explained that “ the carbonic
acid gas was forcing out pent -up bodies of
smoke.” These and other similar explanations
have been quite \yorn out, however. If there was
ever fire in the mine it was in the last weeks of Sept.
The odor of burning pine was more noticeable then than
it ever was before ; in tact, it had hardly before that been
discernible in the towm. There appears to be fire in two
distinct places ; the subsidence in the big quarry has pro-
bably smothered the middle of it, leaving the two ends
burning. The smoke is being now led upward, and thus,
the workings being kept comparatively clean, men are
cautiously bulk-heading up towards the affected parts—a
work which is not without its danger.

A London director (Scotch) of a big (on paper)
W.A. co. lately inspected the mine. His only complaint
to the manager was about the surface hands using long-
handled shovels; in his country, he remarked, the man
who would use such a shovel would be considered down-
right lazy. “ Can’t Australian workmen bend their
backs ? ”

Society
The latest surprising aspect of the Dean
case is that Attorney-General Want says that
Salomons told him that Meagher told him that
Dean told him that he (Dean) was really guilty.
Salomons, apparently, acknowledges that he told
this to Want, but Meagher says that he
didn’t tell anything of the kind to Salomons,
and Dean says most positively that he didn’t
tell it to Meagher. Crick backs up the
Meagher - Dean aspect of the story, but, as he
can only say that he didn’t hear Meagher tell
Salomons or Dean tell Meagher, his statements
have no direct bearing on the ease. Also, Dean
runs the Mosman’s Bay steamer as usual.
» * m
In all the real, or artificial, sinuosities of
the resurrected Dean case, it must not be forgotten
that the questions and answers—the problem and
its solution—on which the whole case hinged were
purely medical ones, and that the medical mem-
bers of the Commission pronounced such doubts
of accused’s guilt as to secure his release. It
must nob be forgotten that there was found in the
dejecta enough poison to have killed all the wives
of the late Brigham Young—so much poison, at
any rate, that, had it passed through Mrs. Dean’s
system, as contended by the prosecution, she
could no more have been alive than if she had
been filled up to the neck with kerosene and set
on fire.

A SIGN OF THE TIMES.—Sketched in a Sydney book-shop.
It, as staked, Salomons, Q. 0., Attorney-
General Want, and Chief Justice Darley, were
the only persons who knew of Dean’s alleged con-
fession, and did not breathe a word of it to any-
body, how did the “rumor” come to the ears of
Kater, M.L.C., with the result that this lamb-
like politician asked a question in the House ?
Assuming, for argument’s sake, the entire truth
of Mr. Salomons’s statement and Mr. Want’s, it
must follow that Mr. Meagher has been telling a
similar story in other quarters.
• # *
The Dean-stricken barristers who have
been constructing theoretical analogies these last
few days between priests and lawyers in the
matter of the inviolability of secrets con-
fided by clients, lose their labour for the
following among a host of reasons: A peni-
tent confessing to a priest considers himself
really confessing to God. The priest is but a
medium, just as a telephone might be. The secret
communicated is, therefore, not the priest’s, but
God’s. That is the idea which rules the case. On
the other hand, when a client gives a secret to a
solicitor, it is the Devil’s —that is to say, the
solicitor’s. The client begins with him, ends with
him, and looks no further. Again, a penitent
confesses to a priest to procure pardon for some-
thing done; a client consults a solicitor not to
obtain pardon for an offence committed, but to
enlist power to prove he didn’t commit it at all.
Professions so radically distinct don’t admit of
analogical treatment.
* # #
Pastor Torrance made the Melb. Anglican
Synod laugh, the other day, by a proposal to club
incomes, and ladle out proportionally, as a solu-
tion of the difficulty of the poor bush parson. All
this is a mere glimmer of what they talk about
outside —the downright burlesquery of giving
childless Bishop Goe and wife £4O a week, with a
free house (on top of their large private income),
and then starting his reverence whining for
driblets in aid of the wretched, hard-working
Anglican preachers, one of whom, the Assembly
was informed by Mr. Torrance, has to keep self,
family, and two horses, on 30s. a week, while
another (with three horses, and a little over £2 a
week) provides for the teeming family which ever
blesses the really hardworking and starved parson.

The visiting justices at Melb. Gaol have
given Emanuel Abrahams an extra six months for
malingering. At latest dates, Louis, the last of
the “ partially paralysed ” brethren, was being
carefully fed with a view to getting him fit for the
electric current which burst up Emanuel's little
game. A gloomy look-out for Louis—
Don’t malinger longer,
Don’t malinger, Loo;
Say you’re getting stronger,
Drop the gammon, do.
Ere the sturdy prison doc.
Galvanises you,
Straighten up and dodge the shock—
Don’t malinger, Loo.
Lift your finger, don’t malinger
Any longer, Loo.

happy if mother wasn’t

was the evidence given

small boy in a Welling-

* «
A lately-deceased
Melb. policeman used
to tell his cronies this
story: “ Onedark night
I was on the Bridge
(Princes) when I heard
a cry for help. I rushed
down to Edwards’ boat-
shed, and there saw a
woman hanging on to
one of his boats. She
had, no doubt, jumped
in, but found the water
too cold. In trying to
pull her out I fell over-
board myself. She
grabbed meandfainted,
but hung on hard. I
grabbed the boat and
hung on for bare life,
for I was wearing my
heavy top coat. We
were rescued, and for
weeks afterwards I was the hero of the hour.
Everyone, from the Governor down, slobbered
over me, but I kept my mouth shut. I couldn’t
swim a stroke.”
• « *
“Whilst the leaders of society take their
wives and daughters to gaze at half-naked women
on the stage, how can we consistently object to
these girls in our hotels?” asks Or. Moloney,
Melb., in defence of the barmaid. But the apostle
of snuffle who objects to the barmaid also objects
to the shapely girl in the tableaux, and the leggy
damsel in the ballet—to woman in all shapes, in
fact, barring the grisly shape of the bony horror
that accompanies him in his “ crusade.” He does
not object on his own account—not at all, he
rather likes them plump and young out of his
business hour—but because they are a lure to the
other man. “Lead us not into temptation”
means to the professional male purist—“ Lead not
the other fellow.”

A certain Southern Mayoralty appears to
be the fate of every insolvent or assignor in that
municipality. During the last 14 years one drank
himself into insolvency, three “boomed” them-
selves bung, two have taken the Mayoral allowance
to enable them to hang out, and gone bung imme-
diately afterwards; another got 18 months for
fraud ; and nearly all the rest either were in-
solvent at various dates before accepting office, or
w'ent to the dogs afterwards.

Smellbourne’s new' evening paper, the
News, came out strong on a recent ’orrible murder:
“ When our representative visited the scene this
morning there was no sign that anything unusual
had happened in the vicinity—the trams ran the
same, the dead slumbered as peacefully in the old
cemetery, and the street-hawkers vended their
wares with the unaltered stereotyped cries.” In
the old cemetery, you see, the dead are so very
defunct. Newer corpses might have got up and
visited the gory scene along with the Noose
reporter.

Even the Sons of Temperance and children
of light generally will heave a sympathetic sigh
for a Collingwood, Yic., publican, fined £5 the
other day for having his bar-door open on the
Sabbath, although he proved that, when the police
dropped in, he had merely opened the door to get-
sixpence out of the till to put in the plate at church.
Even the “ cludgy ” lose their respect for the
law when it clashes with the profits.

Dear Bulletin, —I can show you, or any-
one else, a house in a suburb of Sydney, where, in
one 8-shillings-a-week room, live, eat and sleep-
grandmother, mother, and two sons, the latter
(apparently) about 17 and 14 years, respectively.
They were folks in good circumstances. The
father was getting <£lo or .£l2 a week for years.
He disappeared, “for reasons.” They struggled
on and on till it has come to this. Four in one
room, about 14ft. by Allow for fireplace,
boxes, etc., and imagine the rest. The eldest boy
has a billet, and keeps this establishment going,—
C.
* * *
Emma Williams, the woman sentenced by
Vic. law to be man-butchered for having drowned
her two-year-old boy, had previously appealed to
have the child taken in at a R.C. orphanage ; but,
as she was not a Catholic, this could not be done.
A bitter sectarian hatred accuses all Romish
benevolence of greedily seeking after proselytism;
hence the formulation and observance of a strin-
gent rule that strangles some possibilities of real
charity.
SAVED BY A BARMAID.
He used to be an unreflecting boozer
Till Tottie whispered softly in his ear:
“O, why are you a scandalous abuser
Of an excellent capacity for beer?
Your nose is getting red and rather bloated,
It grieves me to behold you going wrong.”
Then an alcoholic maxim Tottie quoted —
“ ‘The drinker of a little drinketh long.’ ”
“ Booze in moderation, ducky : don’t be always tight:
Swill a fair potation, ducky, twice or thrice a night.”
His manhood he asserted when she kissed him on the
brow :
The johnnie was converted, and he’s doing nicely now.
Instead of dissipating all his wages,
And running up a score upon the slate,
He began to mend his ways by eaav stages,
His manner grew impressively sedate,
His boko's pure and spolless as a lily,
The surface isn’t scrofulously rough :
And sometimes Tottie pouts, “ You sober silly,
I didn’t mean you not to take enough !”
“ Booze in moderation, ducky: don’t get beastly drunk.”
She winks a timely warning, and he toddles when she’s
wnnk:
There’s a way where’er the will is, and the boozer’s
guiding star
Is the eye of Tottie Phyllis, beaming brightly at the bar.

A circumstance connected with the Sydney
woman-mincing case recalls an incident in the
life of the late Melbourne surgeon, Beaney.
There was to be a post-mortem examination of the
body of a woman ; but, in the interim between
the decision to hold the examination and the
actual examination, certain internal organs, the
condition of which was most pertinent to the
Crown case, “ went a-missing.”
m * *
Mrs. Fred. Waub and family have, at
latest, booked their passages for Auslra'ia. Mrs.
“ Ranny ” Want remains at her place in the south
of France, where her sons will continue to manage
the vineyard which was planted at great expense
by her late husband.
* * *
F. : Shorthand and type - writers very
often find their craft a handicap. The accountant
of a big Melb. firm lately retired, and, when his
successor was appointed, they passed over the
■correspondent, because he was indispensable at the
“corr.” The man who got the billet was junior
in service and ability. The correspondent, of
course, asked the reason and found it was because
he was the only shorthander in the employ.
When he went home he found his eldest hard at
work with a Pitman teacher. “If I catch you at
that book again,” he said bitterly, “I’ll wring
your neck,”
* » *
As showing the dire poverty amongst Aus-
tralian newspaper men, a last week’s case is worth
noting. A four days’ gathering, sitting six hours
daily, wanted a verbatim shorthand note, and
legible transcript, of the proceedings. A very
moderate payment for such work—requiring the
services of, at least, one skilled and experienced
shorthander —would be <£l Is. a day for attend-
ance, and fid. per folio for transcript, say about
.£6O. Yet the job was done by an old Parlia-
mentary and newspaper hand for .£8 85.—24 hours’
actual note-taking, and about 100 hours’ tran-
scribing 1

Michael Davitt told Sydney Trades’ Hall
that he was quite pleased with the N.S.W. State
schools, and remarked that he had seen no shoe-
less children in attendance. Mr. Davitt must
have been taken to a “superior” school, where
the shoeless are not admitted. In a Sydney
suburb, one day last week, the writer counted a
dozen barefooted boys,

At a South Melb. wedding, the elderly
bridegroom omitted to kiss the blushing bride at
the proper moment. The groomsman, however,
filled the position, and was promptly knocked
down by the husband for his pains.

That the barmaid should vanish the purists all think,

* # •
Said the secretary of London cab-drivers’
benevolent society, in a recent interview :
In many ways the cabman’s lot has improved greatly of
recent years. Rubber tires, for instance, make the long
hours on the box less fatiguing. And in saving of wear
and tear of both vehicle and horse they amply warrant
the outlay of the six pounds that they necessitate.
The first Australian cabman with a smart turn-out,
who puts rubber tires on his wheels, will much
increase his earnings. Rubber tires were running
in London 10 years ago.

colors is the minimum allowance for the hat-
trimming of any woman with a spark of sartorial
ambition.

“Come early to avoid the rush” is the
motto of Vie. grovellers just now. The inhabi-
tants of Longwarry (Gippsland) have decided to
change the name of their dead-horse township to
Brassey. Longwarry was not pretty—but Brassey!
• « »
An Australian novelist, whose books have
made some stir in London, recently eloped with a
gushing young creature who had been three times
married and twice divorced. A man who could
be sentimental under such circumstances must
indeed have a powerful imagination. But pro-
bably the next time he elopes he will go a lone
hand.
• * #
A middle-aged applicant for relief at the
N.S.W. Colonial Secretary’s office said he was
the youngest of a family of 23, was himself the
father of 17 children, and his wife had that morn-
ing been delivered of triplets. He had only a
shilling to pay for necessaries.
* * #
There is one Sydney tradesman who never,
by any chance, misses a seat on the Coroner’s jury
in heavy and gruesome cases. For some years the
reporters have found this gentleman amongst the
“ 12 good men and true ” on all important occa-
sions. How he gets there is known only to him-
self and the summoning officer.
• # *
At Taree (N.S.W.) a medical man was
called to an aboriginal camp to attend, as he
thought, a gin in confinement. As the poor woman
was lying on the ground on a few damp rags, the
doctor had her removed to the hospital, when, to
his surprise, he discovered her to be a white girl
under 18 years of age! She had been lawfully
married by a clergyman on the river to the full-
blooded darkey with whom she was camped.

At Coolgardie, women, of a kind, says a
local chronicler, are now almost as plentiful as
men. There has been a large influx of ladies
from Lonsdale-street.
* * *
Speaking of the Ku-cheng massacres, Tas.
Bishop Montgomerie said there was cause for
thankfulness, inasmuch as many Christiana had
been permitted to join the noble army of martyrs;
and his female auditors “never liked him so
much ”as when he said it. Good. But what of
the fresh faces in Hell—of Chows who, though
they tried for 30 years, could never savee more than
“ God made Adam ? ” Any bishop is welcome to
the good opinion of his admirers, but no Rev.
Thomas Rot can compassionate a loveable Chow
into a decent Christian any more than he can con-
vert our old moke into a Melbourne Cup-winner.
It is a bad fault not to be a Christian ; but has a
Chow only one fault? Our old horse has two;
he’s hard to catch, and he’s no good when he’s
caught.

Melb. Bishop Goe, from his pulpit, de-
nounces sweating. Yet Mrs. Goe, as president
of Melb. Women’s Hospital, is official head of a
big institution where nurses are compelled to
work 82 hours and 92 hours in alternate weeks.
Most of them get no wages, but have to pay an
entrance fee of <£s ss. and find their own uni-
forms.
* * *
The Sydney daily papers have been
pathetically paragraphing how a newly-appointed
“sheriff’s officer,” at one N.S.W. bush town,
“ wished God-speed” to his promoted predecessor,
and spoke, too, “on behalf of the profession.
The ballad-immortalised Sam Hall is made to
imprecate :
“ The sheriff, he’ll come, too,
With all his bloody crew,
Their dirty work to do.”
Yet, whereas Sam Hall was prejudiced against
sheriffs and their myrmidons, the press of Sydney
recognises “ the profession.” But the awful joite
of wishing a bailiff “ God-speed ” shows with what
coarse freedom a superficially pious community is
willing and eager to handle the name of the
Almighty.
The weekly Q, leper, last week, was a
young white woman aged 22, residing at Brisbane.
Q. Govt., however, doesn’t believe in making a
fuss over the matter, seeing that it is itself in-
fected with leprosy—leprosy of the soul.
LOW PRICE OF WOOL.
Commenting (before last week’s welcome rise)
upon the above problem and how to grapple with
it, the National Wool Company of Melb. recom-
mends more care in the management of our small
flocks. It is pointed out that the first thing that our
producers need to observe is economy. This does
not mean parsimony in needful expenditure, nor yet
economy in the labour necessary to bring products
to perfection, but economy in its broadest, fullest
sense—the application of prudent and scientific
management to natural production, and the de-
thronement of the “ruleof thumb ” system. The
present standard average value of a well-classed
and skirted Victorian wool, merino or crossbred,
may be taken at 7gd per pound, while the irregu-
lar, badly classed and skirted wools will only
bring from 20 to 25 per cent. less. The difference
in these prices is in many instances the result of
the difference in wool-growers. The careful man
who spares no pains in bringing his product to
the highest point of perfection secures the highest
market price; the careless, indifferent man has to
take the lowest. The hint here thrown out by
the National Wool Company should command the
notice of sheepfarmers.
When the lawyer is led into errors
And discovered, he feels little awe,
For the gaol does not hold forth its terrors
To the criminal man of the law.
Lo, tlie spirit of Satan, their father,
In the mongers of “Justice” is shown —
A propensity strong to be rather
Too good to their own.
So they pray (shall we say at Megtddo ?)
That the God (that His kingdom may come ?)
Of the fatherless and of the widow
Shall especially prosper the “ bum ! ”
Shall forsake His own weaklings of clients.
At a “bum’s” mere sophistical caw,
To “ sool on ” all the dogs of the giants
Of Riches, Extortion and Law.
Though it seems to the masses a moan,
Though it brings desolation indeed,
It’s a neat little thing of our own >f
Thus to wish a bum-bailiff “ God-speed !
When we levy, the “bum ” is a Caliph,
And the fools will believe at our nod.
We’ll persuade ’em that God is the bailiff.
And, by converse, the bailiff is Gcd.

has just been issued by that old-established drapery
firm of Riley Brothers, 568-570 George-st., Syd-
ney. It contains a wonderful amount of informa-
tion, and quotes the lowest prices of good articles
in Household Drapery, Ladies’ and Children’s
Wear, Men’s and Boy’s Clothing, Boots and
Shoes. Riley Brothers still continue the system
originated by them 20 years ago of sending ,£5
Drapery Bales and £2 parcels, carriage paid, and
a large business is now done daily in
g)stal orders from 6d. upwards. One of Riley
rothers’ latest investments is the purchase of the
entire stock in N. S. W. of Dr. Scott’s celebrated
electric corsets and hair-brushes. Eminent doctors
say, and thousands of testimonials aver, that the
corsets are a splendid remedy for ladies’ ailments,
especially backache, and the electric brushes give
instant relief in cases of sick headache, neuralgia,
lleeplessness. The corsets are just like ordinary
stays, and perfect-fitting. Sizes only 24 to 2!)
inches ; price, 65., post free. The brushes are
pure bristles, not wire ; price, 3s. 6d., post free.
Please send to Riley Brothers, 568 570 George-st.,
Sydney, for new catalogue.
Ladies who value a clear skin and fresh
complexion, and who appreciate the possession of
clean, white teeth and a sweet breath, should
follow the example of their European sisters and
indulge in the use of Amykos. After being out
in the hot sun, or on a dusty road, nothing is more
cooling or refreshing than a little Amykos
sprinkled in the water in which you lave your
face and mouth. One does not require to be
using it all day long, but those who have experi-
enced its benefits say that the morning ablutions
are certainly not complete without it. Purchase
a sample bottle from your chemist.*

846 Macquarie-street North, Sydney,
October 1,1895.
Mr Dear Moorabinda—
Possibly with an eye to counteracting the sinful
effects of the Spring race-meeting, the W.C.T.U.
and its cub, the Y.W.0.A., gave a show in the
race-week, and the air in certain parts of the city
was thick with angular females trimmed like first
and third prize calves, with blue or white rosettes,
and absolutely bristling with literature en suite .
The W.C.T.U. hen-parties are not wholly un-
entertaining. I believe their social-evil evenings
—when all aspects of “evil ” are elaborately gone
into—are solemnly, but unmistakeably cerulean—
and serious blueness sinks in much deeper than
the airier sorts. The “ Y’s ” don’t actively par-
ticipate in these badly-told smoke-room stories, as
you might call them —the Y’s are probationers
who are given all the dry subjects to work up, and
one of them rose despairingly at a recent conven-
tionfand read a paper asking, “ How can we make
our meetings a source of pleasure as well as
profit ? ” The only way I can suggest would be to
“ do ” the Magdalen retreats, and so forth, a day
ahead of the allegedly Christian women, and
spring their funny stories on ’em unaware.
The W.C.T.U. is a dreary and despairing aggre-
gate, with a profound private opinion that it can
buckle the universe to a water-bottle if it really
makes up its mind to do it—but it really attempts
miracles when it ventures outside its own circle of
joyless, brow-beaten infants and hen-pecked hus-
bands. At the Convention, one lady rose to ask,
in a scandalised voice ; “Is there any rule bearing
upon individual members of our union attending
a dance ? ” and the corners of her mouth expressed
the opinion that if there wasn’t there ought to be.
From this, it would appear that when the
W.C.T.U. prevails there is to be no more dancing.
They might as well go a step further, and table a
motion, signed by several Sunday-schools, to
abolish original sin. Think of the stupendousness
of their contract —the abolition of champagne and
the “ Blue*Danube ! ”
Providence, by the way, does not appear to be
very enthusiastically on the side of the Cold Tea
Brigade. One of the first places to run out of
water during the late drought was the Echo Farm
for Inebriates —the denizens whereof had to go
more miles after water than they ever went in all
their lives after whisky. The idea of an inebriate
being expected to turn out full of enthusiasm, with
a bucket each side, in order to fetch his own
draught from the crystal spring an hour’s walk
distant is altogether too new. The Temperance
Farm must have a wonderfully reforming effect.
The success of Juliet Wray in “ Madame Angot ”
(put up for two nights, at Sydney Lyceum, last
week) was another instance of the triumph of mind
over matter. Juliet certainly isn’t up to much as
a pretty picture. Her limbs are Brown-Potterish
—she keeps that far-reaching split in Clairette’s
wedding-garment discreetly caught together in
consequence (attenuated understandings are
mostly responsible for what there is of feminine
modesty observable on the stage); her hands are
not microscopic, and her feet aren’t as pretty as
Palotta’s; also, her voice is shrill and gritty in
several places, and her wig too big for her face,
especially when her hat was off. But, despite all
these pictorial disadvantages, Miss
Clairette was quite electrifying, and she must have
an astonishing amount of pluck as well as impish
cleverness to succeed so thoroughly in a part
which Nellie Stewart has made her own. She has
several marked mannerisms, but experience will
cure all that—though the quicker she learns not
to knit her brows as if her high notes hurt her the
more effective she will be. The awful fixed grin
of the seasoned soprano may be a ghostly thing at
heart, but it looks more festive than a troubled
expression suggesting that something’s gone
wrong with the works. On the second night of
“ Madame Angot ” something did go wrong and
Miss Wray, I regret to hear, fainted after the fall
of the curtain.
The new first tenor taking Tapley’s place with
the Comic Opera Cos. —Mr. Courtice Pounds—is a
favorite with the matinde girl, mostly on account
of Tiis sweet smile, which is said to be a particu-
larly fine effort. And his legs must be all right,
too, for he is addicted to biking and is a rowing-
man as well—he and Wallace Brownlow were at
one time fellow-members of the London Rowing
Club. By repute he has a charming light tenor
voice, is a clever actor, and dances well ; but the
most wonderful aspect of Mr. P. is that he is that
rare bird, a prodigy grown to man’s estate and re-
taining a reasonable amount of genius. Mr.
Pounds was a boy-soprano (church soprano) in his
thoughtless youth, but has since made every effort,
I believe, to retrieve such a horrible past.
The maiden show of the newly-formed Society
of Artists, held at York-street skating-rink, was
sartorially remarkable for the oneness of the
artists on the mouch-about-hat question. From
the chairman to the least considerable painter of
them all everybody was roofed-in softly. Generally
speaking, I liked the pictures, including a great
many women and horse studies, which looks as if
Australian artists were addicted to sentiment and
sport. The trail of “Trilby” was over the women-
pictures, which, after all, are painted for men—
Arthur Streeton’s “Lotus Eater,” for example.
The drapery of silvery gauze was all the women
cared to look at, wishing they had it for a theatre
blouse; as for the pink girl herself on her marble
slab, with her alluring expression somewhat re-
miniscent of PouzoTs “ Femme qui rit,” I’m not
sure she wasn’t femininely designated a “hussy.”
I think the women liked the girl in “ Herrick’s
Blossoms ” best because she was so like a boned
turkey, and the best way for a woman to please a
woman is to look just like that.
There is no bone of contention on the walls this
year for people to haggle over like Sid. Long’s
impressionist bathing-scene of last spring. Among
the many flower-studies Mias Ethel Stephens’s
white roses panel caught my eye at once, and my
favorite animal picture was Mahony’s “ The Cry
of the Mothers ” —mares and foals with a cow and
calf, all, save the one dead foal, instinct with
terror, waiting the oncoming of a flood. The newly-
formed Society of Artists may look with compla-
cency on its maiden outing. Nothing less like
a debut and more like an old-established institu-
tion could well be imagined, from the string-band
at the top of the gallery to the waratahs at the
entrance. The hanging committee only made one
false step, and that was in hanging the Presby-
terian Moderator, puir mon ! quite next door to
the Lotus Eater. The rigid Kirk would surely
object to having one of its shepherds even pic-
torially in such a lightly-clad connection.
Mark Twain has gone to Melbourne after a brief
but successful Sydney season. He had rather a
cruel time of it here at the hands of the autograph-
hunters, and the fiends with confession-books, and
the pitiless possessors of albums who expect genius
to sparkle gaily gratis in order to gratify the
owner’s craving for that sort of thing. And poor
Mark sparkled duly. One of the pearls of wisdom
he left behind him was written in the album of a
Sydney musician. Wrote Mark —who was here
during the fever of the Spring meeting—“lt were
not best that all men should agree. If there were
no differences of opinion there would be no horse-
racing.” Neatly put.
The season of big charity balls will probably
come to an end with the dance at Sydney Town
Hall on 14th inst. in aid of the Bienfaisance
Fran§ais. The tickets are ss. each, and a huge
attendance is inevitable at the price, considering
that all the regulation dais deities have also pro-
mised to attend. Lady Innes is president, and
Mesdames Tauveron and M. O’Brien divide the
onerous duties of hon. secs, to what I hope will be
a most successful ball.
Her many friends will be glad to hear that Miss
Maude Roberts —one of Sydney’s prettiest girls—
who met with a very bad riding accident the other
day, is now out of all danger and nearly con-
valescent. It is a good thing that, although she
was dragged a quarter-of-a-mile, Miss Roberts
did not sustain any facial injury whatever.
Australians who meditate a trip to London will
be glad to hear that Mr. Frank Blackley, once a
well-known Sydney stockbroker, has resumed the
management of the Inns of Court Hotel, Lincoln’s
Inn Fields. Mr. Blackley writes to say that his
aim will be to make the house “a rallying spot
for his Australian friends,” and he has the smiling
countenance of ex-Govs. Jersey, Hopetoun,andPre-
datoros Robinson, who have all written the most
suitable-for-advertising remarks that the heart of
an hotel-manager could desire. Though they do
say that generally the last thing an Australian
abroad longs to meet is another Australian.
Talking of London, Miss Elsa May, the Aus-
tralian warbler, has fallen on her feet in the Big
Smoke apparently having signed a twelve
months engagement as prima donna with W. J.
Turner’s opera co. touring throughout England.
A press-notice describes her as “a grand vocalist
and an acquisition to the co.” In a private letter
received from Camilla Urso, also in London at
time of writing, the gifted violinist expressed her
intention of shortly paying another visit to Aus-
tralia, where the audiences suit her to a T. She
will come vi& South Africa, whither she went on
leaving Melbourne last year. She did not play in
London, where there was “much, too much,
music,” and cold audiences in consequence.
Madame is at present in New York.
I have received from W. H. Paling and Cos. a
copy of Paling’s dance-annual. The cover is a gay
affair, something like the second act of the “ Shop
Girl,” and the contents are equally up to date.
The “ Gaiety” lancers, introducing the latest airs,
are the best point in the annual, which contains
fifteen easy items of an eminently danceable
nature.
From Riooordi and Sons, London, come several
songs of a highly-wrought nature. “ Ask Me
Not,” words by Binnie, and music by G. A. Sala,
begs the beloved object to “Blast me not like a
withered tree,” which sounds as if the darling was
in the habit of using cuss-words, while G.A.S.’s
music to these wild pleadings is about up to a
Xmas-supplement song for the ghost of Sala’s
deadjournal. “Love is Lief,” by Tito Mattei,
is another string of incoherent eroticisms
set to rather obscure music. “Heart to
Heart, E. Bonetti (no thin warbler would
ever sing the music of a man with a name
like that), is also warm off the hob of passion, but
with some melody in the music; and a serenade,
“Dream thou of me,” by Colson Taylor, has the
fevered sentiment decently reined in, and the
music goes smoothly. Two pieces for violin and
piano, “Andante Melancolique,” by Simonetti,
and a Berceuse by William Henley, finish the
“ Riccordi.”
Apropos of music, they were agitating in Syd-
ney recently for band performances on Sunday
afternoon in the Domain, but Major-General
Hutton (an adept at “lending” military bands
for charitable purposes—balls, and so on—gratis,
much to the chagrin of the public bands which
pom-pom dance-music for a living) vetoed the
idea in a way that looked as if the M.G. had
shares in the big drum of the Salvation Army
which has the middle of the road to itself on Sun-
days. He “could not recommend that the band
should devote their Sunday to providing a musical
boon for the population of Sydney,” and “ it was
undesirable on all grounds that soldiers should be
called upon to infringe upon their Sunday for any
duty that could be avoided.”
Certainly, while the band has so many privat®
engagements at which to blow its soul out fo r
mere “ rations ” —a ham-sandwich or so at half-
time—it might very well keep its wind to
itself on Sundays. But, perhaps, if the artistic
Major-General wasn’t quite so prodigally lavish
with military music on society occasions, these
nicely-trimmed dispensers of exhilarating melodies
would have a little time to give to entertaining
their staunch admirers, the general public. For
Sydney is a place directly in need of plenty of
band-music —which the women should go to listen
to in white gowns with red umbrellas.
A brand-new way to recover old debts was hit
upon the other day by a Queensland matron who
had lent a goodish sum of money to a church.
Naturally enough, the management of the sacred
pile made no sort of effort to repay the filthy
lucre, and as the time fixed upon for paying the
debt went past unnoticed, the lady determined to
take matters into her own hands. First, she had
a long and mysterious interview with her sainted
debtors—and what then transpired will never be
known ; but the upshot was that she shortly after-
wards engineered a grand fancy bazaar “in aid of
the church.” And when the thing had been
brought to a successful-issue Madame pocketed
the proceeds herself, apd got her money back in
that way.
I have received from David Jones and Cos. a
copy of their manual of Spring and Summer
fashions, which suggest the Rand wick lawn on
Derby Day seen in a vision—all except the even-
ing dresses, of course, which, by the way, are not
so screamingly decollete as of yore, thank good-
ness. If they catch on, a modest man may hope
to spend an evening at the theatre on a fashion-
able night without blushing. When you come to
think how sylphlike women are in general, the
moderate scoop observable in D. Jones and Co.’s
summer evening gowns should have a warm wel-
come from the raw-bone brigade. J. L. Hordern
and Cos., Pitt-street, are also disseminating an
attractive fashion-plate, which exhibits larger
sleeves and wider skirts than are obtainable any-
where else.
Drapery reminds me, in a way, that a little
Victorian mining township was recently greatly
exercised in the upper-circles over the momentous
question, “Shall tradesmen’s daughters be ad-
mitted to our dances?” After long and serious
argument, a compromise was effected on the basis
that only drapers’ daughters should be tolerated —
a conclusion very easily arrived at, I should think,
considering how an Australian d’s and. is going to
marry an aide-de-camp, and how another d’s and.
is the pet of at least two Gov’mentouses. But, all
the same, this unanimity on the subject of drapery
belles looks awfully as if the elite of that
mining township hadn’t all cashed up for their
Spring bonnets.

cousin of ex-Gov. Oarington— to the Governor-
ship of Westralia, doesn’t seem to give anybody

colonel much unalloyed joy, either, though he will

family yawn. Perth is not a popular appoint-
ment; and the moral certainty that her Ex.
will bolt after six months makes Westralian
society take bub a listless interest in the gyrations
of a personage whom the Fates have decided
shall be but a fleeting show. On dit that one-half
the reason why Westralian Governors’ wives flee
the place, at short notice, is that society is so
inured to being run by a grass-widower that it
hangs round the Gov. from sheer force of habit,
and pays the lady most perfunctory attention.

Writes my Melbourne correspondent:—
“ Entire devotion to the sailor-hat is feminine
Melbourne’s chief sin sartorially. This shape of
hat, which is only becoming to young fresh faces,
is worn by women of all ages and on all occasions,
and an otherwise smart costume is often entirely
spoilt by it. I beheld no less than three at a
toney wedding last week : one topped a Parisian
confection of glad silk and lace ; another crowned
a face, fair and forty at least, garnished with
long horse-teeth—the face, nob the hat, I
mean ; the other really was suitable to a
pretty, flushed-looking seventeen-year maiden
in pink muslin. But to see the S.H. in
all its glory one should go to a Salon afternoon
tea. When viewed from the door the room has
the appearance of a field of small mushrooms,
black and white by reason of the omnipresent
* sailor.’

family for two guineas, for the privilege of over-
looking the arriving Brasseys as they go past.
Quite a windfall for the shop-owner, who probably

ful of letters asking them to be presidents, patrons,

when she played the same parts in Melb. and
Sydney. By the way, pretty Grace Noble has

Lord Willy, she really looks like a saucy little
boy.”

People from such remote suburbs as
Bourke, Wiloannia or Broken Hill, who may
happen to be detained in Sydney overnight, will
find it to their advantage to stay at one of Ellis’s
Coffee Palaces—at Nos. 46 to 52 King-st., and The
Crescent, 631 George-st. City folks are also in-
vited to remember that the King-st. dining-saloon
is open all night as well as all day.*
The friends of one William John George
Carvell are advertising for information as
to his whereabouts. Carvell left Warwickshire,
England, for Queensland, about 15 years ago. He
is supposed to have once kept the North Queens-
land Hotel, George-street, Sydney, and to have
been interested in some North Queensland gold-
mines. Anyone with news of the missing man is
referred to the advertisement in this issue.*
Visitors to the Spouting Spring at Heli-
don, 70 miles from Brisbane, are always astounded
at the quantity of water they can consume at the
Spring. Ten or 12 tumblers in an hour or so are
taken with comfort. The reason is that the
tissues of the body are absorbing the water all the
time. Something beneficial is always added to
the system by every glass of Helidon Spa water
consumed. Messrs. Tooth and Co., Kent
Brewery, are the Sydney agents.*
Henry Lawson’s book, which may be
called “Block and Backblooks : Australian Verses
by Henry Lawson,” or something of that sort, will
be out shortly and will, like “Banjo’s” (orders
for which are rapidly flowing in), be a handsome
volume printed in new type on fine paper. The
price will be ss, 6d., post free from this office, and
The Bulletin, after seeing the material of which
the book is composed, has no hesitation in saying
that it deserves to be, and will be, an immediate
and permanent success.
Same Story Everywhere.
In Sydney, as in every other town and city in
the world, when Warner’s Safe Cure was first
introduced people shook their heads and would
hardly believe when they heard of the cures from
long-standing biliousness, jaundice, indigestion,
or kidney disease effected through its use. Now
the army of believers is daily increasing, and you
can hardly turn without meeting one who owes
life and health to the virtue of this remedy. Do
we believe them? How can we help doing so?
Here they are, and thousands of letters like the
following are enough to make the strongest sceptic
hesitate before crying down something he has not
tried. Read this letter from Miss Maude Whyte,
Forest Lodge, Sydney. Do you think for one
moment that this conscientious lady would mis-
represent facts so important to the general public?
Then weigh carefully her experience and let it
guide you. She writes ; “In some things it takes
a deal of persuasion, and even demonstration, to
secure my faith, and one of these was Warner’s
Safe Cure. T had heard about it from friends and
acquaintances quite a number of times, but could
never be induced to try it, supposing it to be what
is commonly called a ‘quack medicine,’ until,
when exceedingly ill from an attack due to in-
activity of the liver, a lady friend put a bottle into
my hand and said, ‘ There, try that.’ I did try it
and with the most surprising results ; for even
after the first dose the heavy, languid feeling I
had experienced began to disappear. I was soon
able to get about, being quite free from all liver
weakness. After such a happy experience lam
only too glad to testify to the efficacy of this
medicine, and I would further recommend ladies
suffering as I was to take a few of Warner’s Safe
Pills, which greatly aid the efficacy of Warner’s
Safe Cure.”*

S. M. Herald now takes up the parable that
N.S.W. Govt, should be able to induce the M.L.
■Govt, to abandon the duty on “green fruit,”
because N.S.W. is removing her barriers to inter-
provincial exchange. Mauriland’s most natural
reply must be—“ That’s no advantage to us.
You’re removing your barriers to all the world
alike; how does that specially favor us? Until
you can reciprocate with some concession, on goes
our duty against you.” Freetrade and reciprocity
are unblendable.
Westralian Govt., the most “loyal” in
the group, is rewarded by the present of an army
colonel named Smith as Governor. Plainly, if
titles are wanted for local toadies to grease,
“loyalty” doesn’t pay. The more rebellious a
province, the greater its prestige at Downing-
sfcreet.

brigade.

S.A. Education Dept, prints all its own
reading-books, which are monthly serials compiled
in the department. They thus contain much local
matter, are up-to-date, fresh and interesting to
the pupils. Also, they bring in to the Govt, a
profit of J 61500 a year. Vic. Govt, thinks of doing
similarly.
Nearly every S.A. legal M.P. has trotted
out Law Reform, but Kingston “overlays ” them
all with a measure providing that anyone who
passes reasonable examinations may practise law.
Good. “I am going,”'he says, “to introduce a
measure of law-reform which will sever the friend-
ships of years.”
During the last three years the retrench-
ments made in N.S. Wales civil service total
.£1,300,000. Premier Reid now proposes a further
retrenchment of ,£200,000. Inference—for over
a decade N.S.W. has been paying away a million
and a half yearly in salaries to persons whose
work was not wanted.
The electoral tocsin is beginning to ring in
the land of Boodlewraith. “ Dick ” Newton, the
sporting auctioneer, is after the Barcoo, at present
held by Labor-member Kerr, and the hyphenated
Curtain-Minns will run for the Flinders, another
Labor electorate. Minns is the advance-agent
and lecturer for the Bulcoeky Political League,
fathered by Premier Nelson.
Australia still continues to be smeared by
the Dirty Colors of Asia. How is the smear-
ing to be stopped ? Procure a white man in the
advanced stages of leprosy. Take him on tour
through the country. Exhibit him in all the
centres of population as an object-lesson. This
will raise “such a sudden flood of mutiny ” that
the Dirty Colors will be wiped out for ever.

Reform,” but only when the proposed reduction

scientious Tory freetrader is M. S. Down with
the Tariff, but don’t be too rough on good old
Capital. Like the fair Desdemotia, he doth per-

Premier Reid’s taunt that the pugnacious
Sohey is not “ a Labor representative who has the
manliness to join a league with his own fellow
laborers,” is signal evidence of a runaway tongue.
Has not Reid, oftener than he has hairs on his
head, declared that the taking of the Solidarity
Labor pledge proved political cowardice instead
of manliness ? And will he not, as soon as he and
the Labor-party come to the parting of the ways,
repeat the contemptuous utterance ? Manliness,
according to the Premier, evidently means stick-
ing to the Government.
William Robert Campbell, M.L.C., the
Reidite of the N.SAV. Council, who posed re-
cently as the red Radical of that Chamber —the
“ fly in amber ” ! —has treated the public to some
of his own ideas of legislative reform. He wants
to see in the Council—Reid’s future Council —the
Admiral, the Commandant, the Bishops, the Car-
dinal, the Protestant “Popes,” and “meneminent
in literature.” “The Banjo,” M.L.C. ! And
why not also the Judges, the Sheriff, the eminent
Bum-Bailiffs, the Public Executioner, and the
Inspector of Stray Dogs ?
A Victorian ex-M. L.A. frankly admitted
to the Vic. Railway Board that he “ had assisted
on several occasions to keep men in the depart-
ment who, by getting drunk while in charge of
trains and by general neglect of duty, deserved
dismissal.-” Tnese drunks were, his own con-
stituents, and he did it, admittedly, for the sake
of their votes. So did almost every other M.L. A.,
and none of them apparently was ashamed of it.
Even Deakin once read, in open court, a letter
from himself to Speight, in which he urged the
railway commissioner to do something or other
which he shouldn't have done for Deakin’s con-
stituency, and presented it as evidence of the
manner in which Spefght was “influenced” by
politicians.
Outside adherents of the N.S.W. Govern-
ment free-port policy are strenuously endeavoring
to resuscitate the old Freetrade and Liberal Asso-
ciation —but encounter the money-getting diffi-
culty. Pulsford’s organisation had some strong
men at its back—instance Burdekin, who equipped
the body with offioes gratis. This support is no
longer forthcoming. Reid forfeited it witli his
Land and Income Tax proposals, and, now that
the pillars and props are gone, there is little hope
for a successful resurrection. Freetraders in
Parliament disfavor the project, recognising that
active branches in their constituencies might dis-
cover fitter persons than themselves—an easy
business in many cases—and their attitude is like
that of one staunch Reidite who refused to con-
tribute to the Foreign-trade council’s expenses at
the election, and told Varney Parkes and co. that
he’d see them in the Devil’s Pocket constituency
before he’d spend a halfpenny on electioneering
outside his own “ beat.”

The Vic. Assembly, during the recent
tariff debate, put a certain variety of Carriage-
bolts on the free-list because it was assured by
some Freetrade members that they weren’t made
ip the province. Also, it put brushes on thefree-
bst because they were only manufactured locally
011 a ver y small scale. It afterwards transpired
that the local brush-making industry was rattier a
big affair, and that_ the aforesaid bolts had been
manufactured in Victoria for 14 years past. An
Assembly which flounders along in such pitiable
ignorance of the conditions of the country for
which it is legislating is a fine example of bow the
world is governed.

The first-class carriages upholstered in green leather
are comfortable to travel in. They are built on the
separate compartment plan, but in many of them (chiefly

up to the roof, and the conversation of persons in one
compartment can be easily overheard in £he next. Steps
should be taken to remedy this defect.

The Giants’ Fight and the After-Battle:
a Glimpse of “the Noble Art.”
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
It was a 24ft. ring, whose upper ropes were bound
with red cloth. At first, these red ropes had no
meaning ; but later, they acquired a terrible signi-
ficance—later, when they marked the bounds of
the arena of blood, and strained and groaned under
the weight of the slaughtered Plaoke.
# # *
In one corner sat Barker, the Queensland giant,
with Dooley, Dunn and Seymour, all famous
pugs, standing behind him; and opposite, seconded
by Tom Taylor, Thompson and Floyd, sat Placke,
the great, handsome, wholesome-looking, honest-
faced, clear-skinned Dutchman. The groups had
only two unscientific men—the two principals.
A Sydney civic official and two turfmen were
timekeepers; Mr. Colonel Macdonald, as the
blundering ring-steward put it, was referee. And
the crowd which had come to the bloody feast was
addressed as “gentlemen,” and politely requested
to keep silence when the referee should count the
seconds over a fallen gladiator.
• # «
“Time!” and the giants shaped, while the
breathless crowd noted that Plaoke, Hercules as he
was, with his 6ft. 5£in. and 16st. 71b,, magnificent
proportions, and limbs which carried him 18 miles
in bitterly-cold open-ocean waters against the rage
of a North Sea tempest, nevertheless looked small
before the towering 6ft. lOin. Barker. Never
before in the history of pugilism had such a pair
met; never before had one-fifth of a ton of live
meat been called upon by soienced-seconds to glut
a well - moneyed crowd with slow, unscientific
butchery.
* * *
The Queensland giant placed his Heaven-born
policeman’s foot well forward in defiance, and
behind it waved the ponderous fists through which
Placke had to break in order to strike face or
body. Placke began the fighting, trying to reach
Barker’s face, and Barker’s seconds chuckled as
they saw how totally unscientific was the simple-
minded foe, and laughed boisterously when their
principal’s right struck Placke upon the jaw, send-
ing him down. That blow would have settled
almost any other man, but Placke’s great heart
and unabused constitution gave him fresh lease of
life. The second round was likewise Barker’s—
he smashed Placke on the left eye, nose and
mouth, causing the blood to flow; and the fight
seemed all over—the reporters put away their
note-books. But, with the third round, Placke
came out fresh again, and, beaten man as he was,
he forced the fighting, hurling himself upon
Barker, smashing him over the right eye, and
making the blood spurt from mouth and nose.
There was consternation in the Barker camp;
the seconds’ jubilation changed to dismay and
excitement. It wasn’t all over; the beaten man’s
pluck and strength were undying. Dooley clenched
his hands at Placke’s onslaught, and shouted,
“Left, Dick! Left! Left!’’ and at last Dick
shot the left with a staggering blow, which almost
sent Placke down, and made the bright blood
stream down his face from a deep cut under the
eye. Dooley and co. “kidded’’ Barker as they
rubbed him down. “ But moind what I tell you,”
said Dooley, “keep away.”
# # #
In the fourth round, Placke made Barker
“smell hell;” he punched him hard upon the belly
and made him groggy. Placke’s rushing was
cowing Barker, whose seconds, in the minute
interval, “kidded” their man vigorously, while
Placke’s instructed him to rush again and infight
Barker during the fifth round. Had Placke
obeyed his seconds he might have won; but he
allowed Barker to pull himself together in the
fifth round, and that and the sixth were mostly
feinting and glaring. With the seventh round the
fierceness of the battle recommenced, and Placke
reeled back upon the ropes with the blood gushing
from eye, nose, and mouth and falling upon his
breast, down which it streamed in many rivulets.
And from that on the rounds were rounds of
blood. As the exhausted men sparred for
breath, and the audience sat mute, many
of them paralysed with the horror of the
spectacle, Placke’s blood could be heard drip,
drip, drip upon the stage, and Barker
would suddenly spring back and spit from his
throat the blood which threatened to choke him.
Placke often drank his —Barker’s manoeuvre was
too risky in his exhausted state. The gloves were
red and chitted ; the stage was slippery. “Resin ! ”
cried one of Barker’s seconds, “ resin—Dick can’t
stand,” and in the minute interval one second
rubbed resin upon Barker’s while Abe
Willis sucked the giant’s eye, which was fast
closing up.
* # #
After every round the seconds sponged their
men on face and body,'fanned them, “kidded”
them, and sent them up again. But Placke was,
practically, dying. His left eye was closed, and
the left side of his face was a great blot of blood,
which, when the men clinched, left a big red
patch upon Barker’s chest. And in these clinches
the men’s bloody arms and gloves, passing round
each other’s bodies, left their trunks almost
covered with smudges of gore. The beaten Placke
was knocked down again and again, but he rose
every time to present his poor, hideously-battered
face to the onslaught of the foe. There was not
a man among the officials there to raise his voice
against this butchery. No police interfered. But
at last the referee stopped the fight by hailing
Barker as the victor. Placke was lifted off the
ropes and borne to his corner.
# # #
Then, in his dressing-room, surrounded by his
seconds, by Barker’s seconds, and the chief M.C.,
Placke, unconscious, began a long and grim
struggle with Death. This was the after-battle in
which the poor, unscienced Dutchman took an
unconscious part, but in which all the experienced
abettors fought desperately against outraged
Nature and avenging Law. Their hearts turned
cold, cold as the body of Placke as it lay there
upon a mattress. They tried to put whisky down
his throat; they held him up and gave him a
teaspoonful, and he threw up over a half-pint of
bloorL Then he swooned off. Two of the party
began to tremble. “ He’s gone! ” said one.
This roused a master - spirit that was there.
“Never mind,” said he, “you’ll only get
seven years like the rest of us. Don’t stand
trembling! If he dies, you and will get
weak enough together on seven years of bran-
mash and hominy. Rub him with whisky.” And
the master-spirit poured the whisky upon Placke’s
body, and the seconds rubbed and chafed it. One
man, in the near presence of an awful calamity,
alone kept his nerve. As Death came closer he
turned him aside with a bold thrust. Seeing a
sickly-looking man stoop down and rub Placke's
feet, he shouted with ironical emphasis—“ Don’t
you rub his feet, you will give him typhoid fever !
You get away and get hurled. Only a strong man
should rub him.” The sickly-looking man left.
And the rubbing went on, while the bold one
explained why he wasn’t a teetotaller. “An old
lady,” he said, “whom I loved was pronounced
dead by two doctors. We rubbed her with
whisky and she lived seven years after.” Placke
showed no signs of reviving ; his breathing was
stertorous. “I have thrown a boot at a man for
snoring half as loud as that,” said the nerveful
gentleman.
* ♦ ♦ ♦
There was a hush when the doctor came. He
put his stethoscope to the man’s heart, he felt his
pulse, he opened the one eye which it was possible
to open, and stooping down and holding a lighted
match over it, examined the pupil. None of the
seconds dared speak. The doctor, it was plain,
was most unfavorably impressed ; but the man of
nerve spoke up without a quail in his heart, with-
out a quaver in his voice. “ You see, doctor,
there’s the wrist-hitter, the elbow-hitter, and the
shoulder-hitter. Now, the man this man fought
is a wrist-hitter.” “What gave him that eye?”
asked the doctor. “ Wrist hitting,” said the iron
one. “Now, what do you think of it, doctor?”
The doctor looked down at Placke, whose face
might have been struck by a half-spent round-
shot. “ There’s great shock to the system ; the
man is very weak ; his heart very slow ; the bone
of the left eyebrow is fractured ; and there is con-
cussion of the brain.” That startled the stony
one just the least bit. “Concussion of the brain ! ”
THE BATTLE OF THE GIANTS.
HARRY PLACKE, the Dutchman
(6ft. 5[?]in.).
DICK BARKER, the Queensland Giant
(6ft. 10in.). [Kerry, Photo.
he exclaimed, with loud incredulity. “ Why, he
hasn’t any brain ! Besides, he must have got it
before he came in. ”
# * •
“ How old is he ?” asked the doctor. “ Twenty-
five, ” said someone. “It would be a great pity if
anything happened to such a splendid man, the
doctor continued, with the admiration of the pro-
fessional anatomist, “ but I can t say whether he 11
come round yet or not. He might come round.”
The tough one stepped aside. “The doctor
don’t kid me,” he said. “ He’ll come
round, with his constitution, all right ! If
he’d won the fight, he’d be all right now,
but his heart’s broken with being beaten.
Why, I remember when the Nigger fought
Dooley ! Why, the Nigger hit him on the point
with the force of the lightning, and it lifted
Dooley clean off his feet, and knocked the bone
out near the eye in a lump as big as a goose-egg.
And didn’t we squirm for three hours after ; we
were frightened, but Dooley came round all right.’
• * •
Here the doctor spoke. “You have seen a
man pull through after being as bad as this?
“Lord, yes, doctor —why I had both my eye-
brows fraetured, and look at me! A man’s as old
as he feels, and I’m 25. I’d take Placke’s chance
fora tenner; don’t you remember how Trickett
went like this after his race with Hanlan ! Insen-
sible for four hours, and then springing up as
lively as a buck wallaby.”
* # •
“What gave him this terrible eye? ” asked the
doctor, as he applied the ice-cap to Placke’s head.
“Well,” said the other, “you see, he met a
policeman outside, and told him he wasn’t hand-
some, and the policeman hit Him with the baton.”
The doctor wanted Placke to be taken to the
hospital, but was temporarily overruled : “He
can only get a good doctor and best attention
there, and he can have those here.” About mid-
night the doctor went away, leaving word* to call
him should Placke seem worse.
There was silence, broken by one of the seconds
striking a match in order to look into Placke’s
eye. The Nerveful One was up. “ What are you
striking a match for? Keep your claw out of his
eye ; bad luck’s sure to come if you have your claw
in it. Your hair won’t want cropping for your
seven years. You’re bald now.” The second blew
out the match. “ Weak ? ” ironically exclaimed
Mr. Orit. “ Weak ? the doctor says—he —is—
WEAK ! He’s strong enough to pull the tusks
out of an elephant. And Game ! he’d fight a cir-
cular-saw driven by the thunder ! ”
“Yes,” said one of the seconds, “they can’t
say this Hollander isn’t game; he’s fighting now”
—and, lifting up the blanket, he showed Placke’s
hands tightly clenched and moving automatically
as if in battle.
“That’s a good symptom,” remarked one
second. “ Of course it is ,” said Mr. Grit; “it’s
the mind contro.lin’ the body. Take that look
off your face,” said Mr. Grit to one second.
“ You’ll turn the man into a seven years’ corpse.”
# » *
They took the poor Dutchman to the hospital
some hours later, and when this went to the
printer he was—after four days—still fighting his
battle with Death.

question.
