is the perennial F. L. S. Merewether, ex-clerk
of the Executive Council, who, since 6th Jan.,
’56, has annually drawn £9OO. Forty years on
Jan. 6 next, at £9OO a year, is just £36,000.
Another N.S.W. judicial pension (£lB2O
annually) lias fallen in through the death, at Turn
bridge Wells, of that curious old gentleman, ex-
Justice Deffell. Ex-Justice Foster now stands
alone as a Supreme Court pensioner, but it seems
certain that there will be other retirements ere
long.
The greatest compliment one can pay an
Irishman (although his accent may be a pro-
nounced Kerry) is to mistake him for an English-
man. Even Michael Davitt is reported to have
said: “A good many people are surprised I
haven’t a brogue.”
A thrill of horror ran through Belgravia,
the month before last, when a certain gilded
colonist in London, replying to a letter signed
“Ripon,” committed the shocking blunder of
addressing the Secretary of State for the Colonies
as “Mr. Ripon, Downing-street.”
Canny “ Dauvit ” Mitchell, Melba’s pa,
has opened his raild-cure-bacon works at Cave
Hill, Vic. After 51 pigs were killed, dressed and
sent to the freezing chamber in three hours, the
company charnpagned over the dead porkers.
“ Dauvit ” anticipates doing 500 pigs per day.
Melb. solicitor Sincock, gaoled for refusing
to have his child vaccinated, writes through the
Herald a farewell to his friends, in which he
quotes the stanza from Lovelace beginning,
“ Stone walls do not a prison make.” Who would
have thought that there was a poetic element in
calf-lymph ?
“It is, after all, an advantage in the
House of Commons,’’ writes a London correspon-
dent to The Bulletin, “ to have once kept a pub.
Bailey, of Bailey’s Hotel, and Tom Curran, late
of the Oxford, Sydney, have both been elected
members of the Kitchen Committee, a position of
some importance.”
Bishop Goe, visiting Lockwood, Vic ,
swallowed a cup of coffee hurriedly at a tea-fight,
to find later on that he became suddenly indis-
posed. Some dirty person had made the coffee with
water out of an abandoned tank, where slime and
dead flies and the late lamented cat had been
accumulating for years.
Mark Twain’s opinion of Louis Becke :
“‘By Reef and Palm’ is one of the best books
that has been produced in recent years. Becke is
a man who knows the South Seas thoroughly, and
has the true feeling for life in these islands. He
writes finely, and I think his name is bound to be
heard of prominently in literature.”
The Governor-elect of Westralia, Colonel
Gerard Smith, is a cousin of Earl Carington.
Which is no particular recommendation, consider-
ing that Gov. Carington aforesaid is in Australia
chiefly known as the person who hanged four boys
who were tried till 4 o’clock in the morning.
The Brookmans, the biggest holders of
Great Boulder shares, are both Adelaide men.
One of them was a partner in a retail grocery, and
the other was a not over-successful jam-manu-
facturer before going to the West. Between them
they hold nearly 50,000 shares —say .£350,000.
A potentate who has been “doing”
Westralia for a London illustrated paper, started
for Southern Cross dressed like a war-correspon-
dent, with a revolver ostentatiously displayed in
his belt and a big sheath-knife located under each
coat-tail. Also, he took with him a private-secre-
tary and a mountain of luggage, including a China
tea-service.
Hori Grey himself relates that George
Meredith, the well-known novelist, to whom the MS.
of Rees’ “ Life and Times of Sir George Grey ” was
submitted by Chapman and Hall for judgment,
returned it with a memo, thus: “I don’t think
there would be any demand for this book if
printed, Sir George Grey being a comparatively
unknown man.”
“‘Mark Twain’ is my trademark,” said
the Innocent Abroad to a Melb. Herald reporter.
“I see,” said the simpering scribe, “your twain
mark, as it were.” Then this amiable zoological
curiosity, who had travelled down from Albury
with the “Murkan,” blandly adds that “Mr.
Clemens seemed completely worn out when he
stepped off the train at Melbourne.”
When G. Republican Dibbs reached
Townsville, the other day, they put him into an
ordinary six-foot half-tester bed, the consequence
being that 4in. of Dibbs stuck out into space. In
the morning the long man made one or two
prayerful remarks and shook the dust off his feet.
On the Towers, however, they were equal to the
emergency, and a special bed was rigged for
Damchicago.
The Duke of York, heir to the British
Crown, who cadged complete collections of stamps
(worth hundreds of pounds in the open market)
from N.S.W. and other Australasian Govts., has,
it is cabled, sold the whole of his philatelical kit
to one of the Rothschilds for £56,000. This is
royalty’s latest device for turning a dishonest
penny—for neither N.S.W. nor any other pro-
vince would provide free stamps for Baron Ferdi-
nand de Rothschild. But such things are in the
blood. Didn’t Alfred Edinburgh sell all his Aus-
tralian presents by auction?
J. G. Ward, the “Pooh-Bah” of Maori-
land, is a self-made man. As a youth, he
was a storeman in the employ of Nioholl and
Tucker, at Invercargill. Having saved money, he
set up a business of his own, which, in course of
time, developed into the Farmers’ Co-operative
Association, for which he is said to have raised a
capital of .£50,000 while in London. His straight-
forward and business-like dealings won the con-
fidence of the farmers, who put him into Parlia-
ment. Mrs. Ward is the daughter of a successful
trader. When presented at the Queen’s drawing-
room, she could give points in appearance to most
of the aristocratic debutantes. Ward has a mar-
vellous grasp of figures, and is free from “side.”
Hori Grey, since his return to England,
has been besieged by all sorts of cranks and fad-
dists. A musical genius wrote a plausible letter,
full of high-flown compliments, inviting the old
man to collaborate with him in composing a Maori
opera. He was enthusiastic over the project, and
confident that there would be millions in it.
Grey’s private secretary was instructed to arrange
an interview, but when the author of the brilliant
idea presented himself at No. 7 Park Place, he
turned out to be a seedy-looking individual with
a purple proboscis and a general appearance of
chronic dissipation. The negotiations abruptly fell
through when he asked the old man to put up a
trifle of £5O for preliminary expenses.

PREMIUMS MODERATE.
Claims paid immediately on proof of death and title.

remunerative investment.
The Insurance Newspaper (London, June, 1895) says of this Society “It is popular, progressive, under able and prudent control, and certainly no office in or

Query—whether the production of spirits
at a seance isn’t a violation of the Excise-law ?
Answer—No ; because the so-called spirits are too
far below proof.
A writer in Sheffield Weekly Telegraph
makes his hero visit “the faro hells in Bourke-
street, Melbourne,” and speaks of a miirder
committed in 1885 “in the desolate bush at Flem-
ington, Victoria.”
The stuffed “ Tantanoola wolf ” is now in
Adelaide Zoo. Hitherto the owners have resisted
all attempts to photo, the beast, which the taxi-
dermist has tried to make look as bulky and tiger-
like as possible. It is a true Asiatic wolf and
quite a handsome animal.
That favorite food, Parsons’ Flaked Oat-
meal, steadily grows in public favor. It makes
bone and muscle and endurance, therefore it is
especially suited to the young man who goes on
the land, to wrestle with the stupendous gum-tree
whose bottom roots are down along with Cain and
Ananias in perdition, and with the kangaroos and
the mortgage and the drought. Also, it is the
only food that is equal to the case of the prospector,
who picks his way through rooks as tough as the
devil’s coat of mail, only to land on wash that
yields one speck to a mountain ; and it is a restora-
tive for the jaw-weary politician and insurance-
fiend, and for the military person whose feet are
tired with retreating at the Easter sham-fight, and
other exhausted citizens in every walk of life.
Every person who wants to live to be 116, and be
an oldest inhabitant, and have his portrait pub-
lished in the papers when he dies, should use it.*

The Coming Human Race.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“I can see it,” said the cynic, as he raised his
weary face ;
“ There will nothing be bub poets in the coming
human race.
From their infancy to manhood, and the highest
to the low,
All will put their thoughts in metre and in rhyme
will tell their woe.
“ Bub I hope that God will call me from this earth
before the time
When the babies in their cradles yell for Ma in
noisy rhyme ;
And the schoolboys ‘ playing marbles ’ in the
backyard of their home
Reckon up their stony winnings in a swinging
little ‘ pome.’
“Grant, O Lord, my bones are resting where no
wattle-blossoms bloom,
And no leery mulga-poets write an ode on every
tomb —
When the time has come; and parsons metrify
their ancient tale,
And they set to jingling verses regulations in the
gaol.
“ Life on earth will be a torture hardly equalised
in hell
When the fishwife gets poetic and the bookie
rhymes his yell,
And the Irish foot-policeman sings a ballad in the
court
Of a black-eyed little damsel who indulged in too
much port.
“ Lord have mercy,’ is my prayer while I yet
have breath and life ;
Slay my body, I implore Thee, ere the wretched
days arrive
When the Parliamentary speeches nought but
blankest verses be,
And the trouser-wearing women rhyme their
slander over tea.
“ Should St. Peter not admit me to the great and
holy show,
I shall gladly enter Sheol rather than return
below,
Where the larrikins in stanzas most abominably
curse,
And the highly-learned Judges sentence you to
death in verse !”
Bourke.
6 x 8.

The Sydney Fine-Art Exhibitions.
Whether or not the long-impending division in
the ranks of her artists, which has led to N.S.W.
possessing two Societies instead of one, has put
the painters on their mettle during the year, it is
certain that the two Exhibitions just opened dis-
play a distinct advance. The elder society,
located in its old rooms, now considerably en-
larged, displays 343 exhibits, of which 163 are oils,
and 144 water-color drawings ; while the infant
(though remarkably mature) institution hangs on
the tastefully-arranged walls of the York-street
Rink 121 works in oils and 48 in watercolor, with
exhibits of other kinds which bring the total up to
223. The Art Society has indeed many pro-
ductions that could have been advantageously
dispensed with, many whose presence is dis-
tinctly prejudicial to the cause of Art, and
some which, though of more or less promise, will
hardly bear criticism ; but there are still pictures
enough of a character to make their mark in any
exhibition. The .'-ociety of Artists shows little
that could profitably be eliminated, and the
standard of achievement is generally higher
than any heretofore reached by local painters,
though there is no work, perhaps, of .such
general attractiveness as several exhibited at the
Pitt-street rooms.
# # •
The sixteenth annual exhibition of the Art
Society occupies three rooms, of which the old one
is now devoted exclusively to water-color, black
and white, &c. The Vice-President, Mr. W.
Lister Lister, enters, this year, both literally and
figuratively, “ fresh fields and pastures new.”
His most important contribution (why use the
word “bracken” in Australia?), “Through the
Bracken ” (43), with a canvas some 9ft. by 6ft., is,
when seen from a sufficient distance, a perfect
piece of half-wild Nature, with the fresh and
living greens relieved by the silvery reflection of
the sunlight on the fern-fronds, and by the ruddy
browns of last year’s growth. To the weary,
pent-up denizen of the city such a work is a
veritable joy. “a. Fig-tree Grove ” (76) is a large
and splendid study of Australian foliage, with the
sun pleasantly chequering the carpet of dead
leaves; while “Unchangeable, save in the Wild
Waves’ Play ” (69) is as distinguished for the
glorious color of the scarcely swelling water as is
“ The Wild, Wild Sea, that No Man Knows ”
(49) for the frostlike freshness of the sparkling
surf. Mr. Arthur Collingridge is best seen in
“Morning, Sydney Harbor” (7), and in “The
City of the Plains ” (39), with the mist empurpling
the urban dwellings, while a golden effulgence
sheds itself upon the hills. “ Ploughing in
Bathurst Plains ” (15) is a creditable production,
and “Locksley, near Bathurst ” (189), a cheerful
country scene in water-colors.
• * *
Seldom has Mr. W. O. Piguenit so thoroughly
excelled himself as in “ The Flood in the
Darling, 1890 ” (25). Here we have a masterful
transcript of Nature under exceptional conditions.
The composition of the picture is unexceptionable;
and the closely studied drawing of the ever-
changing clouds is as admirable as the tender
shimmer on the wide expanse of placid waters.
“A Tarn, Western Highlands of Tasmania” (6)
is a good example of the favorite painter’s best-
known style of work, with clouds of mist boiling
around the half-illumined craggy peaks, and cattle
browsing on the margin of the unruffled lakelet.
Mr. George Collingridge is more than usually to
the fore in his habitual style of work, the most
prominent example of which is “ Mangrove Creek,
Hawkesbury River” (90), a view painted with
excessive conscientiousness; while of Mr. C. H.
Hunt’s varied contributions the happiest, per-
haps, is “Morning on the Murrumbidgee ” (245),
a spacious stretch of lofty hill and meadow.
# # *
Figure subjects are not as yet a strong feature
of Australian Art, but Mr. Gordon Coutts makes
an exceptionally noteworthy departure in this
direction. In “ Too Late ” (42) —a priest present-
ing the crucifix to a dying youth as he lies upon
his pallet in a humble cottage—we have the best
example of this branch of painting yet displayed
in a Sydney exhibition. While the treatment, as
a whole, is more than satisfactory, portions of it
are extremely fine—such as, for instance, the very
skilful handling of the ministering cleric’s head.
The intensity of exalted expression shown in the
glowing features of the Church’s servant is in
touching contrast with the pallid aspect of the
face from which are fading the last gleams of
consciousness ; while the tender pathos with which
the simple story is told, is heightened by a mastery
of drawing and a subdued power of brushwork
which make the picture, perhaps, the painting of
the year. A more homely subject, but treated
quite as effectively in its way, is “ Washing Day”
(23), a typical old woman at the stove-boiler in a
typical Australian back-yard, with quite a twang
of the old Dutch school about her. “In a
Thoughtful Mood ” (26), by Mr. A. R. Coffey, a
curly head and shoulders peering, nude, from out
amass of furs, displays some excellent drawing
and good flesh tints. Other works of Mr. Coffey’s
are well deserving of attention, especially the
upper portion of “ Hard Times ” (16), with its
excellence of color. Mr. Albert Hanson appears
—for the first time, we think—as a figure-painter,
with “ Blacksmith ” (269), a powerful interior of a
forge with men at work, and “An Idyll” (249),
depicting mermaids in a cave, unpleasantly im-
aginative both in coloring and conception. Mr.
Hanson (whose work this year is entirely in water-
color) contributes, likewise, a number of little
English country subjects—perfect gems of their
kind —in addition to an exquisitely-painted coast-
scene, full of tender greys and browns, “TheSea-
birds’ Cry—the Music of the Sea ” (216).
# * *
Never has Mrs. Mary Stoddart produced a por-
trait so superb in her own particular manner as
that of Mrs. Roger Gadsden (44), an elderly lady
of refined and aristocratic mien, clad in black lace
and sables. Miss Edith E. Cusack sends a quan-
tity of startlingly-colored work, of which the best
examples are the portrait heads of “ Doreen ” (35),
an interesting young lady with a top-knot (in
oils), and (in pastels) “ Day Dreams ” (263), and
“Miss Weaver ” (275). Other excellent specimens
of women’s work are Miss Florence M. Green’s
“Fraulein M ” (147), very French and very
chic, and two heads of elderly ecclesiastics,
“ Pater Benedictus ” (125), and “ Brother Nicho-
las ” (149). Few better things by Mr. J. A. Ben-
nett have been seen than the colored head in
water-colors of “ Buila, a South Sea Islander”
(208), while his portrait of Mr. J. D. Fitzgerald
(50) is painted with his usual ultra-conscientious
care. Mr. J. Mather maintains his perennial ex-
cellence in water-colors. His “ Wintry Weather ”
(240) suggest almost a David Cox; “ Under the
Wattles” (196) is a finely-studied piece of hill
and foliage; and “Stony Creek” (256), remark-
able for its exquisite “quality,” depicts a barge
moored on the grey water, under an equally grey
sky. Mr. C. A. Tindall’s “ A Westerly, Circular
Quay ”(201), borders somewhat on the ideal, but
is extremely picturesque, and equally good in
composition, drawing, and coloring. The various
phases of bush life are truthfully, typically, and
most artistically illustrated, by Mr. Edward
Bevan, in some half-score of water-colors, all racy
ofjthe camp-fire, the “billy,” the “sundowner,”
and the “swag,” and are sure to find a
numerous circle of admirers. Bare mention is
all that is possible of Mr. Gerald Fitzgerald,
Mr. Harry G. Garlick (whose “Orphaned,” No.
62, is a work of much promise), Mr. J. Swinton
Diston, Miss Jessie Scarvell, Mr. J. H. Young,
Miss Goddard, Mr. G. V. F. Mann, Mrs, Butter-
worth, and others whose work contributes materi-
ally to the interest and value of the Exhibition.
In the same way, general reference only can be
made to the black-and-white and sculpture,
though the exhibits in these branches are far from
being unworthy of notice.
• # •
In the exhibition of the Society of Artists
the interest centres mainly in the oil paint-
ings. The most prominent production of
Mr. Tom Roberts is “ Bailed Up ” (29), in
which we see the mail-coach stopped by a gang
of bushrangers, on a road skirting a steep hill,
sparsely covered with scraggy trees. Thoroughly
Australian in subject, the scene is treated with
somewhat less life and vigor than might have been
expected. “In a Corner on the Maclntyre”
(17) shows careful painting of the tumbled masses
of white rock and their reflection in the water. A
small full-length panel portrait of Mr. L.
Hopkins (“Hop”—No. 8) is not only an
admirable likeness, but a masterful study in
browns ; while “ Maria Yulgilbah ” (31), the head
of a female aboriginal, and “ An Old Banjo ” (41),
a girl in a muslin bodice, seated and twanging the
favorite weapon of the irrepressible nigger, are
full of character and force. Undoubtedly Mr.
Roberts is most at home in portraits of women.
Mr. J. R. Ashton is singularly modest in point of
superficial area, but his work displays increasing
clearness of handling and directness of purpose.
He contributes a number of small pictures (56 and
67, 71 to 76, and 78 to 80) consisting of bits of old
Sydney and country subjects, all pourtrayed with
remarkable felicity. A lovely scene on the
Hawkesbury, and “Old Cottages, Cumberland-
street,” may be specially noted. His “ Study of a
Head ” (S 9), in pastels, is full of quiet power.
• » •
Few Australian artists throw more poetry into
their work than Mr. A. H. Fullwood, and
none, perhaps, has made greater strides
than his pictures show this year. “Aus-
tralia” (32) is quite a pastoral idyl, with its
browsing sheep in sunlit paddock bounded by
hills of misty grey : while “Solitude” (37), an
evening riverscape, with craggy heights in shadow
under a golden sky, and rushes rising from the
steely stream, describes the utterness, but not the
dreariness, of restful solitude. Again, “The New
Moon ” (100) is an exquisite scene of sunset over
pearly water, with the shore artistically
veiled. Not only in these oil-paintings, but also
in his watereolors, “ The Huon Belle ” (177) a
scene distinguished for the broad but careful
work and deft management of the light, and
“Becalmed” (196), with its wealth of delicate
color, Mr. Fullwood is artistically satisfying.
* * «
To many the most attractive picture of the
show will be Mr. Arthur Streeton’s “ Oblivion ”
(26). A female figure, of winsome mien,
and partially draped in silver gauze, is reclin-
ing on a ledge of silvery marble thrown up
by a tenderly - treated background. The com-
position is singularly happy, the coloring deli-
cate and harmonious without being feeble, and
the treatment altogether shows Mr. Streeton in
a most favorable light. His “ Cremorne Pas-
toral ” (24), too, is by no means the least successful
landscape he has painted. A stretch of hillside
for a foreground, a few straggling trees, the
waters of Sydney Harbor, and the distant heights,
are all the elements of the composition, but
the artist has treated them with consummate
skill, as they are seen under the sunless light. A
water-color drawing of “ Adelaide ” (182) is
another artistic piece of work, showing the “ City
of Churches ” in the distance, and a curious effect
of partial storm. It is in water-colors that Mr.
F. P. Mahoney has most successfully asserted him-
self this year, his “Mid-day Spell” (186), a bush
scene with bullocks resting with their load of
giant timber, being excellently handled and
it is distinctively Australian. We can call to
mind nothing better from this favorite artist’s
brush. Of his oil paintings, “The Cry of the
Mothers ” (16) will be the most popular. The
“ mothers ” prove to be mares and cows in dread of
the rising flood. The artist’s excellent drawing
is here as conspicuous as his sympathy with ani-
mal life. He likewise sends some exceedingly fine
portraits of horses, and one of a male biped. The
natural, well-studied and totally unaffected cattle
drawing of Mr. G. Lambert makes it easy to
predict a high position for this young artist.
Specially noteworthy is the painting of the chest-
nut horse, with the bridle entangled round his
hoofs, in “ A Ticklish Moment ” (42).
* * #
Mr. Sidney Long continues to make rapid pro-
gress. His principal work is “Summer” (44), a
landscape with a water-hole, executed principally
with the palette-knife. Though a trifle garish,
perhaps, in tone, it possesses considerable merit, and
is a striking picture. Mr. Hall Thorpe is another
rising artist, whose future should be assured if he
will somewhat curb his ambition for a time. His
rather startling “Fairy Bower, Manly ” (81), has
considerable merit, however, though we prefer the
less pretentious and very artistic little “View of
Bronte” (23). Miss Ethel Stephens is seen to
excellent advantage in her “Portrait of Mrs.
Julius Caro’* (96), handled with rare delicacy and
much force. Mr. J. S. Watkins is a liberal con-
tributor ; his “Harmony in Grey” (7), a head of
a dark-eyed girl, being among his best produc-
tions. Mr. D. G. Reid is most favorably seen
in his somewhat poetic “ Where the Bracken
Rusted ” (90) (more “ bracken ! ”), and Miss Emily
Meston in a most telling portrait (the strongest
portrait, so far as The Bulletin knows, yet
painted in Australia by a woman) of the
Rev, G. MTnnes (25), and an exceptionally
fine “ Study of Grapes ” (54). Miss Emily
L. Paul has a good genre subject, “ On the Shelf ”
87), and Mr. W. E. Chambers sends a portrait of
Mr. James Spencer (101). Mr. W. A. Moir’s
“Boundary Rider” (86) possesses merit, but is
unequal; and Mr. Sidney Cocks shows some very
artistic water-colors. Out of the few examples of
plastic art, the ruggedly powerful bust of an
“ Aboriginal Warrior Chief ” (214), by Mr.
Nelson Illingworth, should be noted ; while among
the “Black and White ” and the architectural
and decorative drawings and designs will be
found several subjects well deserving attention.
SPORTING NOTIONS.
Zimmerman has arrived at Adelaide per Oruba,
and intends slaying there for some months for strict
training. He will then visit Melbourne and Sydney. He
says he has made no engagements, neither will he issue
immediately a challenge, but he is prepared to race any-
body or everybody. He intends to give up cycling pro-
fessionally after his tour here.
* ♦ * ♦
Zimmerman was 26 on June 11. He began his
athletic career in jumping contests ; it was not till ’B9 that
he first figured in cycling. In ’9O he beat W. VV. Windle,
theretofore invincible, and closed ’9l with 52 firsts, 10
seconds, 3 thirds—many world’s records. In ’92’s spring
he made all the English cyclists wish him dead. He
didn’t do well at first, but when started he fairly ran
over them. ’92 closed with 100 races ; 75 firsts. 10 seconds,
5 thirds. In ’93 he scored 101 firsts out of 111 races. In
’95 lie got married, and is now in the business, and while
in all probability he is still boss cyclist, he is boss cycle-
advertiser.
• « • •
Zimmerman says that a cyclist whose best un-
paced mile record is 2.20 can be pulled through in 2min.
with a pace-maker.
• » * *
Zimmerman attributes his ability to “stay”
during the cycling season to his never getting astride his
machine in the winter time : “The men who train all the
winter generally go slale before the fall.” To this prac-
tice Megson, Lewis, and co., of Australia, offer a big con-
trast ; they are always cycling. “Build up the reserve
force,” says Zim.
* * • ♦
Zimmerman says the hardest race is the quar-
ter-mile. It is a struggle from start to finish, and
exhausts him more than a mile or two miles where a man
is gradually worked up to the final sprint.
» ♦ » *
When the season is beginning Zimmerman gets
upon a road-machine and starts with 10 miles a day.
Slowly he gets up to 30, and all the time he lets the
scorcher pass him on the road. He considers hand-
rubbing the most refreshing and valuable part of training.
* * * •
Since ’B7 Zimmerman has won 400 prizes, includ-
ing 10 pianos, 4 horses, 8 carriages, 25 gold watches, [3
building lots, 40 diamonds, 100 medals, and stacks of
silver plate. He says cement tracks are the best for
speed. He is Oft. high, weighs 1701 b., rides a “bike”
weighing only 191 b., eats plenty of meat when training
but bars potatoes and cucumbers, and says racing has
been good for his health.
* * « *
Most of the Melbourne bikers shave clean.
* * * »
Edison’s latest invention is a clockwork bicycle-
propeller, so arranged that, going uphill, it does practi-
cally all the work, whereas going downhill or on level
ground, it winds itself up automatically.
* * * *
Megson, N.S.W. champion cyclist, like Wat-
son, the English mile and five-miles champion, is only 22
years old -four years younger than Zimmerman. All the
same, Zim. is really no older than Megson, and has four
years’ more experience.
* * * *
Why is football on the down-grade in Melb. ?
asks the chuckle-headed barracker. The wonder is that
the game ever got to its dizzy height of, say, five years
ago. Cricket, as an entertainment for the intelligent
public, is kept alive by the exceptional skill of compara-
tively few players. But at football one strong and active
low-browed ruffian is much the same as another. Mel-
bourne alone turns out about 240 kicking stars, half of
that number being classed Hay I. The average man is
tired of hearing of wonderful Billies, Macs, Micks,
Hughies, &0., &c. He has lost interest in the identities
of these heroes, because the strain of trying to spot them
in the field was softening his brain. The decline of Melb.
football dates back to the season when the local Herald
started to publish tbe photos, of about half-a-dozen
“ leading ” players every blessed week. They were too
numerous, and too unprepossessing in appearance to seem
worth any more idolatry.
• * * *
Prior Vaughan has been lamenting, at Charters
Towers, the condition of the English masses. “Thirty
per cent, only of the people of Great Britain attend
church.” And the men of the Towers listen sympathis-
ingly to the Prior's tale of woe and then adjourn to their
regular Sunday afternoon worship on the football ground.
It would take a smart missionary to round up 30 percent,
of the Towers population to a place of worship.
• * • *
After a recent Melb. football-match the winning
team sought refuge in the pavilion, and remained there
until escorted home by a body of police followed by a
howling mob. The noble game!
♦ * ♦ *
Victorian Rugby players (writes a correspon-
dent) had a rough time of it after their match against
N.S.W. Combined. Nearly every man who got a scratch
suffered from blood-poisoning, and as scratches were
numerous, about half the team were more or less dis-
abled ;in fact, some of them are not yet better. Several
of the Sydney men were also affected. It is said the
trouble was caused by certain chemicals in the artificial
manures used in “ top-dressing ” the ground.
♦ ♦ * *
Suggested in England that the 1896 Australian
team should be managed by the Marylebone Club, “ thus
removing the objectionable gate-money element invari-
ably associated with Australian teams in England."
“Objectionable!” Hum! No more gate-money, no
more Australee-man play. No more rum, no more blanky
hallelujah !
• ♦ * *
Sydney cricket-season began last Saturday by
A. Bannerman scoring a duck’s egg and many smaller
lights following his example. University made the lowest
score, viz., 30, including 2 sundries ; Colreavy, with 11,
being the only double-figure scorer. The highest com-
pleted score was the Glebe's (M’Kibbin’s) club with 88.
The highest scorer of all Saturday’s players was M’Kibbin
with 23; he also bagged 3 wickets for 6. Only threeuther
batsmen reached 20, viz., Turner, Brewster and Driver.
Turner, bowling with no luck as usual, got 3 of the Uni.’s
for 18 ; while shea, his fellow bowler, got 5 for 10. S. P.
Jones obtained 5 wickets for 32, and Newell 3 for 5.
* * • •
Stoddart’s team benefited Melb. O.C. by over
£3900. The M.C.C. members now number 2908, which is
the record, and 239 above last year’s roll. The club’s
total net receipts for last year were £10,000; entrance-
fees and subscriptions came to £623s—an excess of £965
over last year’s total.
♦ * * *
W. G. Grace admits that he is not so much at
home with very fast bowling as he used to be. His eye-
sight has lost some of its alertness, and Richardson’s
express ones have a trick of getting past him before he
knows where ’e are. Now, an elderly commercial gent
will swear that his eyes are as good as ever they were in
his life.
* * * *
A few days ago the cricket-season opened at a
N.S.W. hospital for the insane, and while two patients were
batting, a third brought off a magnificent catch. His
own surprise, coupled with the hearty, if incoherent,
applause of bis audience, put him fairly off his head, and
he cleared off over the fence, taking the ball with him.
All qualified to do so at once gave chase, but it took them
half-an-hour to “ find the ball ’’—three or four miles away
in the bush. Meanwhile the men at the wickets had
compiled 278 runs. Which recalls the remark of the
Frenchman who caught a passing glimpse of a Rugby
football match: “It was wrong of him to steal ze ball,
but zey should not have executed him on ze spot.”
♦ ♦ ♦ «
In Melbourne, “ progressive euchre,’’ with its
expensive prizes, has become so great a tax that in many
circles players limit their liabilities by paying so much
per head into the pool at the beginning of the season, the
money going to purchase the trophies.
* * » *
N.Q. cricket-match. Bowler,appealing: “How’s
that?” Umpire; “How many is he?” Bowler:
“Ten.” Umpire (decidedly); “Hout.”
♦ * ♦ *
Surrey seems to be rich in bowlers since
Lohmann returned to work. Tall Richardson stood out
of the team when they played Essex, the other week, but
the gap was filled by Smith, anew man, who took 10
wickets for 50 runs.
* • ♦ *
Walter Giffen, like Jack Lyons, is now a share-
broker in Adelaide.

• * • •
“What is to prevent bookmakers standing beside
the tote and offering machine odds and a shade over?”
In Maoriland, the police. Or they try to. A punter
wishing to invest 10s. and not knowing anybody trust-
worthy enough, or willing to trust him, as a partner in a
£1 tote-lioket, has to sneak round to the back of the
scraping-sheds and hand his money and initials to an
individual who is carefully dressed to look unlike a book-
maker. This man, or boy, furtively looks over his
shoulder every now and then and asks the punter to “Get
away quick, please.” The wagers are, at convenient
intervals, booked by another furtive-looking chap on an
ordinary race-card. In the towns nearly all the betting is
done on the street, the “book” considering it safer to
risk pneumonia by standing out in the wet surreptitiously
laying the odds, than to have detectives poking into his
office to put a bit on.
» ♦ • ♦
Tote v. Bookie. At the recent S.A.J.C. spring
meeting the Derby candidate, Auraria, paid 30s. on the
tote. The bookies declined to accept tote commissions,
“No money Auraria” being theory. Showing what a
nice chance the public would have had to get on at 6 to 4
had there been no machine.
• ♦ • *
Estimated that Robinson and party, now shoot-
ing buffaloes on Melville Island, have killed 1500 beasts
since their hunt began ; also that, since ’B6, 30,000 buf-
faloes have been slain altogether on island and mainland.
Obviously, it is only a question of time till the species is
exterminated altogether.
* * * *
Novel tennis point. In a recent ladies’ tourna-
ment the hair of one lady came down, so the umpire
allowed her to retire to do it up. She stayed away Ihr,
20min., thus securing a draw under the rules. Result—a
ruling that in all future matches ladies whose hair comes
down will just have to play with it down, or default.
♦ * * •
Dick Barker, the alleged prize-fighter, stands
nearly 7ft., weighs 16st., and hails from Queensland. At
Cooma, N.S.W., lives Dick Harvey, born and bred on the
Snowy River, not far from “ Jinderbyne,” who stands
6ft. lOin. without boots, and, at 23 years of age, weighs
16st.
« « « «
The late Alec Robertson would never tell a
friend what horse to back. He’d say “Give me your
money and I will put it on for you.” To a particular
friend, who questioned him, he replied: “Well, I
wouldn’t like to tell a hundred lies every day, and it isn’t
good enough to publish one’s knowledge regarding a
particular horse, so I make a rule never to tip anything
for anybody.”
« • «
Jockey Jim Hayes, who has at last persuaded
the V.R.C. to reinstate him, rode the first win of his
new era at Flemington last Saturday. He bestrode
Hova, and won a good race from a good horse, Wallace.
Hova, though not favorite, was pretty well backed, and
the public cheered Hayes greatly. Hayes having had
plenty of time for reflection, it should take much success
to turn his head now.
* * * *
On his running in the September [Stakes at
Caulfield last Saturday, Wallace must have a big show in
V.R.C. Derby. He made a fine run at Hova in the last
furlong, and, although too late to catch him, showed
some of his father’s pace. This was a w.f.a. race, and
some good horses finished behind which didn’t figure to
any extent in the betting.
* ♦ * •
By running Hova so close a race at w.f.a. in the
Sept. Stakes (1J miles) at Caulfield, Wallace shows he has
retained his two-year-old form. The distance was out
out in 2min. 9|sec., and as such recent winners as Bruin
and Eiridsforde were behind the leading couple, the per-
formance may be looked on as good. Whoever draws
Wallace in the big sweep should draw the winner of the
V.R.C. Derby.
* ♦ « *
The Maorilanders’ version is that, of those who
finished behind the first three, in the Sept. Handicap,
The Possible will probably prove to be the best at Flem-
ington, by which time he will be fit. They say people
haven’t seen the best of Maxim’s three-quarter brother.
* • * ♦
If Mr. Hordern trusts Vivian in V.R.C. Derby
that horse should secure a place, though he cannot beat
either Wallace or Challenger.
♦ ♦ *
Common, looking in splendid condition, was
served up hot for the Seeholme Stakes at Randwick, on
Saturday, but he performed ingloriously. He has since
gone to Melb., and, as a mile is his best distance, he may
run forward next Saturday at Caulfield in the Toorak
Handicap, in which he is well-treated with Bst. 71b.
» * * »
The Ballarat sports are standing the double
Idolater and Dreamland. A good combination, as, bar
accidents, backers may be assured of a run in both
instances.
* * * ♦
Tote-bettors make their own odds. Two horses
made a dead-heat of it in a race at Auckland (M.L.), the
other day. The favorite paid 16s. for the pound invested,
and the outsider £139 10s.
* * * *
Why don’t the racing authorities exercise more
control over the naming of racehorses? They allow more
than one horse of the same name to run in the same
event, as witness Pile’s Carlton and Hale’s ditto, which
were racing against each other in Adelaide recently.
Besides, no horse with a name bearing a marked similarity
to another should be registered. For instance, we see
Malula, Maluma, and Maluna all in the same race, and
Marusa a stable mate of Maruma. This is grossly unfair
to the public.
* * • ♦
People who have read many Maorilanders’
assertions that St. Hippo is as good as Carbine, expected
a revelation as to the true form of this heavily-weighted
horse at Flemington, but a cracked hoof has upset his
party’s arrangements. However, he may yet be well
enough to don silk. Carbine had a cracked hoof before
he won the Cup.
* ♦ ♦ ♦
St. Hippo, top weight in Toorak Handicap, and
Caulfield, Melb., and N.Z. Cups, hasn’t won a race for
two years, and a couple of his first get at the stud have
just popped into existence in M.L.
* * * *
N.S.W. will be strongly represented in the
coming Vic. campaign. In addition to the much-belauded
Hopscotch (who may turn out a flash-in-the-pan, like
Brockleigh), Mahee, Solanum and Alcmena may be ex-
pected to run well in Caulfield Cup, whilst it will take a
good horse to put down Delaware in Melb. Cup. The
wins of Georgio and Oxide at Randwick last Saturday
considerably enhance his form, as he gave Georgio two
stone and a beating in the Craven Plate, and is credited
with being “ tons ” better than his stable-mate, Oxide.
Mahee, Alcmena and Toreador should also show pro-
minently, the latter especially deserving a tick against
his name.
♦ ♦ ♦ *
It is the opinion of at least one shrewd Rand-
wick racing-man that before Melb. Cup flag falls there
will be a preponderance of N.S.W. money pn Mahee.
* * * *
It is now almost a certainty that Bob Ray will
be absent from the Vic. garland competition, his swollen
knee rendering further training impossible at present.
By the way, his owner, always fortunate in horsey bar-
gains, experienced a gleam of consolation on Saturday
when Havanna, bought for 175 guineas the previous
week, scored in the hurdles at Randwick.
* * « •
Noted that the Admiral is an absentee from the
Caulfield Stakes ; but owner Cook is represented by the
Merry Boy and the Heroine.
* ♦ ♦ ♦
Taranaki’s last in the Sept. Handicap on Satur-
day isn’t a good augury for the future, but it may not be
worth anything, especially as he carriedß.il. His Cup
imposts are considerably less.
• • ♦ *
Armilla, by Castor—Necklace, is reckoned fast
enough to be sent from M.L. to contest Maribyrnong
Plate, which her sister, Necklet, won a few yean ago.

In Melb. Cup of ’65 the grey pony, Tory Boy,
beat the 1500-guinea imported horse, Panic. Yet a few
years afterwards the grey was sold at Kirk’s for 15 guineas
as a hack—and a small gold-mine could not have bought
Panic.
». * * •
Out of 34 Melb. Cups the lamb has saved his
coat on seven occasions only—Archer, 2 to 1; Barb, 6 tol;
Tim Whiffler, 5 to 2 ; Don Juan, 3 to 1; Martini-Henry,
5 to 1 ; Malua 6 to 1, and Carbine.
» ♦ • •
The luok of horse-racing. Two days before
Zulu’s Cup, the trainer wired to owner : “ Horse lame
and off feed. Shall I scratch him ? ” The answer came
back : “ No, give him a run.” And the lame fraud, at a
100 to 1 chance, left behind him such good ones as Dare-
bin, Sweetmeat, and Savanaka—and was never heard of
afterwards.
* • * *
Of the 34 Melb. Cups Victoria claims 19, N.S.
Wales 13, S.A. and Tasmania one apiece.

When Tom Hales first thought of setting up a
stud, he much fancied Lochiel, but a Melb. vet. pro-
nounced the horse to be saturated with disease. Looking
at recent performances in N.S.W. of Lnohiel’s two-year-
old progeny, Tom tears his hair and howls for that vet.’s
blood.

his bowling this year.

“J. Young”: The palpable device of many
professional tipsters is to send a different horse to each
customer, till they have gone all round the list, and then
begin again.
Piles Cured. — Owing to the fact that numbers
of people think piles are incurable, the proprietor
of the new “ Cannalin ” remedies has given in-
structions that free samples and descriptive
pamphlets are to be sent to all applicants in the
colonies. Those who have obtained no relief,
even after years of treatment or from operation,
are specially invited to write to the dep6t for
Australasia, 257 George-st., Sydney, N.S.W.*

Unpaid, in this connection, is good.
Louis Pasteur, who died last week, aged
73, rendered splendid service to science and
humanity. He made the grand generalisation
that disease is due to the presence of living organ-
isms, and experimentally showed how —as in
hydrophobia—the body may be immunised and
defended against their attacks. All the recent
discoveries of serums antagonistic to cholera, or
diphtheria, or snake-poison, have their root in the
laboratory of the famous French chemist.
“ There are lots of people very anxious to
save this motion against Judge Windeyer from
coining on. Let it come on and be dealt with on
its merits. But if there is any more dirt thrown
about by the head of the Department of Justice
in this province, let me tell him that, whatever
the consequences, and whatever the results may
be to me, I shall speak out in the matter and
make him wish to God he had kept his mouth
quiet.”—W. P. Crick, M.P., in N.S.W. As-
sembly.
“Jim” Toohey, of the big Sydney brewing
firm of J. T. and J. M. Toohey, whose death in
England is cabled, left N.S.W. a few months ago
on a holiday trip. He was about 46 years old,
and a native of Victoria, but up to the very day of
his departure from Sydney he was a wild Irish
boy. He was a good-hearted fellow of many
friends and many parts, and had dabbled more or
less successfully in politics, the drama, sport and
business. For over 10 years he was an active
member of N.S.W, Legislative Assembly, and in
five Parliaments sat for South Sydney. He was a
strong Protectionist, and freely spent both money
and personal energy in advancing Australian
nationalistic principles. His last public escapade,
labelled by himself “Go I to Tamworth” {to
oppose G. Republican Dibbs, who had politically
offended him) will be fresh in the memory of Corn-
stalks!

and M.R.O.S., L.A.C. (London), writes:—“ I am

Next Monday is Eight-Hour Day, and the
Demonstration Committee has completed every
possible arrangement necessary to the Success of
the celebration. Enormous numbers of tickets
have already been sold, the splendid exhibition of
art-union prizes in Priddy’s old shop, 438 George-
st., having proved a capital advertisement. The
association football-match between Balmain and
Pyrmont Rangers should prove a great attraction,
while the day will be suitably wound up by
T. A. Kennedy’s marvellous hypnotic enter-
tainment in the Town Hall at night. There
are 12 entries for the band-contest, including all
the regimental bands, so that the music for the
procession should be first-class. Monday has
been declared a general holiday within the county
of Cumberland,*
Attorney-General Want says that Salo-
mons’s statement re the Dean case is backed up by
“ other facts that have come into his possession,”
but which, up to the time of writing, he hadn’t
disclosed. This is a (S.M. Herald copyright)
“ reprehensible ” and one-sided mode of argument.
The “facts” which J. Want thinks he is in
possession of might possibly be demolished by the
other side and proved to be mere wind, if only
they were disclosed. But so long as they are not
disclosed they can’t be refuted, and so Want hits his
opponent without giving him a chance to hit back.
The Attorney-General should have said nothing
about this information until he was in a position
to reveal it. ___

“Is there any necessity for me to live
with the woman ? ” asked a wife-beater of Mr,
Keogh, P.M., at' Eitzroy, the other morning.
“Not for the ensuing quarter, at any rate,”
promptly replied Mr. Keogh, as ho gave him three
months’ hard.
Dr. Syme, of Melb. Hospital, on the
curious and anomalous laws of human heredity:—
It is apparently more common for qualities to be trans-
mitted from the female, and especially to the male off-
spring ; while it is also common for qualities to be trans-
mitted from the male parent to the female offspring. So
that if this rule held good for two generations the males
would resemble their maternal grandfather, and the
females their paternal grandmother. Of course in very
many cases the transmission is from male parent to male
offspring ; but the first rule is certainly very common, and
helps to explain why genius need not be hereditary, and
why the sons of great men so seldom attain distinction.
Dear Bulletin, —Among the reforms re-
commended by certain fossils in high places to
spoil the business of the abortionist no allusion
was made to the only effective remedy—the
legitimisation of children born out of wedlock.
The so-called illegitimacy of the child, and the
consequent disgrace to the mother, are the chief
incentives to women to “ hide their shame,” even
at the risk of their lives. Strange that the birth
of a child, the greatest good a woman can do the
world, should, under any circumstances, be
accounted her “shame!” When all children are
legitimate, and every mother a wife by the fact of
the birth of her child, illegal operations, baby-
farming and infanticide will vanish. Doubtless
the parson-flock will hold up hands of pious horror
at such an idea, and prate of the “ sacredness of
marriage.” They do not mean marriage, but the
legal and religious form which adds importance to
their cloth, puts power in their hands—and cash in
their pockets. Away with long-dead but unburied
superstitions, and let us compile a marriage-law
bused on humanity and justice.— Wakea.
Bulletin correspondents (writes one of
them) have been disputing over the relative
measures of honor which belong to the pioneers of
the Broken Hill mines. But for the last seven
years I have not seen in print, nor have I heard
spoken, the name of the man who really was the
pioneer of the N.S.W. silver-mining industry;
indeed, the honor has been absolutely taken from
him and given to others. His name was Charles
Nickle, and his last public appearance, so far as I
know, was at a banquet tendered to W. R.
Wilson (he of St. Alban’s) upon his retiring from
the general managership of the Barrier Ranges
Silver Mining Association. In ’75, Nickle worked
a lead “ show ” at Thackariuga, laboriously got a
waggon-load of the galena ore down to the sea-
board, and shipped it. The vessel foundered !
He afterwards sent away a second lot, but this
barely paid expenses, and the effort to open up
the field was practically abandoned for four years.
Then Nickle found the Clifton; the Maybell
and Maybell North followed. It was not till
after Thackariuga and Puruamoota (“The
Soakage ”) were almost famous that Charles
Rasp pegged out the Broken Hill mine;
and how much he knew about it was shown by the
fact that when he returned to Mount Gipps head
station he told them he had struck a hill full of
tin, and gave notice to leave forthwith. It is
quite true that after this George M’Culloch, then
manager of Mount Gipps, now art-connoisseur of
London (you ought to have seen—and heard!—
M’Culloeh in those days) helped the industry
along; but in the real pioneering times, when it
seemed to be a fight between miner and squatter,
M'Culloch was hostile to the prospectors. Cer-
tainly the latter did not have quite as much
respect for fences as they should have had. The
Brodribbs were at that time on Poolamacca
Station, and Mrs. Brodribb came in to spill a
bottle of champagne over the first smelting fur-
nace at Broken Hill—on May 6, 1886 (over 10
years after Nickle’s ore had gone to the bottom).
George M'Culloch presided at the banquet on
that occasion. J. S. Reid and Walter Sully have
been mentioned ; “ Randolph the Reckless ” re-
gards them as the only two “ early Broken Hill
men ” who deserve much praise—for perspicacity,
at any rate. To Reid, Broken Hill certainly
owes much: he “barracked” for it more than
any other man, and not only in his paper.
But Sully got his interests more by good luck
than good managment, and for a long wliile per-
sistently fought for Silverton (now almost deserted)
as the centre of the field, instead of Broken Hill.
So much for his part in the development of Broken
Hill ! Anyway, there isn’t much credit due to
anybody for opening up a mine like the B.H.
Proprietary, which was manifestly a payable thing
almost from the start; the credit (if it belongs to
anybody, and perhaps that is doubtful) really
belongs to the men like Nickle, McLean, the
Nolans and others who opened up Thackariuga
and Purnamoota, and the men like W. R. Wilson,
who furnished them with money to do it.
Talking of Broken Hill and those very
early days, it is noticed by old hands that hardly
any of those who roughed it then, and did not
clear out before the boom, afterwards left it with
much over a “liver” in their pockets. Apart
from the eight or ten early Broken Hill men, not
six pioneers of the Barrier kept anything to speak
of. The men who did make the money were
those who did not go there till the boom was a
certainty, knew it was only a boom, treated it as
such, were prepared for a collapse at any moment,
and, when the collapse did come, cleared. The
storekeepers, hotel-keepers, &e., who were “making
fortunes ’’—and they deserved them for what they
put up with —were almost without exception
stranded. A line for Coolgardie ?
Ladies who value a clear skin and fresh
complexion, and who appreciate the possession of
clean, white teeth and a sweet breath, should
follow the example of their European sisters and
indulge in the use of Amykos. After being out
in the hot sun, or on a dusty road, nothing is more
cooling or refreshing than a little Amykos

STATUTORY DECLARATION,

South Wales, do hereby solemnly and sincerely declare as follows : Whereas I suffered severely from
a very troublesome affection of the Nerves, which affected me so much that I was quite unable to
attend to my business. I tried several doctors and many remedies, but all of no avail. Whereas I, as
a last hope, obtained some of Herr Rassmussen’s Purely Herbal “Alkaline Vitality Pills,”
which I hereby solemnly and sincerely declare Completely Cured Me within a few weeks, and
although tiiis is over five years ago I have remained well ever since. The Alkaline Pills also
cured my wife, and I make this solemn declaration conscientiously believing the same to be true.—
THOS. MUDIMAN.
Subscribed and declared at Sydney this 23rd day of November, 1891, before me.—S. T. Whiddon,
J.P. Also witnessed by A. Baly, Esq., a Commissioner of the Supreme Court of New South
Wales and Victoria.

Jabez Hare has been sent to gaol in Mel-
bourne for petty Jarceuy. Jugged Hare.
Quebec province, Canada, has a law be-
stowing 100 a. of Government land on every
father of 12 living legitimate children. Already
2000 fathers of a dozen have claimed and got their
reward. But one Paul Belanger, of Le Loup, is
dissatisfied, because, being father of three dozen of
the right brand, he thinks he ought to get three
hundred-acre prizes. One M. Theoret, of St.
Genevieve, looking ahead, backs Belanger up on
the ground that his 30-year-old wife has already
presented him with 17 children, having achieved
triplets twice, and twins three times.

Professor PEART—Professor PEART—Pro-
fessor PEART, engaged from Imry Kiralfy’s
Indian Exhibition, Earl’s-court, London. The
Champion High Diver of the World ! The Latest
and Greatest London and American Success. He
will DIVE from the DOME of the Exhibition
Building, Melbourne, into a tank containing only
6ft. of water. From the Son, London : “ Head
foremost 150 ft. ! The latest sensational dive. Up,
up, high above the sea of faces, to a little platform
just below the glass roof, a man in the costume
of a diver is slowly being hoisted. He settles him-
self upon the scanty planking, whilst beneath him
the spectators are hushed into silent wonder at the
daring of the feat. Ah ! he springs into mid-air,
and then with theswoop of a bird upon its prey falls
rapidly to earth. Will he accomplish his self-im-
posed task in safety or will he crash into the rows of
chairs which hedge round the little tank of water
the diver has for his destination? This is the
thought which flashes simultaneously through the
mind of every spectator. An indefinable murmur
of relief runs through the gathering, for the diver
strikes the water with the velocity of an express,
throws up a cloud of spray, and then, streaming
with water, he bows his acknowledgments of the
applause which greets his successful accomplish-
ment. Twice a day does Charles Owen Peart dive
this greatest of all dives.”
The original and only Talking Horse, Mahomet,
took London by storm and became the rage. Was
performing in four music halls nightly for two
years. This is not a trick horse, but an EDU-
CATED HORSE, He adds, subtracts, divides,
multiplies, tells the time by any watch, and will
answer any questions. This is the only horse
which has been a witness in a court of justice.

i Two flamingoes and two young ostriches
were killed at Adelaide Zoo by rats, which
gnawed through their legs and then dined off their
heads. Three brood mares from a racing stud,
the owner of which did not wish their progeny to
get into other hands, were lately sent to the Zoo
to feed the carnivora, and the lions and tigers
have also eaten several racehorses killed on
Adelaide course.
Wired to a Melbourne daily :
We have hart a splendid fall of rain here. This will
have the effect of laying the dust on the roads, extin-
guishing bush fires, and causing the grass to spring.
Also, it will have the effect of making some mud,
keeping the clothes on the line from getting dry,
and making the top of the roof wet. When a big
Melbourne paper sets about publishing encyclo-
paedic information of this character it ought not
to stop half-way.
After much talk, the Melb. Board of
Works passed a law to compel its contractors to
pay a minimum wage of 6s. a day to laborers. The
law exists, but contractors simply “ take a lunar ”
at it, and the minimum wage continues to be the
lowest price at which a man will sell the labor of
his hands under the compulsion of threatened
starvation. The Board’s officers do not trouble
to discover whether the enactment is obeyed
or not, but when a glaring case of non - com-
pliance is thrust under their noses all they can
do is to deduct from moneys due to the defaulting
sweater the difference between the wage paid and
the alleged standard of 65., and pay the money
into the account of the Board—not to the unfor-
tunate laborers. So that the Board actually profits
by the extortion.
For coughs, colds you can’t beat Oolemane
and Sons Bucalypte Extract.*
N.S.W. Judge Simpson’s decision that
drunkenness on the part of a wife isn’t legal
ground for divorce unless the lady is in a state of
habitual rum for three years, and neglects her
domestioduties for that period, is ratherastounding.
Under this precedent, the lady may be in an awful
condition of gin-and-cloves for, say, two years
and nine months. Then, if she straightens up for
six months or so, the offence isn’t legally habitual,
and she can start on another brandy-crawl for
another 2| years. Also, it appears that no
amount of drink will fill the bill unless the lady
also “neglects her domestic duties.” The Bul-
letin reads of a case in point, in the shape of a
lady who performs her domestic duties most
assiduously when she is “on her ear.” On
these frequent occasions she is possessed of
a demon of washing-up, and scrubs all the
crockery in the house until it is reduced to chaos.
She also washes the sewing-machine or the
chiffonier and sometimes the pictures as well,
though generally she falls off the chair while she
is reaching them down, and puts her foot through
them. Then she flies round vigorously to get the
dinner ready, and when the old man comes home
he finds a burnt leg of mutton deposited on the
tapestry table-cloth, and the potatoes on the floor,
and a capsized cruet somewhere else, and a pud-
ding that has been recently snatched out of the
ashes. That lady doesn’t neglect her domestic
duties ; she performs them with all her heart and
soul, and with a misdirected zeal that is more than
commendable. Where does she come in according
to Judge Simpson’s reading of the law?
For rheumatism use Colemane and Sons
Eucalypte Special Oil.*
“ Adulteration,” said Bright, “ is a form
competition.” The absurd Tories down South treat
the importation of shoddy as a direct result of
Victorian Protection. The English mills that
turn out tweed, as the National Wool co. testify,
with never a thread of natural wool, are the re-
sults of Victorian Protection. The English
factories that make the manufacture of “devils”
a principal branch of trade are the result of Vic-
torian Protection. The association of the town of
Brummagem with everything that is spurious is
the result of Victorian Protection. As every
advantage that accrued to England during the
past 50 years is the result of Ereetrade, so every
disaster that has accrued to Victoria during the
last five years is the result of Protection, and the
land-boom and the robbery of the people by the
banks had nothing to do with it. As all the evils
that have happened to England in 50 years are
the result of natural causes, so all the benefits
that have happened to Victoria in 25 years are the
result of natural causes and G-od Save the Queen.
A GRAND LITERARY INSTITUTION.
Cole’s Book Arcade now runs from the centre
of the block in Bourke-street to the centre of the
block in Collins-street, Molb., a distance of 660 ft.,
and contains the following 17 departments, mostly
literary and artistic : 1, New Books; 2, Second-
hand Books; 3, Stationery ; 4, Music ; 5, Fancy
Goods ; 6, Toys ; 7, Perfumery ; 8, Pictures ; 9,
Artists’ Materials ; 10, an Ornament Exhibition ;
11, a Japanese Exhibition ; 12, a Wonderland ;
13, a Smiling Gallery; 14, a Child’s Picture
Gallery ; 15, a Bargain Room ; 16, a Tea Room ;
17, a splendidly arranged new Wholesale Book
Department, containing half a million books. The
main walks of the establishment are half a mile
long. •
In N.S.W. Assembly the other night
Mr. Crick drew the attention o f the Minister for Justice
to the fact that people who went to visit prisoners at
Darlinghurst Gaol were ushered in by the public flogger,
and he asked whether the department would, in consider-
ation of the feelings of women and others visiting the
gaol, place another official on duty at the public door?
In reply, Minister Gould said that he understood the man
performing the duty alluded to was one of the gaol
warders. Of course, the public flogger might be em-
ployed in opening the door for visitors just the same
as any other warder.
When The Bulletin was in the debtors’ prison it
took some time to discover that the round-
shouldered Hercules who acted as messenger was
the flogger of the day. Queerly enough, he was a
man of strongly polemical bent, and to
agnosticism of a most robust order, his god being
Bradlaugh. He couldn’t afford to believe in a
Hell. The gentleman erot so far into the good
graces of politician Dibbs during the latter’s in-
carceration that Damchioago, when he became
Premier long afterwards, appointed him to the
governorship of a country gaol. However, the
locals refused to believe that a man-flayer was fit
to preside over any establishment for the reforma-
tion of criminals, and public opinion was so strong
on the subject that the flogger-governor had to
“get.” And worse followed. The flogger fell in
love and got into legal trouble. Then he died,
and he is now probably flogging lost souls.

Editor Bulletin. —Be H. J. M‘Gooey’s re-
cent letter. Malformations are very scarce among:
snakes in Australia or anywhere else ; the only
two-tailed snake I’ve seen (a “whip”) was at
Cootarnundra, some years ago. On the other
hand, it is quite a common thing for some specie*
of lizards to have two or more tails. Since coming
here (W. A.) I have taken a lizard with three tails,
and the Macleay Museum (at Sydney University)
has one with four. —A. M. Lea. Perth, 12/8/’DS.

“ Crom-a-boo,” that funniest of Irish comic
football songs, is selling faster than ever.*
Medical law in N.S.W., as stated by a
leading Sydney doctor: “ Business men and
lawyers are strictly protected as regards their pro-
perty and professional privileges, but they and the
general public have no protection against quack
pretenders who tamper with their lives in greedy
ignorance. Indeed the law, instead of putting
down the quack and so protecting the public,
actually encourages him. For, while a qualified
man who commits a blunder and kills a patient
by malpractice, can be punished as a criminal,
because, being supposed to know his business, he
should have done better, the quack, not knowing
his business, can plead that he did his best, and
so lead to the Judge pointing out to the jury that
the quack, having done his best according to his
ignorant ability, is therefore entitled to a verdict
of ‘Not Guilty.’ ”
The only known cure for influenza is Cole-
mane and Sons Eucalypte Extract.
Editor Bulletin, —Much has been said in
Vic. Parliament and press against the imposition
of a duty on penny imported literature; one chief
argument being that it would deprive the poor
factory-girl of her novelette. As one who has had
considerable experience in the business, I may
state that the “ poor factory-girl ” is not a voraci-
ous reader of this sort of thing. Domestics are
frequent buyers of novelettes, etc,; but, in many
cases, they are for “ the missus.” The real, solid
supporter of the “penny dreadful ” is the mature
materfamilias, who, having outlived her own
romance (if she ever had one), takes a most
absorbing interest in that of the curly-haired
duke with the long legs, the persecuted heroine
with the impossible waist and feet, and the be-
diamonded marchioness; whereas the factory-
girl, and the domestic when her day’s work is done,
take their romance neat in the Fitzroy Gardens
or at the back-gate, and do not hunger for the
diluted yum-yum of the penny periodical.—
Phobmi.


William Roser (or Roses) left Shad well,
London, in 1869, for Newcastle, N.S.W. Was in
Newcastle in 1875, and was last heard of in
Woolloomooloo in 1880. His aged mother
anxiously enquires for news.

Every man possessed of any small rem-
nant of self-respect should at all times know
“where ’e are,” but it falls to the lot of but few
men to know what they can do until they own a
hand-camera. Not until then, either, do many of
them find out what they can’t do; but that is
nearly always because they get hold of a bad in-
strument or the wrong end of a good one, Baker
and Rouse keep at their central depots in each of
the Australias only the best of these fascinating,
useful, and remunerative little companions. In
price they range from 35s. up to .£1515s., but
each or any of them will do the work it is sold to
do without a present hitch or a possible future
of remorse. B. and R. also teach purchasers how
to use the camera economically, surely, and satis-
factorily. The season for hand-camera work is
now in. It is always in more or less, but just now
it is more in than usual.*

IMPORTANT NOTICE TO CIGARETTE SMOKERS.
WE beg to inform our customers and cigarette smokers generally that the American Tobacco Co.,
Ltd., of N.S.W., who control the sale of cigarettes known under the brands of “Allen and
Ginter’s Straight-cut No. 1,” “Cameo” and “ Old Judge,” and who manufacture the colonial-made
cigarettes called “Pure Gold ” and “ Dixon’s No. 1,” have given us official notice that they will not
supply us with any of their American-made cigarettes unless we agree to relinquish the agency held by
us for New South Wales for the world-renowned English firm of W. D. and H. O. WILLS, Limited,
the manufacturers of those popular high-class cigarettes known as
“ THBBB CASTLES 77 and “ CAPSTAN.”
We have declined to conform with this request, as we feel it would not be to the nterest of
cigarette smokers in New South Wales to allow the American Tobacco Co., Limited, to monopolise
the whole of the cigarette trade. We, therefore, appeal to cigarette smokers to try the celebrated
Wills’ “Three Castles” and “Capstan” Cigarettes,
And compare their quality with that of any other. We are confident that their verdict will be in
favor of the
“Three Castles” and “Capstan.” Cigarettes,
And by smoking these they will prevent any one Company from attempting to dictate what people
should or should not smoke.
HEYDE, TODMAN & CO. [Agents for W. D. and H. 0. WILLS, Ltd.)
Various Verses .
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
IN AMOROUS YOUTH.
DAS WEISS ICH SCHON.
Forth from what realms of earlier existence
Com’st thou to me ?
Claimed by what irresistible persistence
Yield I to thee ?
Surging from deeps unplumbed by recollection
Swims into ken
Thy soul that erst hath held me in subjection—
But where ? but when ?
All has been said, been thought, been done, ex-
plained
Ages ago.
Only the quintessence of love remained
For us to know.
Therefore it was that while I sat beside thee
I touched no hand.
What need for word, for touch, for kiss to guide
thee ?
We understand.
A CONTRAST.
Matched with this fiery flame wherein I burn,
How cool, my mountain love, your milder mood!
Like morning dew upon the upland fern,
Or first shy leaflets in a vernal wood,
Or like that moonlight eve we spent at Chur
Along the margin of your wild Plessur.
Here in the torrid zone of this embrace,
Sun-smitten by these dark and daring eyes,
When all is ardent fire that fills the place
Beside me, where this radiant body lies,
How faint, how rare, how delicately sweet
Those airs that reach me from your mountain seat!
O, mountain love, dear dayspring on a lawn !
O, fiery mid-day love on the lagoon !
Which is more dear, first flushings of the dawn
Or dazzling splendor of the sun at noon?
Why should I question ? Neither love is loth
Enough for me if I suffice for both.
OUR SECRET.
We have a little secret, love,
A way to say “ Good-night,”
When night
Is anything but good or right.
I in my chamber, just above
Your chamber-roof —one—two—
Throw down my boots, and so do you ;
And thus we say good-night
When night
Is anything but good or right.
ADIEU.
Well! it has come, the hour for you to go,
Your coach-wheels bear you down the dusty
way :
You pass from hence, and yet I know you know
That both our lives are altered from to-day.
What though I never held you, never felt
Your arms about me, never spelt
All through Love’s lovely alphabet,
Yet, dearest, yet
For you and me while time shall run,
While seasons last, while burns the sun,
That shall not end which is begun :
Love’s star once risen cannot set.
Nay I though I touched your shoulder only once,
Once held your hand, nor pressed it tight,
Once felt your breath upon my cheek, and once
Under the starlight said “ Good-night ”
That is enough between us two :
We made a lovers’ sacrament :
You go from hence, it is too true,
But never more, from me to you,
Be said, “Adieu, sweetheart, adieu.”
Venice.
BETA.
TERPSICHORE. —A FRAGMENT.
The music did not play to her,
She made the music with her feet!
And though I could not hear their stir
I felt the players caught their beat
And took her time of dancing rhyme,
And made their playing to its chime
Like echoings.
She seemed to swim in harmonies,
And floated in her self-made airs
As a ship rippling summer seas
Makes movement music unawares ;
Or, like the maids from sea-green glades
Whose swimming sounds like serenades
To barques becalmed.
Those tuneful, tireless, tempting feet!
Ah, had they eyes ? that only when
Her petticoats were indiscreet
They peeped a moment at the men.
Or had they ears ? and heard the cheers
Of silent hearts, that full of fears
They shunned the light.
Alberton, S.A.
S. MONTA PRESTON.
A Splendid Year for Corn .
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Corn ! Dad had never seen anything like
it. Ten feet high, and the cobs full and as long
as your arm.
No one but old Tom Anderson had ever known
a better season. The first year he started was a
much better one, so he used to say. Most of the
cobs grown on his farm that year were too large
to be put through the corn-sheller, and from a
single stalk he pulled 12 of the finest cobs he ever
saw, and threshed a bushel of beautiful corn from
the tassel, which he kept for seed.
• • •
It was the healthiest-looking crop we had
had. Dad was elated, and no wonder, for he had
sworn and sweated a great deal over it, keeping
cattle off, heeling it up and cutting suckers.
But the ’possums commenced to play the devil
with it, so Dad gave a whip that Joe found to
Jimmy Anderson for some powder and shot, and
Dave made a trap out of the gin-case that mother
used to keep the clothes in. It was a well-made
trap, too, so Dad said, and was the very thing for
catching ’possums with.
Dave set it on the edge of the corn, near the lane.
The first night it didn’t catch any. Dave shifted
it. The second night—none again. The third
night one of the Maloneys shifted it. Joe saw it
at their place a long while after with rockers on
it, and a red-headed kid in it propped up with a
pillow.
* • •
Dad had no idea we would get the quantity
we did from eight acres the old bark barn
could only hold a little more than half, and so
we put a few dray-loads into the back room, and
we boys had to crawl over a heap of cobs every
night to get to bed. Pleasant! it was pleasant!
And what a pile of husks there was around the
back-door when we starting husking ! I remem-
ber mother made new pillows and refilled the bed-
ticks that year—and how we used to enjoy a rest
on the fresh husks !
Each of us did a share of the husking, and did
it at night. God knows we would be tired enough
at the end of those days, but the husking had to
be done and we couldn’t afford to do it in day-
time.
• * •
We borrowed Anderson’s corn-sheller—a
toothless old machine that only half-shelled the
cobs—the other half we did with our hands.
* * #
Dan carted the corn to the railway-station.
He liked going there, and always waited to see
the train come in. The porter said he hoped we
hadn’t much, as he was sick of throwing corn
about —everyone was carting corn, according to
him, until the shed was full of the blessed stuff.
# # #
Old Anderson and Dad were talking to-
gether at the slip-rails. Anderson was looking
for his cows and was on horseback. The youngster
was behind him —double-bank. The old man’s
eyesight had nearly gone, and he always took the
youngster to see the cows.
Dad stuffed a letter he had been showing An-
derson into his pocket and said :
“Well, I can see nothing ahead of me, Ander-
son, but ruin —absolute ruin. When one does get
a good crop, there’s nothing for it —nothing
There was a long silence, then Anderson said ;
“ That’s it— you've said it! ” Then he left, and
Dad went and carried in a back-log for the fire.
Q’sland.

Chow v. Christianity. The Central Metho-
dist Mission building in Lonsdale-street, Melb.,

Portion of the rabbit-fence west of Eyre’s
Creek (Q.), near the S.A. border, is now being

either food or water for upwards of 83 miles on
one point of the line.
Dear Bulletin, —Travelling from Cunna-
mulla to Charleville, recently, 1 met a mob of
travelling sheep. On one of the “wings ” was a
horseman with whom I had a little talk. During
our yarn he extracted a large sling from his
pocket, fitted it with a stone from the saddle-bag
and aimed the missile in the direction of a few
stragglers. The effect was to instantly round up
the wing. He told me that the boss forbade dogs
in working the sheep, and this was a labor-saving
invention of his owm. Certainly an original idea.
—Lex.
Dear Bulletin, — I notice that Tozer con-
gratulates Kanakaland on the decrease of leprosy.
A few facts : A man recently treated in Norman-
ton hospital for syphilitic paralysis of the hand
and mysterious leg-sores of the same nature was
discharged, and subsequently sent to Dunwich.
On arrival there he was pronounced a leper, and
duly isolated. After the man left the hospital,
and before he was removed to Dunwich, he took
possession of an empty house on the cricket-
ground, in which he camped for a month during
the ’95 flood. This house was afterwards used as
a dressing-room during the ,annual cricket-match
between Normanton and Croydon, and it is every
day used as a playhouse by the children of the
neighborhood. No attempt has been made to
disinfect either the house or the hospital. In the
early part of July the local medical officer officially
reported to the P.M. the existence of two
aboriginal lepers in the blacks’ camp (I hear there
are five). The camp is on the town lagoon, within
400 yards of the post-office. It is believed that
this report was transmitted to the Col. Sec.
(Tozer aforesaid), but up to date no action
has been taken by Govt. There are in the
camp altogether 200 blacks ; all invade the town
every morning looking for tucker and doing odd
jobs in return. You will see them in the stores
picking potatoes and onions; at private houses
they carry water from the public wells (and I
have heard they carry it from the well in the
school-yard); they nurse children, peel potatoes,
and perforin all sorts of domestic duties. What
will the harvest be ?— Consanguineous. Nor-
manton, 25/8/’95.

week.

Nearly all the Kanaka murderers hail
from the one island—Malayta. It would pay
Boodlewraith to buy up the Malayta heathen
and pack them back to their own dear little
island. Certain humanitarians in the North
object to disarming the Polynesian, as he would
then be unable to protect himself against his homi-
cidal brethren.
Picture of the kangaroo —
from a recently-published Eng-
lish dictionary. The kangaroo,
it will be observed, carries his
tail between his legs. The
picture was evidently drawn
just after the last series of
test-cricket-matches.
Pulsford, M.L.C., says
that N.S. W. Councillors should
be appointed for eight or ten
years, and that the House
should consist of 60 members,
ten being appointed yearly. Pulsford apparently
thinks that eight or ten times ten are 60.
Dear Bulletin, —In a recent issue refer-
ence is made to the occupation of Flogger as being
“ the meanest of all vocations.” Socially speak-
ing, doubtless this is true; but, pecuniarily
considered, the emoluments attached to it tend to
make the position almost an enviable one,
especially in the eyes of retired marines. For
instance, the Flogger at Darlinghurst ranks as a
senior warder, at 9s. 3d. per diem (Sundays
included), occupies superior quarters at the gaol,
has a prisoner-servant allowed him, and is pro-
vided with fuel, light, brushware, soap, matting,
and other conveniences ; has all washing, ironing,
and mangling done gratis for himself and family.
His travelling allowance amounts to 7s. 6d. per
day when on “ detached duty.” In addition he is
in receipt of a navy pension of 2s. per day.
Twenty years ago the pay of a “Scourger” in
N.S. W. was 2s. 6d. per day, minus emoluments. —
An Ex-Warder.
What the Argus calls “an interesting and
thoughtful paper ” was recently inflicted on Melb.
Reform Club by Dr. Jamieson —the inevitable
Madden in the chair. Gist of the Jamiesonian
deliverance :
Lunatics and criminals were unfit for the franchise, and
why should not the wholly uneducated, or the man with
no fixed residence, the shiftless, the improvident, be also
considered unfit?
Interesting and thoughtful, but not exhaustive.
Why should not the enormously wealthy man
with seventeen fixed residences, and the penurious
and usurious grabster, and the wheezy man with
fatty degeneration of the heart and a bad supply
of blood to his brain, be also considered unfit?
If Dr. Jamieson is to be considered fit because he
has a “fixed residence,” why not an oyster? It
all comes back to Benjamin Franklin’s illustrative
story re property-franchise ; “In the morning
Smith owned an ass, and being a man of property
he had a vote. In the evening the ass died, and
then Smith had no vote. Therefore, it was the
ass, not Smith, which really owned the vote.”
How, in face of that story, they ever dared to
found the National Ass, is a mystery.
